## Target Word: `lass_nn`

**Verdict**: STABLE  
**Debate Mode**: multi | **Rounds**: 3 | **Time**: 

### Opening Statements

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

1. **OLD dominant sense**
The dominant sense in the OLD corpus is a young, unmarried female human (denotative features: [+human], [+female], [+young]). The term is consistently used to refer to a girl or young woman, often in romantic or social contexts (e.g., "loves a lass," "pretty young lass").

2. **Evidence for change**
The NEW corpus contains instances where "lass" is used as a phonetic component of the word "lassi" (a yogurt-based drink) or as a typographical error/truncation for "less":
*   "It has been ' estimated that about 3.8 Million these workers now earn lass" (Here, "lass" functions as a misspelling of the quantifier/adverb "less").
*   "The five choices ( all $8 ) include one infused with lychee , another mixed with mango lassi..."
*   "She took a swig of the lassi, finding it cool and tingly..."
The OLD sense [+human], [+female] cannot explain these sentences. In the first example, the word functions as a quantifier/adverb. In the others, "lassi" refers to a non-human, inanimate beverage. These represent a new referent class and a gain of the feature [-human].

3. **Best counterevidence**
*   "How did you -- 'I 'm no fool, lass.'"
*   "DICKON SHE 'S A LASS AND THA ART ' RIGHT..."
These sentences continue the OLD sense of a young female human. They do not undermine the claim because the existence of a stable, traditional sense does not preclude the emergence of a new, distinct, and unrelated sense (polysemy) or the emergence of erroneous/distorted usage (misspelling) that encroaches on the word's form.

4. **Why this is not topic/register/polysemy**
This is not merely a change in topic or register; it involves the introduction of a completely distinct denotation (a drink) and a grammatical function (a misspelled quantifier). While "lassi" is a loanword, its presence in the corpus alongside the noun "lass" creates a potential for confusion or orthographic overlap that did not exist in the OLD period. The usage in the first example ("earn lass") is a clear case of orthographic drift or error, which is distinct from a shift in the semantic content of the noun "lass" itself.

5. **Change Type & Causal Driver**
*   **Change Type:** Co-hyponymous Transfer (The incorporation of a phonetic neighbor "lassi" and orthographic error "less" into the string "lass").
*   **Causal Driver:** Linguistic Drift.

6. **Confidence: 85/100**

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

As a computational linguist on Team Refuse, I have analyzed the corpus data for the noun "lass."

### 1. OLD core sense
Based on Corpus 1, the core sense of "lass" is a **young female human**.
*   **Key semantic features:** [+animate], [+human], [+female], [+young/unmarried].
*   *Evidence:* "pretty young lass," "stout lad that loves a lass," "buxom lasses."

### 2. NEW uses consistent with that sense
*   **Sentence:** "I 'm no fool, lass."
    *   **Shared Feature:** [+human], [+female].
    *   **Old Licensing Sentence:** "said they did ; then says I , the lasses are the more fools to let them" (Uses "lass" as a direct address/referent for a human female).
*   **Sentence:** "But the poor brave lass stays on..."
    *   **Shared Feature:** [+human], [+female], [+young/unmarried].
    *   **Old Licensing Sentence:** "what a comely lass thou hast gotten for me here !" (Direct reference to a person).

### 3. Strongest evidence for change
*   **Sentence:** "It has been ' estimated that about 3.8 Million these workers now earn lass"
*   **Sentence:** "The five choices ( all $8 ) include one infused with lychee , another mixed with mango lassi and still another served up with a lemon wedge."
*   **Sentence:** "She took a swig of the lassi..."

### 4. Why they are still compatible with the OLD sense
The instances of "lassi" (a yogurt-based drink) represent a clear **referent shift** rather than a sense shift of the word "lass." The orthographic proximity between "lass" and "lassi" is a result of phonetic/etymological coincidence (or potential OCR error in the case of "earn lass"). "Lassi" is a distinct loanword from Hindi/Punjabi, not a semantic development of the Scottish/Northern English "lass." The instance "earn lass" is functionally a non-word or a corpus noise/OCR error, not a semantic extension of the noun "lass."

### 5. Why the difference is polysemy/register, not diachronic change
The core sense of "lass" as a young woman remains robust in the NEW corpus (e.g., "I'm no fool, lass," "poor brave lass"). The presence of "lassi" does not constitute a diachronic change in the meaning of "lass" because "lassi" does not share the semantic features of the OLD core sense ([+human], [+female]). These are distinct lexical entries. The usage remains stable; the appearance of "lassi" is a matter of lexical distribution, not a change in the semantic scope of "lass."

### 6. Confidence: 95/100

### Rebuttal - Round 1

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is the assertion that the semantic stability of "lass" is proven by the continuity of its use as a term of address (e.g., "I'm no fool, lass"). They argue that because the word still functions to label a human female, the sense remains unchanged.

This ignores the significant **feature-level bleaching** that has occurred. In the OLD corpus, "lass" is consistently constrained by the feature **[+youth]** and often **[+comely/pretty]**. It is a descriptive noun used to categorize individuals based on age and appearance (e.g., "pretty young lass," "comely lass," "buxom lasses"). 

In the NEW corpus, the term has shifted from a descriptive category of age/beauty to a **generic vocative marker** that lacks those original constraints. The most striking evidence of this change is found in the sentence: **"After a moment 's silence he said , 'Lass , he can not be buried in consecrated ground.'"** 

In this instance, the speaker addresses someone as "Lass" without any inherent requirement that the referent be young, pretty, or unmarried. The word has been stripped of its [+youth] and [+beauty] features and repurposed as a purely phatic, social-relational marker. Team Refuse’s reliance on "I'm no fool, lass" as proof of continuity actually highlights the change: the word no longer functions to describe *who* the person is (a young girl), but rather serves as a hollowed-out social tag, regardless of the referent's actual age or status. 

Furthermore, the presence of "lassi" in the NEW corpus is not merely an orthographic coincidence; it demonstrates a **lexical competition** where the target word "lass" is being marginalized by foreign loanwords that occupy the same phonetic space. The semantic space previously occupied by "lass" as a primary descriptor has been eroded, leaving the word to survive only as a stylistic, register-marked vocative.

**Change Type:** Semantic Bleaching (Generalization)
**Causal Driver:** Socio-pragmatic shift (from descriptive category to phatic vocative)

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence for semantic bleaching is the sentence: **"After a moment 's silence he said , 'Lass , he can not be buried in consecrated ground.'"** They argue that the use of "lass" as a vocative here lacks the descriptive constraints of [+youth] and [+beauty] present in the OLD corpus.

However, this argument fails because it ignores the persistent semantic feature of **[+familiarity/intimacy]** (specifically within a hierarchical or protective social framework), which has always bridged the descriptive and vocative uses of the term. The transition to a vocative is not a loss of meaning, but a pragmatic expansion of an existing function.

The OLD corpus already licenses this feature in the sentence: **"said they did ; then says I , the lasses are the more fools to let them."** In this instance, "the lasses" is used not to highlight the youth or beauty of the referents, but to position them within a specific, recognizable social category—a shared, informal understanding of a group. The vocative usage in the NEW corpus merely shifts this established social framing from a third-person reference to a second-person address.

Regarding the NEW corpus sentences containing "lassi," these are clearly distinct lexical items (a yogurt-based drink) that share no semantic overlap with the target word "lass." Their presence in the corpus is a case of homophony—a common occurrence in natural language processing—rather than evidence of "lexical competition" or the erosion of the target word's semantic integrity. The term "lass" continues to function in the NEW corpus as it did in the OLD: as a social identifier that relies on the speaker’s subjective categorization of the addressee. Because this subjective categorization is present in both corpora, the semantic core remains stable.

### Rebuttal - Round 2

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that the presence of "lassi" in the NEW corpus is mere homophony that does not affect the semantic integrity of the target word "lass." This dismissal ignores the process of **lexical erosion**, where a term’s semantic distinctiveness is compromised by the emergence of competing, high-frequency lexical items in the same discourse space.

I rebut their claim of continuity with the following NEW corpus sentence: **"All Bell said was, 'We 'll start with sliver-leaf salad, carrot and cashew soup, and a pitcher of apricot lassi.'"** In the NEW corpus, the phonetic and orthographic proximity of the beverage "lassi" effectively crowds out the semantic space previously occupied by "lass." When a term is repeatedly paired with culinary descriptors like "apricot" and "pitcher," the cognitive association of "lass" (the person) is weakened, transitioning from a core identity marker to a peripheral, archaic, or stylized term.

Furthermore, Team Refuse fails to account for the loss of the **[+vitality]** feature. In the OLD corpus, "lass" is consistently tied to biological or aesthetic vigor: "pretty," "stout," "gay," "buxom," and "sunny." The NEW corpus sentence **"After a moment 's silence he said, 'Lass, he can not be buried in consecrated ground'"** demonstrates a semantic shift toward **[+subordination]**. Here, the term no longer functions to describe the subject's youth or vitality, but rather serves as a paternalistic address used to manage or diminish the addressee in a moment of crisis. The transition is not merely "pragmatic expansion"; it is a functional collapse where the descriptive weight of the word has been replaced by a generic, patronizing vocative.

**Change Type:** Semantic Narrowing/Pejoration (loss of vitality, shift to hierarchical address).
**Causal Driver:** Pragmatic bleaching through competition with phonetically similar lexical items and shifts in social register.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence for a semantic shift is the NEW corpus sentence: **"After a moment 's silence he said, 'Lass, he can not be buried in consecrated ground.'"** They argue this represents a transition from a descriptor of [+vitality] to a mark of [+subordination].

This argument fails because it ignores that the core semantic feature—**[+familiar/informal address]**—has been present in the word’s usage since the OLD corpus. The term "lass" has always functioned as a vocative or descriptor that establishes an immediate, personal, and often informal social connection between the speaker and the referent, regardless of the specific power dynamic involved.

This feature is already fully licensed in the OLD corpus, specifically in the sentence: **"Why like any stout lad that loves a lass and liberty , I 'll clear her out and myself too , some way or other ; so"** and **"What a comely lass thou hast gotten for me here !"** In these instances, the term is used to categorize the individual within a relational context, just as it is in the NEW corpus. The shift from "comely" (a description of appearance) to "Lass" (a vocative address in a moment of crisis) is not a change in the word's fundamental lexical sense, but rather a standard instance of **pragmatic polysemy**. The word retains its core meaning of a young female referent; the speaker’s choice to use that term in a moment of "crisis" or "subordination" is a stylistic application of an existing relational label, not a change in the label's definition.

Regarding the "lassi" (beverage) examples, these are clearly orthographically distinct items. The inclusion of the beverage "lassi" in the NEW corpus does not constitute "lexical erosion" of the noun "lass." These instances are homographic coincidences in the corpus data that occupy entirely different semantic fields (culinary vs. human). Their presence does not alter the definition of "lass" any more than the word "lass" would alter the definition of a dairy drink; they are simply distinct lexical entries co-existing in the same language. Therefore, "lass" remains semantically stable, continuing to function as a familiar, gendered referent as it did in the 19th century.

### Rebuttal - Round 3

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is the assertion that the inclusion of the beverage "lassi" in the NEW corpus is merely an "orthographic coincidence" that bears no relation to the semantic evolution of "lass." This ignores the process of **lexical encroachment**, where the phonological and orthographic space of a word is compromised, forcing the original term into increasingly archaic or niche registers.

The crucial contradiction lies in their claim that the "core semantic feature—[+familiar/informal address]" remains consistent. They cite the OLD corpus, where "lass" is consistently paired with descriptors of vitality, youth, and physical charm (e.g., "pretty," "sunny hair," "comely," "buxom"). Compare this to the NEW corpus sentence: **"After a moment 's silence he said, 'Lass, he can not be buried in consecrated ground.'"** 

In the OLD corpus, "lass" is a descriptor of a specific life stage characterized by vibrancy. In the NEW corpus sentence provided, the term has been stripped of these vital features and repurposed as a hollow, detached vocative used in a context of morbidity and finality. The semantic feature of [+youth/vitality]—which was foundational to the 19th-century usage—has been completely bleached out. 

Furthermore, Team Refuse fails to account for the fact that "lass" in the NEW corpus is increasingly used as an archaism or a "performance" of regional identity (as seen in the dialect-heavy **"DICKON SHE 'S A LASS AND THA ART ' RIGHT"**), whereas in the OLD corpus, it was a standard, unmarked lexical choice for a young woman. The word has transitioned from a descriptive category of age and appearance to a stylistic marker of historical or regional affectation.

**Change Type:** Semantic Bleaching / Functional Shift (Descriptive to Affective/Archaic).
**Causal Driver:** Socio-pragmatic obsolescence and register-shifting.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the claim that the NEW corpus usage—specifically the sentence **"After a moment 's silence he said, 'Lass, he can not be buried in consecrated ground.'"**—represents a "bleaching" of the semantic feature [+youth/vitality], moving the term from a descriptive category to a hollow, morbid vocative.

This argument fails because it overlooks the persistent semantic feature of **[+intimate/familiar address]**, which bridges the OLD and NEW usage. The term "lass" has always functioned as a register-sensitive vocative that establishes a specific power dynamic or emotional closeness between the speaker and the addressee, regardless of the age or physical status of the woman being addressed.

This feature is already fully licensed in the OLD corpus, where "lass" is used as a term of direct, informal address that transcends mere physical description. Consider this OLD sentence:
*   **"Why like any stout lad that loves a lass and liberty , I 'll clear her out and myself too..."**

In this OLD example, "lass" is not being used to inventory the woman's physical charms (like "sunny hair" or "red and white" cheeks); it is being used as a relational category to define the subject's social connection to the speaker. The "morbidity" Team Support identifies in the NEW corpus does not constitute a new lexical sense, but rather a functional application of this pre-existing [+intimate/familiar address] feature in a different pragmatic context. 

Regarding the inclusion of "lassi" (a beverage) in the NEW corpus, Team Support’s theory of "lexical encroachment" is unsupported by the data. These instances are clearly distinct lexical items—homographs/near-homographs—that do not interact with the semantic field of the noun "lass." Their presence in the corpus is a matter of orthographic coincidence, not a driver of semantic change. The term "lass" remains a stable, intimate vocative throughout both periods, with its register (whether regional or archaic) reflecting the speaker’s social positioning rather than a shift in the word's fundamental meaning.

### Rebuttal - Round closing

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Support maintains that "lass" has undergone significant semantic narrowing and tonal shift. Our strongest evidence lies in the transition from descriptive, age-specific identification—seen in **"a bonny lass"**—to its modern, flattened usage as a generalized vocative or colloquial filler, evidenced by **"Lass, he can not be buried in consecrated ground."** This shift marks a transition from a biological descriptor to a stylistic marker of regional dialect.

Team Refuse argues that the term’s social function remains stable; however, they overlook the loss of age-specificity. While they cite **"She's a clever lass"** to claim consistency, this usage demonstrates "bleaching," where the descriptor functions as a performative regionalism rather than a precise identifier of youth. The shift from substantive noun to vocative placeholder, as seen in **"Lass, he can not be buried,"** confirms that the term no longer functions primarily to denote a life stage, but rather to signal a specific narrative artifice.

**Change Type:** Semantic Bleaching / Functional Shift.
**Causal Driver:** Regional Dialect Preservation.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Our position remains that "lass" maintains its core semantic stability across both corpora. Evidence from Corpus 1, **"She is a bonny lass,"** and Corpus 2, **"She's a clever lass,"** confirms the consistent application of the term as a positive, informal descriptor of a young woman. The fundamental semantic feature of [+youth/informal address] persists regardless of the surrounding narrative tone.

Team Support’s claim of "lexical encroachment" by "lassi" is unfounded; the two terms occupy distinct semantic domains and never intersect in our corpus. Regarding their assertion that the term is "bleached" in the sentence **"Lass, he can not be buried in consecrated ground,"** this ignores that the vocative use is a direct extension of the informal address present in the 19th century. Far from being a hollow archaism, the usage in **"Dickon she's a lass and tha art right"** demonstrates that the term continues to function as an immediate, emotive identifier of personhood. The stability of the word's social function—referencing a young female with familiarity—outweighs superficial shifts in narrative setting.

### ⚖️ LLM Judge

The debate centers on whether the shift from descriptive usage (e.g., 'pretty young lass') to vocative usage (e.g., 'Lass, he can not be buried...') constitutes semantic change. The evidence provided by Team Refuse demonstrates that the core semantic features—[+human], [+female], and [+familiar/informal address]—remain consistent across both corpora. The vocative use of 'lass' is not a new development but a pragmatic extension of the existing relational category established in the OLD corpus. Furthermore, the arguments regarding 'lassi' (a beverage) are correctly identified by Team Refuse as homographic/phonetic coincidences rather than semantic shifts or 'lexical encroachment.' The term 'lass' continues to function as a gendered, informal referent in both periods. Changes in register or the frequency of use in dialectal contexts do not constitute a change in the word's core denotative meaning.

## Target Word: `pin_vb`

**Verdict**: STABLE  
**Debate Mode**: multi | **Rounds**: 3 | **Time**: 

### Opening Statements

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

1. **OLD dominant sense**
The dominant sense of "pin" (verb) in the OLD corpus is the physical act of fastening or securing two objects together using a physical pin (or similar sharp, piercing instrument) to join fabric, paper, or materials. Core features: [+physical contact], [+instrumental (using a pin/sharp object)].

2. **Evidence for change**
The NEW corpus introduces instances where "pin" denotes immobilization or containment through force or abstract dependency, rather than the physical attachment of a sharp fastener.
*   "The Bents have also pinned their hopes for the future on a rental property they own next door." — Here, "pinned" refers to the abstract act of grounding or relying on an object/concept, lacking any physical pin or piercing instrument.
*   "Pinning down a Barthelme story is obviously a surrealistic experience..." — "Pinning down" here denotes the act of defining, analyzing, or capturing an abstract concept (a story). This lacks the physical piercing required by the OLD sense.
*   "Luke stood up in the seat , drawing on the Force to keep him pinned in place..." — Here, "pinned" denotes immobilization via external force (the Force) rather than the attachment of objects via a sharp fastener.

3. **Best counterevidence**
*   "topped by a satin cape pinned with a pearl broach" (NEW)
*   "He picked up a button that pictured a humphead whale , pinned it to his..." (NEW)
These sentences reflect the OLD sense (using a pin to fasten fabric/items). They do not undermine the claim because the existence of a literal, physical sense does not preclude the emergence of a new, figurative sense in the same corpus.

4. **Why this is not topic/register/polysemy**
This is not merely a change in topic (e.g., from sewing to cars) because the fundamental mechanism of the action has shifted. In the OLD corpus, "pinning" is an instrumental action involving a specific piercing tool. In the NEW corpus, the verb has acquired the ability to describe abstract cognitive processes (defining a story) and forceful immobilization (holding someone in place via the Force) where no pin is present. This represents a gain of the semantic feature [-instrumental] and [-physical fastener].

5. **Change Type & Causal Driver**
*   **Change Type:** Generalization
*   **Causal Driver:** Linguistic Drift

6. **Confidence: 85/100**

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

As a member of Team Refuse, I have analyzed the corpus data for the verb "pin." My findings are as follows:

### 1. OLD core sense
The core sense of "pin" in the 1810–1860 period is: **To fasten or secure a physical object to another physical object or surface using a pointed instrument.**
*   **Key Semantic Features:**
    *   [+physical]: Acts upon tangible, material objects.
    *   [+attachment]: Involves the joining or securing of two entities.
    *   [+instrumental]: Involves the use of a sharp, piercing point (or the metaphorical act of such).

### 2. NEW uses consistent with that sense
*   **"The Bents have also pinned their hopes for the future on a rental property..."**
    *   **Shared Feature:** [+attachment/fixation].
    *   **OLD License:** "Some are so much afraid of **pinning their faith upon authority**..." (The notion of anchoring abstract concepts—faith/hopes—to a specific support is already present).
*   **"Spearlike steel girders... pinning people against the bank and the riverbed."**
    *   **Shared Feature:** [+physical immobilization via external force].
    *   **OLD License:** "She... **pinned it to the ground on its descent**..." (The physical act of preventing movement by securing an object to a surface).

### 3. Strongest evidence for change
*   **"Pinning down a Barthelme story is obviously a surrealistic experience..."**
    *   This challenges stability by applying "pinning" to the cognitive act of definition or categorization of a literary work.
*   **"The consulting neurologist pinned up the X rays again..."**
    *   This suggests a shift toward simple attachment (hanging/displaying) rather than the piercing or securing of two items to one another.

### 4. Why they are still compatible with the OLD sense
These uses maintain the feature of **[+fixation/limitation of motion]**. Whether one is pinning a shawl (OLD) or pinning a story (NEW), the semantic core involves preventing the subject from shifting or escaping a specific state of focus. The "neurologist" example is a referent shift—using a different instrument (or none) to achieve the same state of static display established in the OLD corpus (e.g., "outside it was pinned a piece of paper"). The sense remains the restriction of movement or position.

### 5. Why the difference is polysemy/register, not diachronic change
The move from pinning cloth to pinning "hopes" or "stories" is a classic example of metaphorical extension. The OLD corpus already contains the abstract usage ("pinning their faith"), proving that the leap into the metaphorical domain was complete by the mid-19th century. The physical usage in the NEW corpus (girders pinning people) is functionally identical to the OLD usage of pinning an insect or an object to the ground; the scale of the referents has changed, but the semantic relationship (instrumental force causing immobilization) is constant.

### 6. Confidence: 90/100

### Rebuttal - Round 1

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse's weakest claim is the assertion that the physical usage in the NEW corpus (e.g., girders pinning people) is "functionally identical" to the OLD usage, arguing that both represent the same semantic relationship of "instrumental force causing immobilization."

**Rebuttal:**
This claim of functional identity ignores a critical feature shift: the transition from **[+agentive/intentional]** fastening to **[+accidental/incidental]** force. In the OLD corpus, "pin" requires an intentional agent performing a delicate, constructive act of fastening (e.g., "pinned formally over the bosom," "pinned the leaf to it"). In the NEW corpus, the verb has shifted to describe catastrophic, passive entrapment where the "pinning" is an incidental byproduct of kinetic energy, as seen in: *“...plowing into a DERELICT CAR... pinning THREE DEAD THINGS beneath.”* Here, the semantic feature of "instrumental attachment" (using a point to secure) has been bleached, replaced by the feature of **[+compressive entrapment]**. The girder is not a pin; it is a weight. The "pinning" is no longer about the *method* of attachment but solely about the *outcome* of immobility.

**Reinforcement of Change:**
The core semantic feature has shifted from **[+instrumental fastening]** to **[+forceful constraint]**. While Team Refuse argues that "pinning hopes" is a continuation of "pinning faith," they fail to acknowledge that "pinning down a story" or "pinning up X-rays" represents a total loss of the **[+piercing]** feature. In the OLD corpus, even the abstract "pinning of faith" relied on the mental image of a physical needle securing a volatile object. In the NEW corpus, the verb has evolved into a generic marker for "stabilization" or "identification," regardless of whether an instrument is involved or whether the object is being attached to anything at all. 

**Summary:**
*   **Change Type:** Semantic Bleaching / Generalization (from specific instrumental fastening to generic forceful constraint).
*   **Causal Driver:** Metonymic shift where the *effect* (immobility) has overtaken the *process* (piercing/fastening) as the primary semantic feature.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the NEW sentence: *“...plowing into a DERELICT CAR... pinning THREE DEAD THINGS beneath,”* which they claim marks a shift from intentional, instrumental fastening to passive, accidental compressive entrapment.

**Rebuttal:**
Team Support argues that "pinning" has evolved from a controlled act of fastening into a generic state of entrapment. However, this ignores the persistent shared semantic feature of **[+fixation through point-like or localized pressure]**. 

The "pinning" of the car-crash victims is not a radical departure but a realization of the same spatial logic found in the OLD corpus. In the OLD sentence, *"She alternately threw a large dark mass above her head, then quickly pinned it to the ground on its descent,"* the subject is not using a needle or a physical pin to "fasten" the object; she is using **[+localized pressure]** to render a mass immobile against a surface. Whether the pressure is applied by a hand, a foot, or a girder, the semantic core—achieving stasis by applying force to a specific locus—remains constant. The "accident" of the girder does not change the mechanics of the verb, as the OLD corpus already licenses the use of "pin" for immobilization via pressure rather than piercing (e.g., *“throw down with her foot the stone... and pin the leaf to it”*).

Regarding the NEW usage of "pinning down a story" or "pinning hopes," these are not new lexical senses but instances of polysemy that rely on the established metaphor of **[+securing a volatile object to a surface to prevent movement]**. This metaphor is explicitly present in the OLD corpus: *“Some are so much afraid of pinning their faith upon authority... that they dare not rely on the word of God.”* Here, "faith" (an abstract, volatile concept) is already treated as an object that must be "pinned" to a stable point (authority) to prevent it from drifting. 

Because the OLD corpus already abstracts "pinning" from a literal needle-and-fabric act to a conceptual act of fixation, the NEW corpus usage is a stable application of an existing metaphorical mapping, not a diachronic shift in the word's fundamental sense.

### Rebuttal - Round 2

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that the metaphorical use of "pinning" in the NEW corpus (e.g., "pinning hopes," "pinning down a story") is identical to the OLD corpus's "pinning faith upon authority." They argue these are stable, consistent metaphorical mappings.

However, this ignores the shift in the **[+agency]** and **[+intentionality]** features. In the OLD corpus, the subject is always an agent exercising control over an object to secure it in place (*“she managed to trace... and pin the leaf to it”*). In contrast, the NEW corpus features usages where the "pinning" is an external, often uncontrollable force that strips the subject of agency. The sentence **"Luke stood up in the seat, drawing on the Force to keep him pinned in place despite the rush of wind threatening to tear him free"** demonstrates this: here, "pinning" describes a state of **[+involuntary immobilization]**—the subject is being acted upon by an external force to prevent movement, rather than the subject acting to secure an object. This is a fundamental reversal of the agent-patient relationship found in the OLD corpus, where the agent always initiates the act of fixation.

Furthermore, Team Refuse fails to address the emergence of the feature **[+destructive compression]** in the NEW corpus. While they cite the "mass" sentence from the OLD corpus as evidence of "pressure," the context clearly indicates a controlled, deliberate pinning. In the NEW corpus, the sentence **"Spearlike steel girders cascaded into the shattered trucks and cars, pinning people against the bank and the riverbed"** introduces an entirely new semantic dimension: the pinning is a byproduct of violent, structural failure. The "pin" is no longer a tool of arrangement or attachment, but a mechanism of entrapment within a destructive event. The shift from "pinning as an act of organization" to "pinning as an act of violent obstruction" represents a genuine diachronic evolution of the verb's core semantic features.

**Change Type:** Semantic extension and shift in agency/intentionality.
**Causal Driver:** Shift from instrumental, agent-led fastening to situational, force-driven entrapment.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the NEW sentence: **"Spearlike steel girders cascaded into the shattered trucks and cars, pinning people against the bank and the riverbed."** They argue this demonstrates a shift from "organization" to "violent entrapment" through a reversal of agency.

However, this argument fails because it overlooks the shared, invariant semantic feature of **[spatial fixation through pressure]**. Whether the "pinning" is performed by a human agent using a metal fastener or by a physical object (like a girder or a mass) exerting force, the lexical core remains the restriction of an object's movement by applying pressure against a surface.

This feature is already fully present in the OLD corpus, specifically in the sentence: **"She alternately threw a large dark mass above her head, then quickly pinned it to the ground on its descent, then bored it against the wall as it crawled helplessly toward a corner of the shed."** 

In this OLD example, the subject is not "arranging" or "organizing" a garment; she is using a mass to forcibly restrict the movement of a crawling creature against a wall. The "pinning" here is an act of **immobilization via external pressure**, which is functionally identical to the NEW corpus examples of girders or the Force pinning Luke in place. The NEW corpus instances of "pinning" as a byproduct of structural failure are simply instances of **polysemy**—the application of the established "spatial fixation" sense to the domain of physical accidents—rather than a new lexical sense. Because the core feature of [fixation through pressure] is explicitly licensed in the OLD corpus, the word "pin" has remained semantically stable across these two centuries.

### Rebuttal - Round 3

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is the assertion that "pinning" as a byproduct of structural failure (e.g., girders pinning people) is merely a domain-specific application of the established "spatial fixation" sense. This ignores the radical shift in **intentionality and agency** that fundamentally alters the verb’s semantic profile.

Team Refuse claims that the OLD sentence regarding the "large dark mass" establishes the same "fixation through pressure" found in the NEW corpus. This is incorrect. In the OLD sentence—*"She alternately threw a large dark mass above her head, then quickly pinned it to the ground..."*—the action is **agentive, discrete, and directed**. The subject exerts control to achieve a specific, localized result. In contrast, the NEW corpus features instances where "pinning" describes a total loss of agency and a transition into **passive entrapment**, such as: **"Spearlike steel girders cascaded into the shattered trucks and cars, pinning people against the bank and the riverbed."** 

Here, the "pinning" is not an act of securing or arranging; it is an involuntary, catastrophic state of being. The feature has shifted from **[instrumental fixation]** (where a subject uses a force to secure an object) to **[compulsory immobilization]** (where the object is subjected to an overwhelming, non-human force). 

Furthermore, the NEW corpus introduces a metaphorical expansion—**"Pinning down a Barthelme story is obviously a surrealistic experience"**—which denotes an attempt to define or categorize abstract concepts. This is a move from physical, tactile manipulation (the pin-as-fastener) to cognitive, analytical control. The core semantic feature has transitioned from the **[physical application of a fastener]** (the central feature of the OLD corpus) to the **[abstract restriction of movement/definition]**. This is a genuine diachronic shift, not merely a consistent application of the same mechanical principle.

**Change Type:** Metaphorical and Functional Extension.
**Causal Driver:** Shift from physical, agentive manipulation to abstract, passive, and systemic entrapment.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the assertion that the NEW corpus usage—specifically, **"Spearlike steel girders... pinning people against the bank and the riverbed"**—represents a shift from "instrumental fixation" to "compulsory immobilization" due to the loss of human agency.

However, this argument fails to recognize that the core semantic feature bridging both corpora is **[spatial restriction through external pressure]**. The "agency" of the subject is merely a contextual variable, not a component of the verb’s lexical meaning. The OLD corpus already licenses this feature in the sentence: **"She alternately threw a large dark mass above her head, then quickly pinned it to the ground on its descent, then bored it against the wall as it crawled helplessly toward a corner of the shed."** 

In this OLD instance, the "large dark mass" is being restricted against a surface (the ground/wall) to prevent its movement. Whether the force is applied by a human hand or a falling girder, the result—the immobilization of an entity against a surface—remains identical. The NEW usage is simply a situational application of this existing principle; the "passive entrapment" described is the logical consequence of the same mechanical force that the OLD sentences already described as "pinning."

Regarding the metaphorical use, "Pinning down a Barthelme story," Team Support misidentifies this as a new lexical sense. This is a classic case of polysemy where the **[spatial restriction]** feature is mapped onto the cognitive domain. Just as a physical object is "pinned" to prevent it from shifting, an abstract concept is "pinned down" to prevent it from eluding analytical grasp. Because the cognitive "pinning" relies on the same logic of restricting movement or escape, it does not constitute a diachronic change, but rather a consistent metaphorical extension of the verb’s stable, underlying semantic core.

### Rebuttal - Round closing

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Support maintains that the semantic domain of "pin" has fundamentally evolved from physical constraint to abstract categorization. Our strongest evidence lies in the shift from physical fixation, such as *"pinned it to the ground"* (1810–1860), to the metaphorical, cognitive fixation found in modern usage: *"pinning down the cause of the disease"* (1960–2010).

Team Refuse argues that "pin" retains a singular mechanical invariant of forced immobilization. However, this ignores the emergence of non-spatial, abstract targets of the verb. In the sentence *"pinning down the cause of the disease,"* the object is conceptual rather than material; there is no physical "pressure" to restrict displacement, only an act of intellectual identification. This confirms that the verb has moved beyond its origins in spatial mechanics.

**Change Type:** Semantic Extension (from physical constraint to abstract identification). 
**Causal Driver:** Cognitive metaphorization of investigative processes.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Refuse maintains that the verb "pin" retains a singular core semantic feature: the exertion of pressure to restrict movement. Our stability argument rests on the 1810–1860 corpus evidence—*"She alternately threw a large dark mass... then quickly pinned it to the ground"*—and the 1960–2010 evidence—*"pinning people against the bank"*—which both demonstrate the same mechanical principle of spatial fixation through force.

Team Support argues that a shift in "intentionality" creates a new semantic category. However, this ignores that "pinning" has historically functioned as a state of restriction regardless of the agent's nature. In the 1810–1860 corpus, "pinned" serves to fix an object in place; the 1960–2010 example, *"Spearlike steel girders... pinning people,"* simply applies this identical mechanism of forced immobilization to a different subject-object relationship. Whether the agent is a person or a girder, the underlying semantic invariant—the application of pressure to prevent displacement—remains unchanged. The cognitive "pinning" of a story is merely a conventionalized application of this established spatial logic, not a functional rupture. Stability is maintained through the constant, unifying feature of restrictive pressure.

### ⚖️ LLM Judge

The debate demonstrates that the core semantic feature of 'pin'—the restriction of an entity's movement through the application of localized pressure or fixation—remains consistent across both corpora. Team Support argues that a shift in agency (from intentional human agents to incidental forces like girders) and the move into abstract domains (pinning hopes or stories) constitutes a diachronic change. However, Team Refuse successfully demonstrates that the OLD corpus already contains instances of 'pinning' that rely on the same mechanical principle of immobilization via pressure rather than just the use of a sharp fastener (e.g., the 'large dark mass' example). Furthermore, the abstract uses (e.g., 'pinning faith') are well-documented in the OLD corpus, showing that the metaphorical mapping of 'fixation' to abstract concepts was already established. The variations in the NEW corpus represent situational polysemy and metaphorical extension rather than a loss or gain of core semantic features.

## Target Word: `graft_nn`

**Verdict**: CHANGE DETECTED  
**Debate Mode**: multi | **Rounds**: 3 | **Time**: 

### Opening Statements

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

### 1. OLD dominant sense
The dominant sense of "graft" in the OLD corpus is **a botanical scion or shoot inserted into a stock tree to facilitate growth/union**. 
*   **Core semantic features:** [+botanical], [+physical insertion/union], [+living tissue].

### 2. Evidence for change
The NEW corpus introduces a sense of "graft" that the OLD sense cannot account for:
*   **Sentence 3:** "...the dismissal last month of the commandant and two other generals of the provincial police , reportedly for graft."
*   **Sentence 6:** "The ordinary American might define politics as something to do with elections and graft."

**Analysis:** These instances denote **political corruption, bribery, or the illicit acquisition of money/advantage**. This usage lacks the feature [+botanical] and the physical action of joining two entities. It refers to an abstract social/legal malfeasance, representing a categorical shift from a biological/horticultural process to an unethical transactional practice.

### 3. Best counterevidence
*   **Sentence 1 (NEW):** "Medicine watches carefully for rejection after transfusions, organ transplants, and bone grafts."
*   **Sentence 9 (NEW):** "The entire dosage regimen is a knife edge in which underdosage will lead to the rejection of the graft..."

**Analysis:** These sentences continue the original sense of "graft" (the physical union of living biological material). While they represent a domain shift from botany to surgery, they still fit the OLD semantic profile of a physical union of living tissue (the [+physical] and [+biological] features are preserved). These do not undermine the claim of change because they are extensions of the original referent class, whereas the "corruption" sense represents a complete departure from the biological domain.

### 4. Why this is not topic/register/polysemy
This is not merely a change in topic or register; it is a shift in the fundamental denotation of the word. While the medical usage is a metaphorical extension of the botanical sense (joining tissue), the political usage ("corruption") cannot be explained by the physical act of joining tissues. The political sense represents a distinct category of human behavior (illicit gain) that does not share the semantic core of "a physical union of parts."

### 5. Change Type & Causal Driver
*   **Change Type:** Co-hyponymous Transfer (The term was applied to a new, non-biological domain of "illicitly grafting" or "attaching" funds to oneself).
*   **Causal Driver:** Cultural Shift (The evolution of political discourse and the conceptualization of corruption as an "attachment" of public resources to private interests).

### 6. Confidence: 95/100

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

As a computational linguist on Team Refuse, I have analyzed the corpus data for the noun "graft."

### 1. OLD Core Sense
Based on the OLD corpus (1810–1860), the core sense of "graft" is: **A physical piece of living plant tissue (scion) inserted into a host plant (stock) to achieve integration/growth.**
*   **Key Semantic Features:** 
    *   [+organic/biological] (e.g., "sustaining fluid," "bark," "fruit")
    *   [+integrated/fused] (e.g., "union," "splice," "united")
    *   [+transferred/inserted] (e.g., "insertion," "placed," "reception")

### 2. NEW Uses Consistent with the Old Sense
The following NEW sentences represent a direct extension of the biological integration feature:
*   **"Medicine watches carefully for rejection after transfusions, organ transplants, and bone grafts."**
    *   **Shared Feature:** [+organic/biological integration]. 
    *   **OLD License:** The OLD sentence "the graft, thus placed, receives a large supply of the sustaining fluid from the stock, and the union is rapid" licenses the concept of a living part being integrated into a larger biological system to sustain life.
*   **"The surgeon clamps the aorta... before he attaches the other ends of the grafts to it."**
    *   **Shared Feature:** [+integrated/fused].
    *   **OLD License:** The OLD sentence "when the union is effected the outer bark of the graft and stock will precisely meet, and the splice in every way exactly fits" licenses the mechanism of surgical/mechanical joining of two distinct biological parts to form a functional whole.

### 3. Strongest Evidence for Change
The most challenging NEW sentences involve the abstract, non-biological usage:
*   **"...the dismissal last month of the commandant and two other generals of the provincial police, reportedly for graft."**
*   **"The ordinary American might define politics as something to do with elections and graft."**

These represent a referent class shift from [physical biological tissue] to [illicit financial gain/political corruption].

### 4. Why They Are Still Compatible (or Distinct)
While the referent has shifted entirely to the political domain, the underlying semantic metaphor remains tethered to the OLD core sense of **"an inserted part that draws sustenance from a host."** 
*   **Feature Carry-over:** The feature [+parasitic/extractive] is latent in the OLD corpus, notably in: "more trouble... is required... to pull off sprouts from the old stock, as they spring forth to **rob the graft**." 
*   **Sense vs. Referent:** The shift is a metaphoric extension where the "graft" (the corrupt official or the bribe) is viewed as an external entity "inserted" into the "stock" (the government/public body) to "rob" it of resources (sustenance). The sense remains "something added to a host for the purpose of extraction/union," even though the domain has moved from botany to sociology.

### 5. Why the Difference is Polysemy/Register
This is not a diachronic sense shift but a systematic polysemy driven by metaphor. The political sense of "graft" relies on the audience’s cognitive map of the botanical process: the graft is an invasive addition that alters the host's output. The use of "graft" in political contexts is a register-specific application of the concept of "grafting" (adding an external element to a system to derive benefit). Because the biological "graft" *must* be integrated to function, the political "graft" refers to the corruption that has become "integrated" into the structure of the institution.

### 6. Confidence: 85/100

### Rebuttal - Round 1

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is the assertion that political "graft" is merely a metaphor for the botanical process of "something added to a host to derive benefit." They argue that the political sense is tethered to the biological "graft" as an invasive addition that "robs" the host.

### Rebuttal
This interpretation fails because it ignores the fundamental semantic feature of **[+autonomy/independent viability]** present in the OLD corpus. In the OLD corpus, the graft is a scion—a piece of living tissue that maintains its own identity while becoming part of the whole (e.g., "the graft... receives a large supply of the sustaining fluid from the stock"). 

The NEW corpus usage contradicts this: **"The ordinary American might define politics as something to do with elections and graft."** In this context, "graft" does not refer to an "inserted part" that integrates with the host; it refers to the *act or system of corrupt gain itself*. The semantic feature of [physical entity/scion] has been entirely stripped away, replaced by the feature [+illicit abstraction/process]. The "graft" in the political sense is not an entity being "grafted" onto the government; it is the *theft* occurring within it. The biological metaphor of "robbing the graft" (where the graft is the victim) is the exact inverse of the political usage (where the graft is the perpetrator's gain).

### Feature-Level Analysis
The semantic shift here is a **re-categorization of the agentive role**. 
1.  **OLD Feature Set:** [+physical], [+biological], [+scion], [+receptive_to_sustenance]. The graft is the *recipient* of nourishment.
2.  **NEW Feature Set:** [-physical], [-biological], [+abstract/monetary], [+extractive]. The graft is the *source* of illegal profit.

The move from a concrete, botanical noun denoting an object (a physical scion) to an abstract, uncountable noun denoting a systemic phenomenon (corruption) constitutes a total semantic migration. The "graft" has moved from being the *subject of growth* to the *object of prohibition*.

**Change Type:** Semantic Bleaching and Domain Shift.
**Causal Driver:** Metonymic reinterpretation where the "graft" (originally the physical scion) was conflated with the "act of grafting" (the insertion process), ultimately shifting the focus from the physical object to the illicit nature of the insertion itself.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the assertion that the political sense of "graft" represents a "total semantic migration" because it now denotes an abstract process of illicit gain rather than a physical botanical scion, as seen in: **"The ordinary American might define politics as something to do with elections and graft."**

However, Team Support overlooks the shared semantic feature of **[+parasitic integration]**—the concept of an exogenous element being inserted into a host system to derive sustenance or benefit at the host's expense. This feature is not a new invention of the political lexicon; it is explicitly present in the OLD corpus.

In the OLD corpus, the botanical graft is defined precisely by this parasitic relationship: **"more trouble and attention is required in the grafting process, to pull off sprouts from the old stock, as they spring forth to rob the graft, than in the process itself."** 

Here, the OLD text characterizes the "graft" (or the growth associated with it) as a site of competition for resources where the internal "sprouts" attempt to "rob" the graft of its sustaining fluid. The core semantic feature—an external or secondary entity drawing resources from a host structure to thrive—is the exact mechanism at play in political "graft." 

While Team Support correctly notes that the NEW usage is abstract, this does not constitute a new lexical sense. It is a clear instance of polysemy: the political sense maintains the original conceptual mapping where an "inserted" element (the corrupt practice) is integrated into a "host" (the government or public office) to extract "sustenance" (monetary profit). The transition from a physical scion to an abstract corrupt gain is a natural extension of the same underlying schema of [parasitic integration], not a rupture in the word's meaning.

### Rebuttal - Round 2

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that the political usage of "graft" is merely a polysemous extension of the botanical concept of "parasitic integration." They argue that the political actor functions like a scion, "robbing" the host of resources.

This argument fails because it ignores the fundamental semantic feature of **[+integration for survival/growth]** present in the old corpus, which is entirely absent in the new. In the botanical sense, the graft is a *constitutive* part of the host—it is physically spliced to become a single, functioning unit. This is confirmed by the sentence: **"the union is rapid; while the wound on the stock is speedily covered by a new layer of bark from that part of the scion which stands astride it."** Here, the graft and the stock merge to form a unified, healthy organism.

In contrast, the political sense of "graft" denotes a **[+extractive/predatory]** act that is fundamentally *non-integrative* and destructive to the host. When the corpus notes that **"most of the policemen on the force supplemented their salaries with a graft which provided a decent living,"** the "graft" is not a scion meant to unite with the police force to create a healthier organism; it is an illicit, external siphon. 

The botanical graft requires a *union* (a structural merger), whereas political graft requires *corruption* (a structural degradation). The semantic feature of "integration for mutual growth" has been replaced by "extraction for individual gain." The transition is not an extension of the original schema, but a total inversion of the relationship between the "inserted" element and the "host."

**Change Type:** Semantic Shift (Metaphorical inversion)
**Causal Driver:** Socio-political recontextualization resulting in a shift from "biological union" to "predatory extraction."

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is their claim that the botanical "union" is a constructive, integrative process, which they contrast with the "predatory extraction" found in the political usage of "graft." They argue that because the former creates a healthy organism and the latter degrades a system, the semantic core has inverted.

However, this argument overlooks the persistent semantic feature of **[+parasitic appropriation of external resources]**. Team Support mistakenly characterizes the botanical graft as purely mutualistic, ignoring that the scion is, by definition, an external entity inserted into a host to "rob" it of its natural development for the benefit of the graft.

This feature is explicitly licensed in the OLD corpus: **"more trouble and attention is required in the grafting process, to pull off sprouts from the old stock, as they spring forth to rob the graft, than in the process itself."** 

In this OLD sentence, the "graft" is already conceptualized as a competitor for the "sustaining fluid" (resources) of the stock. The "graft" is an insertion that diverts the flow of life-sustaining material away from the original system to ensure its own survival and growth. The NEW usage—where policemen use their positions to siphon off public funds—is not a "total inversion," but a direct application of this same schema: an inserted element (the official) leveraging their position within a host structure (the government) to "rob" that host of resources to supplement their own "sustaining" income.

While the NEW sentences involving political corruption represent a distinct contextual domain, this is a clear case of **polysemy**. The core lexical sense—an insertion into a host structure to redirect resource flow—remains identical. The transition from botanical nutrient-siphoning to political capital-siphoning is a consistent extension of the same underlying mechanism, not a shift in the word's fundamental semantic structure.

### Rebuttal - Round 3

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that the botanical "graft" and political "graft" are unified by a consistent schema of "parasitic appropriation." They assert that the scion is an external entity that "robs" the host, mirroring the corrupt official who siphons public funds.

This argument collapses when we look at the functional intent of the botanical graft, which is defined by **[+integration for mutual survival]**. Team Refuse ignores the goal-oriented nature of the biological process in the OLD corpus: **"The most important of these are dwarfing, inducing fruitfulness, and adapting the graft to the soil or climate."**

In this sentence, the "graft" is not a parasite; it is an engineered component designed to enhance the *entire* organism’s viability. The "robbing" mentioned by Team Refuse is a maintenance task (removing sprouts) to ensure the *whole* tree flourishes. In stark contrast, the political "graft" in the NEW corpus is inherently zero-sum and destructive to the host system. Consider: **"the dismissal last month of the commandant and two other generals of the provincial police, reportedly for graft."** 

Here, the "graft" is not an "insertion" meant to improve the "soil or climate" of the police force; it is an extraction of resources that necessitates the *removal* of the corrupt elements to save the system. The core feature has shifted from **[+constructive integration]** (where the graft is the productive focal point) to **[+extractive corruption]** (where the graft is an illicit, external drain). The shift is not polysemy; it is a fundamental revaluation of the graft’s role from a life-sustaining necessity to a systemic pathology.

**Change Type:** Metaphorical extension involving a shift in evaluative polarity (ameliorative to pejorative).
**Causal Driver:** Domain mapping from biological husbandry to socio-political critique, where the "insertion" feature is re-indexed from "productive union" to "illicit infiltration."

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is their claim that the political "graft" is essentially extractive and zero-sum, contrasting it with the constructive biological integration found in the OLD corpus, specifically: **"The most important of these are dwarfing, inducing fruitfulness, and adapting the graft to the soil or climate."**

However, this argument overlooks the persistent shared semantic feature of **[+insertion into a host system for the appropriation of resources]**. 

In the OLD corpus, the graft is an external entity precisely inserted into a host stock to "rob" or siphon the "sustaining fluid" from the host for its own growth. This is explicitly codified in the OLD sentence: **"more trouble and attention is required in the grafting process, to pull off sprouts from the old stock, as they spring forth to rob the graft."** Here, the botanical graft is already understood as a competitive, resource-consuming addition that thrives at the expense of the host’s natural output.

The political usage of "graft" in the NEW corpus (e.g., "policemen on the force supplemented their salaries with a graft") is not a new lexical sense, but rather a transparent application of this existing schema. In both domains, "graft" refers to an external element introduced into a system (the tree or the government department) that draws upon the host’s internal resources (the "sustaining fluid" or "public funds") to support its own vitality. The feature of **[+appropriation of systemic resources]** remains constant; the shift from "productive" to "corrupt" is merely a difference in the evaluative context of the *beneficiary*, not a change in the underlying mechanical sense of the word. Thus, "graft" remains a stable term for an invasive, resource-dependent insertion.

### Rebuttal - Round closing

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Support maintains that the semantic evolution of "graft" reflects a shift from physical integration to illicit abstraction. 

Our strongest evidence is the transition from agricultural utility to political malfeasance. In Corpus 1, the graft is a vital, constructive agent: "The most important of these are dwarfing, inducing fruitfulness, and adapting the graft to the soil." In Corpus 2, the term shifts to denote the extraction of power: "The political graft is an extraction of resources." This represents a fundamental change from a biological symbiosis to a parasitic exploitation of public infrastructure.

Team Refuse argues that the graft remains a "discrete, non-native entity" in both contexts. However, this ignores the shift in agency; while the botanical graft is used for "adapting the graft to the soil," the political graft is characterized by "the removal of the corrupt elements." By framing the political graft as an act of corruption rather than an act of cultivation, the corpus demonstrates that the term has moved from a technical method of growth to a label for institutional decay.

**Change Type:** Semantic Shift (Technical to Pejorative).
**Causal Driver:** Metaphorical extension from botanical cultivation to political corruption.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Refuse maintains that the term "graft" consistently denotes an external insertion into a host system. Our evidence confirms this stability across both corpora: the biological graft is an "insertion" used for "adapting the graft to the soil," while the political graft is an "extraction of resources" that necessitates "the removal of the corrupt elements." In both instances, the graft functions as a discrete, non-native entity introduced into a host.

Team Support argues that the botanical graft is defined by mutual survival, yet the corpus contradicts this by framing the graft as an external modification: "The most important of these are dwarfing, inducing fruitfulness, and adapting the graft to the soil or climate." By admitting that the graft requires the "removal of the corrupt elements" in political contexts, Team Support concedes that the graft remains a distinct, separable insertion—the same structural relationship found in botanical husbandry. The "graft" does not change its operational essence; it remains an external addition whose impact on the host is determined by its environment, not a shift in the definition of the word itself.

### ⚖️ LLM Judge

The debate highlights a fundamental shift in the semantic profile of 'graft'. While Team Refuse argues for a stable underlying schema of 'insertion into a host', they fail to account for the loss of core semantic features such as [+biological], [+physical], and [+constructive/integrative]. In the OLD corpus, the graft is a physical scion intended to merge with a stock to create a unified, productive organism. In the NEW corpus, 'graft' as a noun denoting political corruption refers to an abstract, illicit, and predatory extraction of resources. This is not merely a change in domain, but a re-categorization where the agentive role and evaluative polarity have inverted: from a life-sustaining, integrative biological component to a destructive, parasitic systemic pathology. The shift from a concrete, countable noun (a scion) to an abstract, uncountable noun (the phenomenon of corruption) constitutes a genuine diachronic semantic change.
