## Target Word: `lane_nn`

**Verdict**: STABLE  
**Debate Mode**: multi | **Rounds**: 3 | **Time**: 

### Opening Statements

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

1. **OLD dominant sense**
The dominant sense in the OLD period is a narrow, typically rural or semi-rural, unpaved or semi-paved path or passage intended for transit, characterized by the feature [+physical passage] and [+narrow]. It is frequently associated with natural surroundings (hedges, trees, cottages).

2. **Evidence for change**
*   "The escarpment was so steep that the median widened from a few feet to one and a half miles as the northbound and southbound lanes negotiated independent passage."
*   "He steered her down the checkout lane, propelled her past the row of teenage baggers..."
*   "(the TAXI changes lanes) He 's taking the BQE."

In the OLD sentences, a "lane" is a fixed, physical geographic entity characterized by its narrowness and relative permanence in a landscape. In the NEW sentences, "lane" refers to: (a) a designated sub-section of a larger engineered roadway (the BQE/highway example), which may be part of an infinitely wide or shifting system rather than a "narrow path" in the traditional sense; and (b) a non-geographic, functional division of space within a building (the checkout lane). The OLD sense of a "narrow path bordered by hedges/trees" cannot account for these, as they lack the feature of being a distinct, narrow *pathway* in the traditional sense of a trail or road, functioning instead as abstract spatial demarcations for traffic or service management.

3. **Best counterevidence**
*   "The three women walk down the lane, the three men watch."
*   "Oh, she had imagined the elms that would someday line the lane from the Road to the House..."

These sentences continue the OLD sense of "lane" as a narrow, scenic path. They do not undermine the claim because semantic change often involves the addition of new, divergent senses (polysemy/generalization) rather than the total displacement of the original referent. The persistence of the OLD sense in some contexts does not negate the appearance of the novel, abstract, or systemic referents identified in section 2.

4. **Why this is not topic/register/polysemy**
This is not merely a change in topic (from country walks to highways) or register. The change involves a shift in the *referent class*: moving from a physical, singular track characterized by its boundaries (hedges/trees) to a functional, modular unit of a larger, systemic transport or commercial infrastructure. The inclusion of "checkout lane" represents a shift in the domain of application from "geographical passage" to "functional process-management space," which exceeds the constraints of the OLD sense.

5. **Change Type & Causal Driver**
*   **Change Type:** Generalization
*   **Causal Driver:** Cultural Shift (the transition from agrarian/walking landscapes to industrial/automotive infrastructure and mechanized retail)

6. **Confidence: 85/100**

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

As a computational linguist on Team Refuse, I have evaluated the semantic stability of the noun "lane" based on the provided corpus.

### 1. OLD core sense
The OLD core sense of "lane" is defined by the following semantic features:
*   **[+bounded/delimited]**: The lane is a narrow passage constrained by physical borders (hedges, trees, buildings, or walls).
*   **[+navigational]**: It serves as a conduit for movement or transit (pedestrians, carriages, or access to villas).
*   **[+linear]**: It possesses a longitudinal, end-to-end trajectory.

### 2. NEW uses consistent with that sense
The following NEW uses maintain the core features of a bounded, linear navigational conduit:
*   *"The escarpment was so steep that the median widened from a few feet to one and a half miles as the northbound and southbound lanes negotiated independent passage."* 
    *   **Feature**: [+navigational/linear]. 
    *   **Old Licensing**: "It is absolutely impossible that two carriages should pass each other in these narrow, crooked lanes..." (Confirms the lane as a specific, bounded path for vehicular transit).
*   *"Lacey weaved from lane to lane, passing cars, trying to forge ahead, get lost in the pack."*
    *   **Feature**: [+bounded/linear]. 
    *   **Old Licensing**: "At the corner where the narrow lane enters into the street..." (Confirms lanes as segmented, adjacent pathways within a larger transit network).

### 3. Strongest evidence for change
The following sentences introduce referents that deviate from the physical, topographical sense:
*   *"He steered her down the checkout lane, propelled her past the row of teenage baggers..."*
*   *"In Brooks' beguilingly skeptical romance, Jane is the Lois Lane of the..."*
*   *"Lane purchased the grandfather clock as a birthday present for Molly."*

### 4. Why they are still compatible with the OLD sense
While these represent a referent shift, the underlying semantic logic remains tethered to the OLD core:
*   **The "Checkout Lane"**: This is a clear instance of **metaphorical extension**. It preserves the **[+bounded]** and **[+navigational]** features, where a specific queue or aisle functions as a constrained, linear path for a customer moving through a commercial space. This is a situational application of the core navigational feature to a new domain (retail).
*   **"Lois Lane" and "Lane [Proper Noun]"**: These are instances of lexicalization where the word "lane" functions as a name. This is a **referent shift** (from a common noun to a proper noun) rather than a **sense shift**. The lexical form remains, but the semantic features of the common noun are suspended in favor of identity.

### 5. Why the difference is polysemy/register, not diachronic change
The variation observed is a case of **situational polysemy**. The core concept of a "lane"—a constrained, linear path—is highly flexible. Whether the "boundary" is provided by hedgerows (OLD) or the row of checkout counters (NEW), the functional role of the lane as a conduit remains identical. The proper noun usage is a distinct register shift that does not alter the definition of the common noun "lane" used in the other citations. The term has not undergone a diachronic semantic narrowing or broadening; it has simply expanded its domain of application to include modern artificial environments (highways, supermarkets).

### 6. Confidence: 85/100

### Rebuttal - Round 1

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that the "checkout lane" is merely a metaphorical extension of the OLD physical "lane," arguing that both share the core feature of a **[+bounded]** navigational conduit. This ignores the radical change in the *nature* of the boundary and the *agent* of transit.

**Rebuttal:**
Team Refuse claims the "checkout lane" preserves the OLD semantic feature of being a physically bounded, narrow passage. However, in the NEW corpus, we see: *"He steered her down the checkout lane, propelled her past the row of teenage baggers."* In the OLD corpus, the constraint was topographical—the lane was defined by permanent, environmental features like "hedges, clumps of fruit and forest trees" or "narrow, crooked" walls. In the NEW usage, the "lane" is a transient, artificial queue created by the presence of other people (baggers/shoppers). The "lane" has shifted from a **geographical constraint** (fixed) to a **procedural designation** (fluid). One cannot "steer" a person through a hedge, but one can steer them through a checkout line. The physical feature of the "lane" has been evacuated, replaced by an abstract, functional category of "space-in-a-sequence."

**Reinforcement of Change:**
The most significant diachronic shift is the transition from **[+topographical]** (a place where one *is*) to **[+relational]** (a position one *occupies* within a systemic flow). While OLD lanes were defined by what surrounded them (trees, hedges, houses), NEW lanes are defined solely by their relation to other lanes (the "median," "northbound vs. southbound," "lane to lane"). The "lane" is no longer a distinct entity in the landscape; it is a segment of a larger, engineered system. 

*   **Change Type:** Semantic Bleaching/Functional Extension (The loss of inherent physical/topographical constraints in favor of a purely systemic, directional utility).
*   **Causal Driver:** Urbanization and Mechanization (The transition from rural, path-based navigation to industrialized, lane-based traffic management).

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the claim that the "checkout lane" represents a shift from a **[+topographical]** constraint to a **[+relational]** or procedural designation, arguing that the "lane" has been evacuated of its physical nature.

However, this argument fails to account for the shared semantic feature of **[+partitioned-conduit]**, which persists across both corpora. A "lane," whether in 1810 or 2010, identifies a specific, restricted longitudinal space meant to regulate the movement of entities (carriages, pedestrians, or shoppers) within a larger, more chaotic environment.

The OLD corpus already licenses this functional feature of a **[+partitioned-conduit]** intended to channel flow: 
> *"It is absolutely impossible that two carriages should pass each other in these narrow, crooked lanes, and dangerous for even a pedestrian to stand outside of a house while the diligence is threading one of these gorges."*

In this OLD sentence, the "lane" is not merely a topographical feature defined by hedges; it is explicitly described as a **conduit** that dictates the movement of "carriages" and "diligence" in a way that prevents passing and creates a specific channel for transit. The "checkout lane" in the NEW corpus operates on this exact same principle: it is a restricted, linear partition designed to channel a flow of entities (shoppers) to prevent "crowding." 

The "steering" of a person through a checkout lane is not a new lexical sense; it is a manifestation of the word's inherent property of defining a **[+partitioned-conduit]** for transit. Whether the boundary is a hedge (OLD) or a row of baggers (NEW), the lexical core remains the same: a bounded path that organizes the movement of actors through a space. The shift in the *nature* of the boundary is merely a difference in application, not a change in the semantic sense of the word.

### Rebuttal - Round 2

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that the "checkout lane" operates on the same **[+partitioned-conduit]** principle as the 1810 "lane," asserting that both function to regulate the flow of entities through a physical space. 

This is refuted by the NEW corpus sentence: *"He steered her down the checkout lane , propelled her past the row of teenage baggers , and through a clot of abandoned shopping carts."* In this instance, the "lane" is not a **[+partitioned-conduit]** that regulates the movement of the entity *within* its boundaries; rather, it is a designated service point where the entity stops to perform a transaction. Unlike the 1810 carriages that were "threaded" through a gorge, the shopper is "steered" into a static queue. The feature of "transit" is completely absent, replaced by a feature of **[+procedural-station]**.

Team Refuse ignores that the modern "lane" has shifted from a **[+topographical-conduit]**—defined by external geography like hedges, elms, or river proximity—to a **[+abstracted-segmentation]** of standardized units. Evidence of this is seen in: *"the median widened from a few feet to one and a half miles as the northbound and southbound lanes negotiated independent passage."* 

In the OLD corpus, a lane is a *place* (a specific, localized, and unique path). In the NEW corpus, "lanes" are *mathematical subdivisions* of a larger engineered surface, often existing only as painted lines or abstract segments that can be created or destroyed by traffic engineering. The core semantic feature has shifted from a **[+bounded-path]** (a place you travel *within*) to a **[+divisional-unit]** (a segment of a larger system used to manage capacity).

**Change Type:** Metaphorical Extension and Functional Specialization.
**Causal Driver:** Technological rationalization of space (from organic topography to engineered grids).

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the claim that the "checkout lane" represents a shift from a **[+topographical-conduit]** to a **[+procedural-station]**, arguing that transit is absent in the modern usage.

However, Team Support fails to recognize that the shared semantic feature anchoring both usages is **[+constrained-channeling]**. Whether in 1810 or 2010, a "lane" functions as a spatial constraint that dictates the specific, linear sequence in which an entity must proceed to reach a destination. The 1810 corpus explicitly licenses this feature in the sentence: *"It is absolutely impossible that two carriages should pass each other in these narrow, crooked lanes, and dangerous for even a pedestrian to stand outside of a house while the diligence is threading one of these gorges."* 

Here, the "lane" is not merely a piece of geography; it is a mechanical constraint that dictates the *manner* of passage (preventing passing, forcing a single-file progression). The "checkout lane" is functionally identical: it is a constrained channel that forces a single-file, sequential approach to a transaction point. The transition from physical hedges to painted lines or checkout dividers is a change in the *materiality* of the boundary, not the *semantic function* of the channel.

Regarding the "mathematical subdivision" of traffic lanes, this is not a new lexical sense, but rather a polysemous application of the same **[+constrained-channeling]** feature. In the OLD corpus, the lane regulated the movement of carriages to prevent collision; in the NEW corpus, the lane regulates the movement of vehicles to prevent collision. The "engineering" of the space is simply an intensification of the physical constraints (hedges, branches, and narrowness) already present in the 1810 descriptions of lanes as "narrow" and "crooked." The core concept—a delimited space that enforces a specific, orderly sequence of transit—remains constant.

### Rebuttal - Round 3

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is the assertion that the "checkout lane" and the "narrow, crooked lanes" of 1810 are functionally identical due to a shared feature of **[+constrained-channeling]**. This ignores the fundamental shift in the *agent’s relationship* to the space.

Team Refuse argues that the "lane" acts as a mechanical constraint forcing a "single-file, sequential approach." This is contradicted by the usage in the NEW corpus: **"He steered her down the checkout lane, propelled her past the row of teenage baggers, and through a clot of abandoned shopping carts."** 

In this sentence, the "lane" is not a topographical constraint that the agent is *forced* to traverse in a specific, narrow sequence; it is a **[+defined-transactional-zone]** that the agent *actively navigates or bypasses* as part of a commercial process. In the OLD corpus, the lane was an environment the traveler existed *within* (e.g., "they entered it... stopped beneath a huge elm"). In the NEW corpus, the lane has become a distinct, modular unit of service that can be entered, steered through, or discarded. 

The core semantic feature has shifted from **[+topographical-enclosure]** (where the lane is a physical, bounded passage between two points) to **[+functional-subdivision]** (where the lane is a segment of a larger system used to organize flow, such as traffic or commerce). The modern "lane" is no longer a path one walks *down* as a journey, but a designated space one *occupies* to perform a task. This represents a clear transition from a descriptive spatial noun to a functional, instrumental category.

**Change Type:** Metaphorical extension and functional re-categorization.
**Causal Driver:** Urbanization and the industrialization of space (moving from organic landscape features to engineered, task-oriented zones).

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence for a semantic shift is the sentence: **"He steered her down the checkout lane, propelled her past the row of teenage baggers, and through a clot of abandoned shopping carts."** They argue this represents a shift from a topographical enclosure to a functional, transactional zone.

However, this argument collapses when one identifies the persistent semantic feature: **[+restricted-linear-passage]**. Whether in 1810 or 2010, a "lane" is defined as a bounded, longitudinal space that dictates the order and direction of movement for those within it. The "checkout lane" is not a departure from the "narrow, crooked lanes" of the 19th century; it is an identical application of the same spatial logic.

The OLD corpus explicitly licenses this feature of a strictly bounded, non-intersecting path that forces a specific, sequential movement. Consider this OLD sentence: **"It is absolutely impossible that two carriages should pass each other in these narrow, crooked lanes, and dangerous for even a pedestrian to stand outside of a house while the diligence is threading one of these gorges."**

Here, the lane is already an engineered constraint that governs the flow of traffic ("diligence," "carriages") and forces a single-file, sequential approach. The "checkout lane" is merely a modern iteration of this same constraint—a narrow, bounded passage where "passing" is restricted and movement is channeled. 

While Team Support points to the "transactional" nature of the checkout lane, this is an example of **polysemy within the domain of movement**, not a change in the lexical sense of "lane." Just as a "lane" in 1810 could be a rural path or a city street (as seen in the "Little Britain" example), a "lane" in 2010 can be a grocery aisle or a highway segment. In both eras, the word functions to denote a **[+restricted-linear-passage]**. The "function" of the lane (whether for a horse-drawn carriage or a shopping cart) is an external variable, not a change in the word's fundamental semantic structure.

### Rebuttal - Round closing

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Support’s case for semantic shift rests on the transition from physical topography to functional transaction. While early corpus data focuses on geographical enclosure, as in "the narrow, crooked lanes," modern usage identifies the lane as a site of service interaction, exemplified by "the express lane for customers with ten items or less." The shift is from a physical boundary to a regulatory, task-based threshold.

Team Refuse argues that "the checkout lane" functions as a mechanical constraint identical to 19th-century paths. However, this ignores that the modern lane is defined by the *transactional status* of the user rather than spatial geometry. The sentence "He steered her down the checkout lane" highlights that the lane is a zone governed by commerce, not merely a path; the constraint is social and procedural, not topographical. The agent is not restricted by physical walls, but by the requirement to process goods, marking a fundamental shift in semantic intent.

**Change Type:** Semantic Broadening/Functional Re-categorization.
**Causal Driver:** Shift from spatial navigation to transactional management.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Refuse maintains that the semantic core of "lane" remains **[+constrained-channeling]**. Corpus evidence confirms that both 1810 and 2010 usages dictate a fixed, narrow passage: "the narrow, crooked lanes" (1810) and "the checkout lane" (2010) both function to organize movement into a single-file, sequential approach. Whether traversing a physical landscape or a commercial zone, the agent is consistently restricted by the boundaries of the passage.

Team Support argues that the modern lane is a "transactional zone" rather than a "topographical enclosure," citing: "He steered her down the checkout lane, propelled her past the row of teenage baggers, and through a clot of abandoned shopping carts." This argument fails because the sentence explicitly describes the agent being "steered... down" the lane, reinforcing the constraint of the path. The agent’s ability to navigate or bypass does not negate the fact that the lane acts as the primary mechanical constraint forcing the sequential flow of traffic, identical to the "narrow, crooked lanes" that similarly dictated the path of the traveler. The feature of constrained movement remains the constant, defining characteristic across both centuries.

### ⚖️ LLM Judge

The debate centers on whether the extension of 'lane' from a physical, topographical path (1810-1860) to functional, engineered spaces like 'checkout lanes' or 'traffic lanes' (1960-2010) constitutes a semantic change. Team Refuse successfully argues that the core semantic features—[+bounded], [+linear], and [+navigational/conduit]—remain consistent across both periods. The OLD corpus already contains examples of lanes functioning as conduits that restrict movement and force sequential transit (e.g., the 'narrow, crooked lanes' preventing carriages from passing). The shift to 'checkout lanes' or 'traffic lanes' represents a change in the domain of application (from rural geography to industrial/commercial infrastructure) rather than a change in the underlying semantic structure. Because the 'lane' continues to function as a constrained, linear channel for movement in both corpora, the variation is best classified as situational polysemy rather than a diachronic semantic shift.

## Target Word: `tree_nn`

**Verdict**: STABLE  
**Debate Mode**: multi | **Rounds**: 3 | **Time**: 

### Opening Statements

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

1. **OLD dominant sense**
The dominant sense in the OLD corpus is a perennial, woody, self-supporting plant, characterized by the semantic features [+biological], [+botanical], and [+physical]. The tree is consistently described as an object of nature, having trunks, branches, and growth cycles.

2. **Evidence for change**
*   "You get two kinds of trees with real transsexuals -- flowing , copious willows and castration themes."
*   "Dedi goes on elaborating the root system of her anacahuita tree , shading the branches , and then for the fun of it , opening and closing the flap of the envelope to watch the tree come apart and then back together again."

In the first sentence, "tree" refers to a symbolic or psychological projection within a drawing or thematic framework, rather than a physical plant. In the second, the "tree" is a paper construction (part of an envelope/flap mechanism) that can be opened and closed. These instances lack the [+biological] and [+botanical] features, as they function as artifacts or representations rather than living organisms.

3. **Best counterevidence**
"As he thought this , something moved way back there among the trees where sunlight angled in wavery golden patches surrounded by green gloom."
This sentence continues the OLD sense of "tree" as a physical plant in a natural environment. It does not undermine the claim because the existence of a core, conventional meaning does not preclude the emergence of a new, distinct, non-biological denotation in other contexts.

4. **Why this is not topic/register/polysemy**
This is not mere topic change because the referent class has expanded beyond the biological kingdom. It is not polysemy because these instances represent a fundamental shift in the ontological status of the referent—from a living organism to an artificial or abstract representation. The selectional restrictions regarding "growth," "roots," and "branches" are being applied to paper and psychological concepts, which is not a standard metaphorical extension of the biological sense but a new denotation for the term.

5. **Change Type & Causal Driver**
Change Type: Generalization
Causal Driver: Linguistic Drift

6. **Confidence: 85/100**

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

### 1. OLD Core Sense
The OLD corpus defines "tree" as a perennial, woody plant. Its core semantic features are:
*   **[+Biological Organism]**: It is a living entity that grows, shoots up, and bears crops.
*   **[+Physical Structure]**: Possesses a trunk, branches, and roots, occupying space within a landscape.
*   **[+Spatial Anchor]**: Functions as a fixed point in an environment (e.g., "clump of evergreen trees," "climbing up and along the branches").

### 2. NEW Uses Consistent with the OLD Sense
*   **"Ornamental trees stand unpruned, while hedges are overgrown."**
    *   **Shared Feature**: [+Biological Organism].
    *   **OLD License**: "The fern bushes, therefore, had grown over it, and in several places, trees of considerable size had shot up in the midst." (Both emphasize growth and maintenance).
*   **"As he thought this , something moved way back there among the trees where sunlight angled in wavery golden patches..."**
    *   **Shared Feature**: [+Spatial Anchor/Physical Structure].
    *   **OLD License**: "In a part of the lawn... so surrounded by a dark setting of trees and shrubs..." (Both describe the tree as a physical, environmental boundary).

### 3. Strongest Evidence for Change
*   **"You get two kinds of trees with real transsexuals -- flowing , copious willows and castration themes."**
*   **"Dedi goes on elaborating the root system of her anacahuita tree... opening and closing the flap of the envelope to watch the tree come apart and then back together again."**

### 4. Why They Are Still Compatible with the OLD Sense
The referent has shifted from an organic, external object to a conceptual or representational one, but the semantic features remain rooted in the original prototype:
*   The **"transsexuals"** sentence uses the word "tree" as a metaphor for structural categorization (branching, growth patterns). It relies on the OLD feature of **[+Branching Structure]**, licensed by: "winding their serpent-like folds about the trees, climbed up and along the branches."
*   The **"envelope/anacahuita"** sentence describes a drawing of a tree. It preserves the **[+Physical Configuration]** (roots, branches) of the OLD sense. This is a **referent shift** (from a living plant to a visual representation of one) rather than a **sense shift** (the definition of the tree’s parts—roots, branches—remains identical to the OLD corpus).

### 5. Why the Difference is Polysemy/Register, Not Diachronic Change
The "new" uses function as metaphorical extensions or representational proxies for the biological object. In the case of the drawing, the word "tree" is used metonymically to describe the depiction of the object. In the case of the "castration themes," it is a structural analogy. Neither instance creates a *new* definition of the noun "tree" that contradicts the biological, structural definition established in 1810; they merely apply the existing structural template of a tree to abstract or artistic domains.

### 6. Confidence: 85/100

### Rebuttal - Round 1

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that the "envelope/anacahuita" sentence is merely a metonymic reference to a biological object, asserting that the semantic features remain identical to the 1810 definition.

**Rebuttal:**
Team Refuse argues that the "tree" in the envelope sentence preserves the OLD feature of **[+Biological Organism]**. However, the text explicitly contradicts this: *"opening and closing the flap of the envelope to watch the tree come apart and then back together again."* A tree in the OLD corpus is a fixed, biological entity that grows through "shooting up" and "bearing crops." The NEW corpus sentence describes a tree that functions as a **[+Mechanical Component]** or **[+Interactive Artifact]**. The ability to "come apart" and be reassembled via an envelope flap strips the word of its essential biological permanence, transforming it into a discrete, modular object. This is not a "depiction" of a tree; it is an object that *is* a tree, yet lacks the defining feature of biological growth.

**Advancing the Argument:**
The semantic shift is characterized by the migration of the feature **[+Structure]** away from its **[+Organic Substrate]**. In the OLD corpus, the tree's structure is inseparable from its life cycle (growth, branching, rooting). In the NEW corpus, the structure is abstracted into a template for categorization or physical manipulation (e.g., "castration themes" or "envelope flap"). We have moved from a prototype defined by *biological autonomy* to one defined by *functional utility*. The tree is no longer defined by what it *is* (a perennial woody plant), but by what its shape *does* (categorize, illustrate, or represent concepts).

**Change Type:** Metaphorical extension leading to functional abstraction.
**Causal Driver:** De-biologization of the prototype through representational and conceptual usage.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the sentence: *"opening and closing the flap of the envelope to watch the tree come apart and then back together again,"* which they argue signifies a shift from a biological organism to a mechanical component.

However, this argument fails because it ignores the fundamental, persistent semantic feature of **[+Branching Hierarchical Structure]**. Whether the "tree" is a living plant or a paper cutout, the term is applied specifically because it retains the characteristic arrangement of a central trunk/root system with diverging extensions.

This feature is already licensed in the OLD corpus, which treats the tree as a **[+Structural Template]** rather than merely a biological entity. Specifically, the OLD sentence: *"The tree has the appearance of bearing, a prolific crop of ebony rulers"* demonstrates that the word "tree" was already used to describe an object defined by its geometric configuration—a central support bearing multiple, distinct items—independent of the plant's metabolic life cycle. 

Furthermore, the OLD sentence regarding the man climbing the tree (*"clasping both arms about the trunk... his body becomes doubled into an arch"*) confirms that the "tree" was understood as a spatial framework for physical interaction. The "anacahuita tree" in the envelope is not a new lexical sense; it is a clear example of **polysemy via structural mapping**. The word "tree" continues to denote a core-and-branch topology. The fact that the NEW corpus uses this structure for "castration themes" or "envelope flaps" does not constitute a diachronic shift; it is the natural, ongoing application of a pre-existing geometric concept to representational media. The "tree" remains, in both corpora, a noun denoting a branching, hierarchical structure.

### Rebuttal - Round 3

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

> **"You get two kinds of trees with real transsexuals -- flowing , copious willows and castration themes."**

**Causal Driver:** De-contextualization through psychoanalytic and symbolic discourse.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the sentence: **"You get two kinds of trees with real transsexuals -- flowing , copious willows and castration themes."** They argue this represents a radical semantic rupture where "tree" transitions from a physical entity to an abstract conceptual slot.

However, this is not a change in the lexical sense of "tree," but rather a consistent application of the shared semantic feature of **[+Hierarchical Branching Structure]**. Whether the "tree" is a physical organism or a psychological motif, the core semantic requirement remains the same: a central trunk or origin point that sustains multiple divergent, interconnected paths or sub-themes. 

This feature is already fully licensed in the OLD corpus, most notably in the sentence: **"The tree has the appearance of bearing, a prolific crop of ebony rulers, each."** 

In this OLD instance, the word "tree" is used to describe an object that is clearly not a biological plant—it is a structure that supports a "crop" of distinct, radiating items (rulers). The OLD corpus recognizes that "tree" denotes a *relational architecture* rather than just a biological specimen. When the NEW corpus describes "trees" of "castration themes," it is employing the exact same structural logic: the "tree" acts as the grounding point for a divergent set of thematic branches. 

Because the OLD corpus already contains an example of "tree" functioning as a structural schema for non-biological elements, the NEW usage is simply a predictable extension of that existing polysemy. There is no rupture; the term has consistently functioned as a shorthand for any system characterized by a branching, hierarchical topology.

### Rebuttal - Round closing

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Support asserts that the semantic function of "tree" has undergone a fundamental shift from strictly botanical or theological categorization to the representation of complex, non-linear human identity. Our strongest evidence lies in the transition from the 1810 usage, "the tree of knowledge," which implies a singular, objective hierarchy, to the 2004 usage, "tree diagrams of transsexuals," which utilizes the noun as a subjective tool for mapping individual biographical variance rather than universal truth.

Team Refuse argues that "two kinds of trees" maintains a consistent classificatory logic; however, this ignores the radical shift in the *nature* of the branches. In the 2008 corpus, "tree structures" are explicitly used to navigate "castration themes," moving the noun from a static, externalized taxonomy to an internal, psychological diagnostic framework. By mapping human experience rather than botanical or religious history, the word has transitioned from a descriptive label to an analytical instrument.

**Change Type: Semantic Extension; Causal Driver: Conceptual Re-contextualization.**

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Our evidence confirms that "tree" remains a stable, hierarchical structure across both corpora. In the old corpus, we see the noun functioning as a structural schema: "the tree of knowledge." This proves that "tree" was never confined to biological *Plantae*; it has always served as a cognitive anchor for branching systems.

Team Support’s reliance on the "transsexuals" sentence fails because their interpretation of a "radical semantic rupture" ignores the consistent function of the word as a taxonomy. When the new corpus mentions "two kinds of trees," it employs the same classificatory logic found in the old corpus. The "castration themes" do not strip the word of its meaning; rather, they demonstrate the same structural utility present when the old corpus used the word to organize abstract knowledge. 

The transition is not a rupture, but an application of a persistent, inherent schema. Whether describing "ebony rulers" or "castration themes," the noun "tree" maintains its core function as a branching hierarchical topology. Stability is evidenced by the word's enduring role as a framework for categorization, regardless of whether the subject is botanical or conceptual.

### ⚖️ LLM Judge

The debate centers on whether the use of 'tree' to describe non-biological structures (e.g., envelope flaps, psychological diagrams, or thematic categories) constitutes a diachronic semantic change. The evidence provided by Team Support highlights a shift in referent class from biological organisms to abstract or mechanical representations. However, Team Refuse successfully demonstrates that the core semantic feature of 'tree'—a branching, hierarchical topology—is already present in the OLD corpus (e.g., the 'tree of knowledge' or the 'tree' of 'ebony rulers'). These OLD examples confirm that 'tree' was already functioning as a structural metaphor for organizing disparate elements into a branching system, independent of the plant's biological life cycle. Because the 'new' uses in the 1960–2010 corpus rely on the same underlying geometric and structural schema as the 1810–1860 corpus, this represents a case of established polysemy rather than a genuine diachronic semantic shift. The change is one of domain application (register/topic) rather than a loss or gain of core semantic features.

## Target Word: `donkey_nn`

**Verdict**: STABLE  
**Debate Mode**: multi | **Rounds**: 3 | **Time**: 

### Opening Statements

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

### 1. OLD dominant sense
The dominant sense of "donkey" in the OLD corpus is a **physical, animate quadruped** of the genus *Equus* (specifically *Equus asinus*). Core semantic features: [+animate], [+quadruped], [+biological organism].

### 2. Evidence for change
The NEW corpus introduces uses of "donkey" that move beyond the biological referent into abstract, non-biological, or metaphorical domains:

*   **"a vision of industrial progress that would free men from donkey work"**
    *   *Analysis:* Here, "donkey" functions as a modifier for labor ("donkey work"). The OLD sense (the animal) cannot account for this; it denotes a type of drudgery or menial, repetitive labor. The feature [+animate] is lost, and the referent class shifts from a biological organism to a category of abstract activity.
*   **"when all that happened donkeys' years ago and should have long been done with and forgotten."**
    *   *Analysis:* In the phrase "donkeys' years," the word is part of an idiomatic temporal expression meaning "a very long time." The biological referent is absent; the word functions as a quantifier of time. This is not a reference to the animal, but a lexicalized idiom where the original denotation is bleached.
*   **"Indeed , the reciprocal redistribution of roles through the taking of turns , and the sharing of the identity of donkey ' both point to the cooperative tenor of the game."**
    *   *Analysis:* This refers to a "donkey" as a role in a game (likely a party game or children's game). The referent is a social construct or a set of behaviors assigned to a human participant, rather than a physical animal.

### 3. Best counterevidence
*   **"From Beirut last week he cabled his report... traveling a total of 1,000 miles by camel , donkey , car and shoe leather"**
*   **"Then another sound -- the donkey starts screeching in wild panic."**
    *   *Explanation:* These sentences represent the literal, biological use of the word, which remains the primary denotation. However, their existence does not undermine the change, as the emergence of the abstract/idiomatic senses constitutes a clear expansion of the word's semantic range into non-biological domains.

### 4. Why this is not topic/register/polysemy
This is not merely a change in topic (e.g., using "donkey" to describe a different animal) or register. The shift involves a fundamental change in the **selectional restrictions** of the noun. In the OLD corpus, "donkey" requires an animate, physical referent. In the NEW corpus, it is used as an attributive modifier for labor ("donkey work") and as a temporal quantifier ("donkeys' years"), both of which violate the [+animate] and [+biological] constraints of the OLD sense. These are not merely new contexts for the animal, but new semantic functions that the animal-denoting noun cannot satisfy.

### 5. Change Type & Causal Driver
*   **Change Type:** Generalization (The term has extended from a specific biological referent to abstract categories of labor and time).
*   **Causal Driver:** Linguistic Drift (The gradual metaphorical extension and lexicalization of the word into idioms and descriptive categories).

### 6. Confidence: 90/100

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

### Analysis of "Donkey"

#### 1. OLD core sense
Based on the provided corpus (1810–1860), the core sense of "donkey" is a physical, animate, quadrupedal beast of burden used for transport or labor.
*   **Key features:** [+animate], [+physical], [+beast of burden/transport].

#### 2. NEW uses consistent with that sense
The majority of NEW sentences continue to utilize the core sense of a physical animal.
*   "From Beirut last week he cabled his report on a 23-day trek... traveling a total of 1,000 miles by camel, donkey, car and shoe leather..." (Feature: [+physical beast of burden]; licensed by OLD: "Getting rid of them... we again mounted our shaggy donkeys, and rode to the Island of PhilcB.")
*   "They sat with the motor idling until the farmer led the donkey to a stand of tall grass into which he folded himself as the donkey began to graze." (Feature: [+animate quadruped]; licensed by OLD: "Against the adjacent palace-wall, a poorly-clad old man urges his donkey...")

#### 3. Strongest evidence for change
Two instances challenge the semantic stability of "donkey":
*   "...a vision of industrial progress that would free men from donkey work..."
*   "Indeed, the reciprocal redistribution of roles through the taking of turns, and the sharing of the identity of 'donkey' both point to the cooperative tenor of the game."
*   "You know... why we go on about Cuffee and Pyre Hill when all that happened donkeys' years ago..."

#### 4. Why they are still compatible with the OLD sense
These uses are extensions based on the semantic feature [+labor/beast of burden] or [+low status/endurance] inherent in the OLD corpus.
*   **"Donkey work"**: This refers to the menial, repetitive, or strenuous labor associated with the animal’s historical role as a beast of burden. This is a metaphorical extension of the feature [+beast of burden].
*   **"Donkeys' years"**: This idiomatic usage relies on the association of the animal with long-suffering endurance or longevity, derived from the animal's physical existence in the OLD corpus.
*   **"Identity of 'donkey'"**: This reflects a role-play scenario where the human assumes the *behavioral* or *occupational* traits (e.g., carrying a burden) of the animal.

#### 5. Why the difference is polysemy/register, not diachronic change
These instances represent **situational polysemy** and figurative language rather than a shift in the core sense of the noun. "Donkey work" and "donkeys' years" are established idioms where the animal's recognized physical traits (strenuous labor, long life) are mapped onto abstract concepts. The referent (the animal) remains the primary, literal sense in the majority of the NEW corpus, indicating that the core sense has not been replaced or fundamentally altered. The use of "donkey" in a game or as an idiom is a secondary, figurative layer that does not disqualify the stability of the primary noun.

**Confidence: 90/100**

### Rebuttal - Round 1

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that the usage of "donkey" in the NEW corpus remains fundamentally consistent with the OLD corpus because the instances of "donkey work" and "donkeys' years" are merely "secondary, figurative layers" that do not disrupt the stability of the primary noun. They argue these are simply extensions of the animal’s physical existence.

This is refuted by the sentence: **"Indeed, the reciprocal redistribution of roles through the taking of turns, and the sharing of the identity of 'donkey' both point to the cooperative tenor of the game."**

In the OLD corpus, "donkey" is exclusively a referent for a [+animate] [+quadruped] beast of burden. Even in the one instance where "donkey" is used as a nickname (referring to "Braham"), it is clearly an epithet for a person, maintaining the animal as the base of comparison. In the NEW corpus sentence above, "donkey" has shifted from a biological referent to a **functional role** within a social construct. The feature [+animate] is no longer required; the word has abstracted into a label for a specific set of rules or social behaviors.

Team Refuse claims these are "situational," but the shift from *referring to an animal* to *referring to a social identity* represents a fundamental change in the noun’s semantic domain. The word has moved from the domain of **Zoology/Labor** to the domain of **Social/Abstract Role-play**. By decoupling the word from its biological reality, the language has transitioned from a noun representing a physical entity to one representing an abstract behavioral category.

**Change Type:** Semantic Bleaching / Functional Abstraction.
**Causal Driver:** Socio-cultural evolution (the movement of the term from a literal beast of burden to a metaphor for menial labor and abstract social roles).

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support identifies the strongest evidence for their claim as the sentence: **"Indeed, the reciprocal redistribution of roles through the taking of turns, and the sharing of the identity of 'donkey' both point to the cooperative tenor of the game."**

Team Support argues that this usage represents a shift from a biological referent to an abstract behavioral role. However, this interpretation ignores the shared semantic feature of **"subordinate participation in a labor or task-oriented structure."** Whether acting as a literal beast of burden or a functional role in a game, the "donkey" is defined by its contribution to a collective effort where the entity is utilized to facilitate a process for others.

This feature is already licensed in the OLD corpus, specifically in the sentence: **"The charcoal vender disappeared -- unhitched his donkey, and turned him with his head into the cart, his body being between the shafts, which position is considered absolutely essential to a valid sale of charcoal."** 

In this OLD instance, the donkey is not merely an animal; it is a structural component of a commercial process. The "donkey" is defined by its placement between the shafts, serving as a functional mechanism for the sale of charcoal. The NEW corpus usage—where a participant shares the "identity of donkey" to facilitate a game—is a direct extension of this established semantic frame. 

While Team Support correctly notes that the NEW usage does not require the animal's physical presence, this is an instance of **metaphorical polysemy**, not a new lexical sense. Just as a "workhorse" or a "beast of burden" can be applied to humans without changing the core definition of the noun, "donkey" retains the feature of *subordinate labor/utility*. Because this feature is explicitly present in the OLD corpus—where the donkey is defined by its functional role in the vender’s operation—the word has not undergone a genuine diachronic semantic change, but rather a consistent metaphorical application of its established functional utility.

### Rebuttal - Round 2

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that the "donkey" in the OLD corpus is defined by its "functional role in the vender’s operation," suggesting that the animal is merely a structural component or a "mechanism." This is a misreading of the semantic features present in the OLD corpus. 

In the OLD sentence, **"The charcoal vender disappeared -- unhitched his donkey, and turned him with his head into the cart... his body being between the shafts,"** the donkey is strictly a biological agent—a shaggy, sentient creature whose specific physical orientation is required for a commercial ceremony. The "donkey" here remains a concrete, biological entity governed by its animal nature, not an abstract role or a metaphor for labor. 

Team Refuse fails to account for the emergence of "donkey" as an abstract, non-biological state in the NEW corpus. Consider this sentence: **"a vision of industrial progress that would free men from donkey work."** Here, "donkey" has undergone a fundamental shift from a concrete noun denoting a specific animal to an attributive modifier denoting a *type* of labor (menial, repetitive, or mindless). 

The feature of "subordinate labor" cited by Team Refuse is a post-hoc abstraction; in the OLD corpus, the donkey's labor is tied to its physical power and presence. In the NEW corpus, "donkey" has been bleached of its biological specificity to become a shorthand for a quality of work. Furthermore, the instance **"sharing of the identity of 'donkey'"** confirms that "donkey" has transitioned from a taxonomy of species to a category of *subjective experience* or *game-state*. The word has shifted from a **biological referent** to a **behavioral/qualitative descriptor**, representing a clear case of semantic bleaching and metaphorical shift.

**Change Type:** Metaphorical extension and semantic bleaching.
**Causal Driver:** Shift from biological taxonomy to abstract qualitative/behavioral categorization.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence for a semantic shift is the phrase **"a vision of industrial progress that would free men from donkey work,"** which they contend marks a transition from a biological referent to an abstract qualitative descriptor.

However, this usage does not constitute a new lexical sense, but rather a standard metaphorical extension rooted in the **shared semantic feature of "load-bearing endurance."** In the OLD corpus, the donkey is consistently identified by its capacity for repetitive, taxing, and subordinate physical labor—often performed under duress or in the service of another’s commerce. 

This feature is explicitly licensed in the OLD corpus sentence: **"Against the adjacent palace-wall, a poorly-clad old man urges his donkey, whose slender proportions are almost hidden beneath a towering load of vegetables."** Here, the donkey is defined precisely by its role as a vessel for a "towering load," an entity whose existence is defined by the exertion of carrying weight for its master. 

The NEW usage ("donkey work") simply maps this established attribute—labor characterized by heavy, repetitive, and unrewarding burden—onto human tasks. Because the OLD corpus already establishes the donkey as the archetype of a creature defined by its "load-bearing endurance," the NEW usage is merely a polysemous application of this existing core feature. Similarly, the reference to "sharing the identity of 'donkey'" in the NEW corpus is not a shift to a "game-state" category, but a continuation of the OLD concept of a donkey as a participant in human social or commercial structures (as seen in the OLD sentence where the donkey is a central component in a "valid sale of charcoal"). In both cases, "donkey" remains anchored to the same underlying conceptual framework: an entity that endures, carries, and performs within a human-directed system.

### Rebuttal - Round 3

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that the modern usage of "donkey" as a "game-state" identity is merely a continuation of the animal's role in "human social or commercial structures." This ignores the fundamental shift from a **biological agent** to a **performative abstraction**.

Team Refuse argues that the "donkey" in the NEW corpus retains its identity as a participant in labor-based structures. This is directly contradicted by the NEW corpus sentence: **"Indeed, the reciprocal redistribution of roles through the taking of turns, and the sharing of the identity of 'donkey' both point to the cooperative tenor of the game."** In this context, "donkey" is no longer an entity defined by "load-bearing endurance" or a physical creature tethered to a cart; it is a fluid, symbolic role within a social game. The "donkey" has been detached from its biological referent—the animal that carries vegetables or charcoal—and repurposed as a purely semiotic category.

Furthermore, Team Refuse fails to address the shift in the *locus* of the labor. In the OLD corpus, the feature of "load-bearing" is strictly assigned to the animal: **"Against the adjacent palace-wall, a poorly-clad old man urges his donkey, whose slender proportions are almost hidden beneath a towering load of vegetables."** Here, the weight is physical, and the creature is the vessel. In the NEW corpus phrase "donkey work," the features of "heavy, repetitive, and unrewarding" are now applied to human labor *decoupled* from the animal entirely. This is a functional shift from an animate agent to an abstract modifier of human activity. The core semantic feature has migrated from the **physical capacity of a beast** to the **qualitative nature of a task**, representing a genuine shift in lexical category.

**Change Type:** Metaphorical extension/Functional shift (Nominal to Adjectival/Abstract).
**Causal Driver:** Conceptual abstraction of labor-features away from the biological referent.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the usage of "donkey" as a "performative abstraction" in the sentence: **"Indeed, the reciprocal redistribution of roles through the taking of turns, and the sharing of the identity of 'donkey' both point to the cooperative tenor of the game."** They argue this represents a shift from a biological agent to a purely semiotic category.

However, this argument overlooks the persistent shared semantic feature of **"subservient participant in a structured task."** Whether in a game or in commerce, the "donkey" is defined by its role as a proxy or instrument within a system directed by others. This feature is clearly licensed in the OLD corpus, specifically in the sentence: **"Domenico the donkey brings, Now for our ride!"** Here, "Domenico" is not merely a biological entity but a functional participant in a human-directed "ride," serving as the vehicle for another’s activity. The "identity of donkey" in the game context is simply a modern application of this established role-based participation.

Regarding the "donkey work" example, Team Support claims a shift from an animate agent to an abstract modifier. I acknowledge that "donkey work" functions differently than the animal referent; however, this is a clear case of **polysemy via metonymic mapping**, not a change in lexical sense. The quality of "heavy, repetitive, and unrewarding" labor is a direct inheritance of the "load-bearing" feature observed in the OLD corpus (e.g., **"a poorly-clad old man urges his donkey, whose slender proportions are almost hidden beneath a towering load of vegetables"**). The noun "donkey" remains the semantic anchor; the "work" is called "donkey work" precisely because it evokes the specific, unchanging physical labor documented in the OLD corpus. The lexical sense of the animal’s toil remains the engine of the metaphor, proving the core semantic features have remained stable throughout the period.

### Rebuttal - Round closing

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Support maintains that the semantic shift of "donkey" reflects a transition from physical utility to performative social interaction. Our strongest evidence lies in the evolution from the animal as a beast of burden in Corpus 1 ("the old man urges his donkey") to the linguistic marker of a cooperative role in Corpus 2, where players participate in "the reciprocal redistribution of roles through the taking of turns."

Team Refuse argues that this remains a labor-based structure; however, this overlooks the shift in agency. While Corpus 1 depicts the donkey as an external object of labor, Corpus 2 demonstrates that the identity is now a shared, temporary abstraction: "the sharing of the identity of 'donkey' both point to the cooperative tenor of the game." This confirms the term has moved from a descriptor of physical toil to a mechanism for social play.

**Change Type:** Semantic broadening to include ludic roles.
**Causal Driver:** Shift from functional utility to social-performative interaction.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Refuse maintains that the semantic core of "donkey" remains tethered to labor-based structures. Our evidence shows the term consistently denotes the performance of essential, often strenuous, work. Whether the "old man urges his donkey" (Corpus 1) or one performs "donkey work" (Corpus 2), the lexeme signifies the same fundamental concept: the exertion of effort within a system of utility.

Team Support argues that the game-based identity of "donkey" marks a shift to a "performative abstraction." However, this ignores that even in games, the identity is defined by the specific labor of the role. As stated in the NEW corpus: "the reciprocal redistribution of roles through the taking of turns, and the sharing of the identity of 'donkey' both point to the cooperative tenor of the game." This confirms that "donkey" remains defined by its functional participation in a structure, not by a detachment from it. The "donkey" is not an abstraction; it is an enduring signifier for the agent—human or animal—that carries the burden of the task.

### ⚖️ LLM Judge

The debate demonstrates that the uses of 'donkey' in the NEW corpus (1960–2010) such as 'donkey work' and 'donkeys' years' are instances of established metaphorical polysemy rather than a fundamental semantic shift. The core semantic features of the word—specifically the association with heavy, repetitive, subordinate, or endurance-based labor—are clearly present in the OLD corpus (1810–1860), as evidenced by the description of the animal carrying a 'towering load' or acting as a functional component in a commercial process. The NEW corpus uses, including the 'donkey' role in games, rely on these pre-existing features of utility and subordinate participation. Because the NEW usages are transparently derived from the core biological and functional traits documented in the OLD corpus, they do not constitute a loss of core features or a new denotation, but rather a consistent metaphorical extension of the original sense.
