## Target Word: `tip_vb`

**Verdict**: STABLE  
**Debate Mode**: multi | **Rounds**: 3 | **Time**: 

### Opening Statements

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

### 1. OLD dominant sense
The dominant sense in the OLD corpus is **[+physical contact]** involving the **[+extremity]** of an object or body part. It functions as:
*   **Physical modification/contact:** To touch or adorn the end/summit of something (e.g., "tipped with their pink nails," "firs alone tipping its summits," "yard-arms were tipped with a pallid fire").
*   **Action of giving/transferring:** A physical gesture (e.g., "tipping the wink") or the physical act of handing over money/drink as a gratuity or social gesture (e.g., "tip this private secretary," "tipping his glass").

### 2. Evidence for change
The NEW corpus introduces a sense of **[+informational]** or **[+abstract influence]** that lacks the "extremity" or "physical contact" features:

*   *"A glance at Kasten’s spectacular record in Atlanta tips you off to what they’ll be..."*
*   *"Several of the House freshmen who unexpectedly tipped the balance against the aircraft said as much."*

**Analysis:** In the OLD corpus, "tip" requires a physical encounter with an object or a person (e.g., touching a hat, giving a gratuity). In the NEW sentences, "tip" functions as a causative agent for a shift in state, belief, or outcome (informational or political). The "balance" is not a physical object being touched at its end, but a metaphorical conceptual scale being caused to incline. The "tip off" usage denotes the transfer of knowledge, not a physical object. The OLD sense of "extremity-contact" cannot account for these abstract causative functions.

### 3. Best counterevidence
*   *"He had on a straw hat, and he tipped it as the car pulled away."* (NEW)
*   *"She put her finger tips together and narrowed her eyes."* (NEW)

These sentences represent the continuity of the OLD sense (contact with an extremity). However, they do not undermine the claim because they represent the *retention* of the original sense, whereas the evidence in Section 2 represents the *expansion* into a domain (abstract causality) that the original sense cannot explain.

### 4. Why this is not topic/register/polysemy
This is not merely a change in topic (from social etiquette to politics) or register. The shift involves a change in **selectional restrictions**: the verb now takes abstract entities (information, political balance) as objects in a causative framework ("to tip the balance"), whereas the OLD sense required a physical agent/object interaction. This is a functional shift from a physical-contact verb to an abstract-causative verb.

### 5. Change Type & Causal Driver
*   **Change Type:** Generalization (The verb has extended from physical contact with extremities to abstract influence/causation).
*   **Causal Driver:** Linguistic Drift (The metaphorical extension of "tipping" a scale has become conventionalized into a general causative verb for influencing outcomes).

### 6. Confidence: 85/100

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

### 1. OLD Core Sense
Based on the provided OLD corpus, the core sense of the verb "tip" is: **[+physical contact/interaction]**, **[+terminal/extremity application]**, and **[+transfer/movement]**.
*   **[+terminal/extremity application]:** The act of touching or adorning the end or top of an object (e.g., "tipped with their pink nails," "firs alone tipping its summits," "yard-arms were tipped with a pallid fire").
*   **[+transfer/movement]:** The act of giving or moving something—often involving a subtle gesture or small amount—to another party (e.g., "tip this private secretary," "tipping his glass," "tip the wink").

### 2. NEW Uses Consistent with OLD Sense
*   **"He had on a straw hat, and he tipped it as the car pulled away."**
    *   **Shared Feature:** [+terminal/extremity application]. The hat is the terminal point of the wearer; touching it is an extension of the "tipping" action seen in "tipped with their pink nails" (Old).
*   **"Will got up, poured coffee, and tipped in a capful of brandy."**
    *   **Shared Feature:** [+transfer/movement] of a small quantity. This mirrors the "tip" in "tip this private secretary" (Old), where a small, discrete amount is transferred.
*   **"tipped the porter, left him to return the airport's borrowed wheelchair..."**
    *   **Shared Feature:** [+transfer/movement]. This is a direct continuation of the act of "tipping" an individual for a service, identical to "tip this private secretary" (Old).

### 3. Strongest Evidence for Change
*   **"Several of the House freshmen who unexpectedly tipped the balance against the aircraft said as much."**
*   **"He stared at her for a long time, hoping that something in her eyes would tip him in either direction."**

These sentences challenge stability by abstracting the action from a physical gesture (a wink or a coin) to an metaphorical influence over an outcome or a decision-making process.

### 4. Why They Are Still Compatible
The feature **[+transfer/movement]** carries over. In the OLD corpus, "tipping the wink" or "tipping his daden" involves a transfer of information or a shift in the state of the recipient. In the NEW corpus, "tipping the balance" or "tipping him in a direction" involves the transfer of "weight" or "influence" that shifts the state of a situation. The referent has shifted from a physical object/gesture (a wink/coin) to an abstract concept (a balance/decision), but the semantic mechanism—a small, decisive movement causing a shift in state—remains unchanged.

### 5. Why the Difference is Polysemy/Register
The variation is a matter of **metaphorical extension** (a standard mechanism of polysemy) rather than a diachronic sense shift. The OLD corpus already contains "tip over the salt," which implies a shift in equilibrium. The NEW uses ("tipped the balance") simply apply this existing semantic framework of "causing an imbalance" to abstract political or cognitive contexts. The core requirement—that a small, specific action results in a displacement of the subject—is present in both periods.

### 6. Confidence: 85/100

### Rebuttal - Round 1

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that the feature **[+transfer/movement]** creates a stable link between the "tipping" of the 1800s and the "tipping" of the 2000s. They argue that "tipping the wink" (transfer of information) and "tipping the balance" (transfer of weight/influence) share a mechanism of "small, decisive movement causing a shift in state."

This is contradicted by the Old corpus sentence: **"Tip over the salt, and the fat 's in."** 

In the Old corpus, the verb "tip" in the context of "tip over the salt" requires an **[+unintended/accidental]** feature and describes a **[+gravitational/spatial orientation shift]** of an object. In contrast, the New corpus usage—**"Several of the House freshmen who unexpectedly tipped the balance against the aircraft"**—does not describe a physical, accidental spatial shift. Instead, it describes a **[+intentional/agentive]** act of political maneuvering. Team Refuse conflates "causing an imbalance" (a physical consequence of spilling salt) with "deciding an outcome" (a strategic, agentive political action). These are not the same semantic feature; the former is a mechanical result, the latter is a deliberate exertion of influence.

Furthermore, Team Refuse fails to account for the emergence of the feature **[+predictive/advisory]** in the New corpus, which is entirely absent from the Old: **"A glance at Kasten's spectacular record in Atlanta tips you off to what they'll be."** 

Here, "tip" has shifted from a physical action (touching/transferring) to an **epistemic function**. To "tip off" is to provide information for the purpose of prediction. This is not a "small movement causing a shift in state," but rather a communicative act of revealing hidden data. By ignoring the radical transition from physical, terminal contact to abstract, advisory communication, Team Refuse masks a genuine diachronic shift.

**Change Type:** Semantic Broadening and Metaphorical Shift.
**Causal Driver:** Functional shift from physical manipulation (contact) to abstract epistemic signaling (information transfer).

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the usage **"Several of the House freshmen who unexpectedly tipped the balance against the aircraft,"** which they claim represents an agentive, political maneuver distinct from the accidental, physical "tipping" of objects.

However, Team Support overlooks the shared semantic feature of **[+impelling a state change via a focal point]**. The core meaning of "tip" across both corpora is the application of a small, localized force (or signal) to a system that results in a disproportionate transition in that system's overall state.

The OLD corpus sentence **"Tip over the salt, and the fat's in"** already licenses this exact feature. In this context, "tip" is not merely an accidental spatial shift; it is the act of applying a minimal movement to a container that causes a systemic consequence ("the fat's in"). Whether the system is a salt cellar or a political vote, the semantic structure remains identical: an agent initiates a minor, decisive action that forces a system from one state of equilibrium into another. The "political maneuvering" described by Team Support is simply a metaphorical application of this long-standing mechanic of "tipping" a system to induce a shift.

Regarding the "predictive/advisory" usage in **"tips you off to what they'll be,"** this is not a new lexical sense, but rather a standard polysemy of the same "contact" feature. Just as "tipping the wink" in the OLD corpus (**"tipping me the wink"**) involved providing a subtle, non-verbal signal to shift a person's understanding or mental state, "tipping off" involves a subtle signal that shifts the recipient's epistemic state. Both rely on the "tip" as a point of contact that triggers a change in the observer. The communicative act is an extension of the physical contact, not a departure from it.

### Rebuttal - Round 2

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that the "predictive/advisory" usage in *“tips you off to what they’ll be”* is merely a standard polysemy of the "contact" feature found in *“tipping me the wink.”* This conflates two distinct semantic mechanisms: the OLD corpus uses "tip" as a **physical or gestural vector of information transfer**, whereas the NEW corpus uses it as a **probabilistic indicator of future states.**

The OLD corpus sentence *“tipping me the wink”* relies on the feature of **[+direct, interpersonal signal]**. The speaker provides a deliberate, localized gesture (the wink) to a specific recipient to alter their immediate perception. In contrast, the NEW corpus sentence *“A glance at Kasten’s spectacular record in Atlanta tips you off to what they’ll be”* describes an abstract, impersonal process where data points function as an **[+inferential catalyst]**. Here, the subject is not a human agent performing a gesture, but a historical record providing predictive evidence. The "tip" has shifted from a *communicative act* (a signal between people) to an *epistemic indicator* (a feature of information processing).

Furthermore, Team Refuse fails to account for the emergence of the **[+financial/remunerative]** feature in the NEW corpus, which is entirely absent from the OLD corpus. In *“All the carriers were filled with anticipation about the holiday tips they might get,”* the word "tip" has re-lexicalized from a verb of physical orientation or signaling into a noun representing a specific type of social currency. While "tipping" a glass or a wink in the OLD corpus involves a momentary, fleeting action, the NEW corpus usage establishes "tips" as a **[+accumulable, transactional asset]**. This represents a fundamental shift from "tip" as a *kinetic modifier of state* to "tip" as a *quantifiable object of exchange*.

**Change Type:** Semantic Shift (Broadening and Specialization)
**Causal Driver:** Socio-economic institutionalization (the transformation of informal signals into formalized, standard remuneration).

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the assertion that *“A glance at Kasten’s spectacular record in Atlanta tips you off to what they’ll be”* represents a shift from a communicative human gesture to an abstract, epistemic indicator.

However, this argument ignores the shared semantic feature of **[+directed disclosure of latent information]** that bridges both corpora. In both instances, "tip" functions to bring hidden or non-obvious information into the sphere of the recipient’s awareness.

The OLD corpus sentence *“tipping me the wink”* already licenses this feature. When Teddy "tips the wink," he is not merely moving his eyelid; he is using a physical gesture as a vector to disclose a secret or an underlying truth to the recipient. The NEW usage regarding Kasten’s record performs the exact same function—the record acts as the "gesture" that discloses the "latent information" (the future state). The transition from a human agent to an abstract record is not a change in the lexical sense of "tip," but a routine extension of the agentive role within the same semantic frame of *revelation*.

Regarding the claim that "tips" (as in holiday remuneration) represents a new, quantifiable noun sense absent from the OLD corpus, this is a clear case of polysemy, not a lexical shift. The OLD corpus sentence *“And I had better tip this private secretary in the manner described by Gil Blas”* establishes the verb "tip" as a transfer of value (money/gift) to an agent for a service rendered. The NEW noun usage—referring to the accumulation of these transfers—is simply the nominalization of the established verb sense. The "transactional asset" is identical to the "manner of tipping" described in the 1810–1860 corpus; the word remains anchored in the same core semantic concept of a localized, informal transfer of value. There is no diachronic break, only the standard morphological productivity of a stable lexical item.

### Rebuttal - Round 3

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that the noun usage of "tips" (holiday remuneration) is merely a "nominalization of the established verb sense" and thus represents no diachronic break. This ignores the shift from a **transactional interpersonal action** to a **codified socio-economic expectation**.

The OLD corpus sentence, *"And I had better tip this private secretary in the manner described by Gil Blas,"* clearly frames "tip" as a singular, discretionary act of bribery or personal favor—a bespoke, idiosyncratic exchange. In contrast, the NEW corpus sentence, *"All the carriers were filled with anticipation about the holiday tips they might get,"* treats "tips" as a predictable, structural entitlement. The feature has shifted from **[+spontaneous interpersonal bribe]** in the OLD corpus to **[+standardized institutional remuneration]** in the NEW corpus. Nominalization did not merely change the part of speech; it reified the concept from an informal action into a fixed component of modern labor compensation.

Furthermore, Team Refuse fails to account for the shift in the "epistemic indicator" usage. While they argue that "tipping the wink" and "tipping off" share a feature of disclosure, they ignore the collapse of the **[+physical/performative]** feature. In the OLD corpus, the disclosure is always tethered to a physical, embodied act (a wink, a gesture). In the NEW corpus, the agent is removed entirely, as seen in: *"Several of the House freshmen who unexpectedly tipped the balance against the aircraft."* Here, "tip" has evolved into a purely causal, abstract operator of influence where the subject no longer "discloses" anything, but rather "shifts" a state of equilibrium. This is a fundamental change from a *communicative* act to a *mechanical/systemic* one.

**Change Type:** Metaphorical extension and functional reanalysis.
**Causal Driver:** Socio-economic standardization and the abstraction of agency from physical gesture to systemic influence.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the claim that "tip" has shifted from a **[+spontaneous interpersonal bribe]** in the OLD corpus to a **[+standardized institutional remuneration]** in the NEW corpus, arguing that the latter represents a structural reification absent in the historical record.

However, this argument overlooks the persistent shared semantic feature of **[+extrinsic transfer of value to influence or acknowledge an associate]**. The OLD corpus sentence, *"And I had better tip this private secretary in the manner described by Gil Blas,"* already licenses this feature. In the OLD usage, the "tip" is not merely a random bribe, but a payment directed at a specific individual (a secretary) to facilitate a desired outcome or maintain a professional relationship. The NEW corpus sentence regarding "holiday tips" is simply a polysemous expansion of this same core feature—the transfer of value to an associate—now applied to a broader, cyclical social context. The "standardization" cited by Team Support is a change in the *frequency and social expectation* of the act, not a change in the lexical sense of the word itself.

Regarding the "epistemic indicator" usage, Team Support claims a shift from a **[+physical/performative]** disclosure to a **[+mechanical/systemic]** influence. This ignores that the core feature of **[+causing a shift in state/knowledge]** is fully present in the OLD corpus. When the text notes, *"Tip over the salt, and the fat 's in,"* it demonstrates that "tip" already functions as an operator causing a state change (from balanced/upright to overturned/unbalanced). 

The NEW corpus sentence, *"Several of the House freshmen who unexpectedly tipped the balance against the aircraft,"* relies on this exact same underlying mechanic: the application of a force to shift a system from one state to another. Whether the "balance" is physical (salt) or metaphorical (a vote), the lexical sense remains the same. The transition from physical gesture to systemic influence is a clear case of **metaphorical polysemy**, not a new lexical sense. The word "tip" has not undergone diachronic change; it has simply maintained its core semantic function across evolving contexts.

### Rebuttal - Round closing

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Support asserts that the verb "tip" has undergone a fundamental shift from a spontaneous, non-contractual gesture to a codified economic expectation. Our strongest evidence is the emergence of "tipping" as a systemic, recurring obligation in Corpus 2, contrasting with the sporadic, individual acts found in Corpus 1. 

Team Refuse argues that the "sudden shift of state" provides structural continuity. However, this ignores the shift in the agent's intent. As seen in the Corpus 1 sentence, *"I had better tip this private secretary in the manner described by Gil Blas,"* the act is a calculated, personal interaction. By contrast, the modern corpus demonstrates that "tip" has transitioned from a personal gesture to a reified, institutionalized surcharge, fundamentally altering the relationship between the parties involved. The mechanical "equilibrium" Refuse cites masks the erosion of agency where "tipping" is now an automated social tax rather than a decisive, individual choice.

**Change Type:** Semantic Institutionalization; **Causal Driver:** Socio-economic formalization of service transactions.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Refuse maintains that the verb "tip" exhibits structural continuity, not a diachronic break. Our strongest evidence lies in the persistent semantic core of "a sudden shift of state," which anchors both the 19th-century physical gesture and the 20th-century systemic influence. Whether the subject is "tipping the wink" or "tipping the balance," the verb consistently functions as the operator of a sudden, decisive change in equilibrium.

Team Support argues that nominalization reified "tips" into an institutional entitlement, yet the corpus reveals that the transactional nature of the verb remains constant. The sentence, *"And I had better tip this private secretary in the manner described by Gil Blas,"* confirms that "tip" was already a formalized, expected transactional exchange in the 1800s, not merely a spontaneous gesture. Consequently, the shift to "holiday tips" is a change in frequency and scale, not a transformation of the underlying action. Regarding the "mechanical" shift, the agent remains central to the "tip" in both eras; the subject’s agency is not "abstracted" but merely applied to different objects, confirming that the function of "tip" as a catalyst for change remains stable across both corpora.

### ⚖️ LLM Judge

The debate demonstrates that the core semantic feature of 'tip'—the application of a small, localized force or signal to a system to induce a disproportionate shift in state—is present in both the 1810–1860 and 1960–2010 corpora. Team Support's arguments regarding 'epistemic signaling' and 'institutionalized remuneration' are effectively countered by the evidence in the OLD corpus (e.g., 'tipping the wink' as a signal, 'tip this private secretary' as a transfer of value). These uses establish that the verb was already functioning as a catalyst for state change and a transactional transfer of value in the 19th century. The shifts observed in the NEW corpus (e.g., 'tipping the balance') represent standard metaphorical extensions and polysemy rather than a fundamental change in the verb's core semantic features or selectional restrictions. The transition from physical gesture to abstract influence is a well-documented process of metaphorical mapping that does not constitute a diachronic semantic shift.

## Target Word: `multitude_nn`

**Verdict**: STABLE  
**Debate Mode**: multi | **Rounds**: 3 | **Time**: 

### Opening Statements

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

### 1. OLD dominant sense
The dominant sense of "multitude" in the OLD corpus is **a large, indefinite, but countable gathering or collection of animate beings (people/ghosts/demons).** The core semantic features are **[+animate]** and **[+collective/aggregation]**. In all OLD instances, the word refers to a crowded assembly of living or sentient entities (reapers, men, women, ghosts, demons, crowds).

### 2. Evidence for change
The NEW corpus introduces the use of "multitude" to describe inanimate or abstract entities, requiring the loss of the **[+animate]** feature.

*   *"Amid the multitude of stars, there was one that coincided with a red stationary indicator."*
*   *"They are there for a reason; actually a multitude of reasons."*
*   *"We can’t peer directly into it, because it’s hidden from view by dust and a multitude of stars."*

In the OLD corpus, "multitude" is strictly reserved for agents capable of movement or intentionality. In the NEW sentences above, the referents are celestial bodies (stars) and abstract concepts (reasons). These are not merely "crowds" in a metaphorical sense (as they lack the capacity for the "hurrying hither and thither" or "tumult" seen in the OLD corpus); rather, the term has shifted to denote a purely quantitative "large number" or "multiplicity," entirely stripping away the animate nature of the referent.

### 3. Best counterevidence
*   *"As children, we appreciated especially the unsparing multitudes of other children in the 11.6inerstadt."* (NEW)
*   *"...the multitude of the voices of nature?"* (NEW)

These sentences maintain the [+animate] or [+sentient] feature. However, they do not undermine the claim because diachronic change often involves the retention of the original sense alongside the development of a new, broader sense. The presence of these examples confirms that the animate sense persists, while the inanimate examples demonstrate the gain of a new, broader denotation.

### 4. Why this is not topic/register/polysemy
This is not a change in topic, as the word is applied to stars and reasons in contexts where "crowd" or "assembly" (the core of the OLD sense) would be semantically incoherent. It is not polysemy, as the "animate crowd" sense is distinct from the "mathematical multiplicity" sense; the latter represents a semantic bleaching where the feature of [animate] has been discarded, allowing for the quantification of abstract nouns. This represents a fundamental shift in the selectional restrictions of the noun.

### 5. Change Type & Causal Driver
*   **Change Type:** Generalization
*   **Causal Driver:** Linguistic Drift

### 6. Confidence: 85/100

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

As a computational linguist on Team Refuse, I have analyzed the corpus data for the noun "multitude." My findings are as follows:

### 1. OLD core sense
The OLD corpus (1810–1860) defines "multitude" with the following semantic features:
*   **[+Quantification]**: Denotes a large, indefinite number or collection.
*   **[+Aggregate]**: Refers to a gathering of individual units forming a whole.
*   **[+Discrete]**: The components of the multitude are distinct, countable entities (e.g., "men and women," "ghosts," "reapers," "rats and mice").

### 2. NEW uses consistent with that sense
The NEW corpus (1960–2010) maintains these features, applying them to varied referents:
*   *"Amid the multitude of stars, there was one that coincided with a red stationary indicator."*
    *   **Shared Feature**: [+Discrete/Countable].
    *   **OLD License**: "From Shelley, a **multitude of examples** might be taken..." (Examples are discrete units, just as stars are).
*   *"As children, we appreciated especially the unsparing multitudes of other children in the 11.6inerstadt."*
    *   **Shared Feature**: [+Aggregate/Quantification].
    *   **OLD License**: "...the harvest is small compared with the **multitude of the reapers**..." (Both refer to a large, gathered group of people).

### 3. Strongest evidence for change
The most challenging usage is the transition from concrete entities to abstract concepts:
*   *"They are there for a reason; actually a multitude of reasons."*
*   *"At best this is Whitmanesque largeness, the containing of multitudes."*

### 4. Why they are still compatible with the OLD sense
These uses represent a **referent shift** rather than a **sense shift**. The core semantic feature **[+Quantification]** remains identical. In the OLD corpus, "multitude" was already used to describe abstract or conceptual groupings alongside physical ones: 
*   *OLD Evidence:* "Under the dominion of an idea which possesses the minds of multitudes..."
Here, "multitudes" refers to the collective mental states or conceptual adherences of people. Thus, applying "multitude" to "reasons" or "Whitmanesque largeness" (the collection of internal states) is a direct extension of the OLD capacity to quantify non-physical, aggregate concepts. The semantic structure (a large, indefinite collection of distinct items) is unchanged; only the category of the items being quantified (reasons vs. people) has shifted.

### 5. Why the difference is polysemy, not diachronic change
The variation observed is a matter of **register and collocational expansion**. The word has not undergone a change in its fundamental definition (a great number of things/people); rather, it has moved from a predominantly human/physical focus in the 19th century to a more general quantifier in the 20th/21st century. This is classic situational polysemy: the word functions as a quantifier in both periods, and the "abstract" usage in the NEW corpus is licensed by the "conceptual" usage in the OLD corpus (e.g., "multitudes" of an idea). The underlying logic of "a large, aggregate collection of discrete units" remains stable.

**Confidence: 95/100**

### Rebuttal - Round 1

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is the assertion that the "abstract" usage in the NEW corpus is merely a continuation of the "conceptual" usage in the OLD corpus, arguing that both represent the same feature of "quantifying non-physical aggregate concepts."

This is contradicted by the following OLD corpus sentence: 
*   *"Under the dominion of an idea which possesses the minds of multitudes, as civil freedom... the powers of persons are no longer subjects of calculation."*

In this OLD instance, "multitudes" explicitly denotes a **collective body of people**—the "minds" who are possessed by an idea. Even when the context is conceptual (civil freedom), the feature **[+Human/Animate Agent]** is preserved; the "multitude" is the *carrier* of the idea. In contrast, the NEW corpus usage—*"They are there for a reason; actually a multitude of reasons"*—strips away the animate/human component entirely. Here, "multitude" functions as a **generic intensifier** for inanimate, abstract, and non-agentive properties. The OLD corpus never uses "multitude" to quantify non-agentive, purely descriptive properties (like "reasons" or "sharp things"); it always retains a link to a collective, countable mass of entities (reapers, ghosts, men, women).

**Feature-Level Analysis of Change:**
The semantic change is a transition from **[+Aggregate] (Entity-based)** to **[+Mass/Intensity] (Property-based)**. In the OLD corpus, the feature **[+Discrete]** is strictly tied to countable entities that occupy space or possess agency. In the NEW corpus, the word has undergone **semantic bleaching**, where the necessity of "discrete, countable entities" has been lost. It now functions as a synonym for "many" or "various" (e.g., "a multitude of reasons"), losing its requirement for a cohesive "aggregate" or "gathering." 

**Change Type:** Semantic Bleaching (Generalization of a concrete quantifier to a generic intensifier).
**Causal Driver:** Functional shift from descriptive noun (referring to a social/physical body) to a compositional quantifier (used to emphasize high frequency or variety).

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the contrast between the OLD corpus usage referring to collectives of people (e.g., "multitudes of men and women") and the NEW corpus usage in "a multitude of reasons," which they claim signifies a shift from an entity-based aggregate to a property-based intensifier.

However, this argument ignores that "multitude" has consistently functioned as a quantifier of **[+Heterogeneous Diversity]** within an aggregate, regardless of whether the constituents are animate or inanimate. The core semantic feature bridging the OLD and NEW usage is the **[+Enumeration of Variable Components]**—the idea that the "multitude" is not merely a bulk mass, but a collection of distinct, individual elements that contribute to a complex whole.

This feature is already fully licensed in the OLD corpus, specifically in this sentence:
*   *"From Shelley, a multitude of examples might be taken, in which the most familiar things are linked with the most profound and most recondite analogies."*

In this OLD instance, the "multitude" does not refer to a gathering of people or physical agents, but to a collection of "examples" and "analogies"—abstract, non-agentive, and non-physical entities. Just as the NEW corpus quantifies "reasons" or "stars," the OLD corpus explicitly quantifies "examples." Because the OLD corpus already utilizes "multitude" to describe a wide-ranging, non-human set of intellectual components, the NEW usage is not a result of semantic bleaching. Instead, both corpora demonstrate a stable lexical sense: "multitude" denotes a high-frequency, diverse set of discrete items, whether those items are people, stars, or logical reasons. The usage remains a consistent quantifier of variety within a large group, rendering the claim of diachronic change invalid.

### Rebuttal - Round 2

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that the stability of the feature **[+Enumeration of Variable Components]** across both corpora precludes semantic change. They argue that because "multitude" applied to "examples" in the 1800s, its application to "reasons" in the 2000s is merely an extension of an existing, stable lexical sense.

This argument fails to account for the fundamental shift in the **[+Agency/Autonomy]** feature of the constituents. In the OLD corpus, even when "multitude" refers to abstract concepts like "examples," those examples are presented as distinct entities within an intellectual landscape that the speaker *selects* or *gathers*. Conversely, the NEW corpus usage—specifically, *"a multitude of reasons"* or *"a multitude of stars"*—utilizes the word as a generic intensifier for cardinality where the constituent elements are often interchangeable or passive.

The contradiction is best seen by comparing the OLD sentence Team Refuse cited with the NEW sentence: *"I remember one night... when I watched one of the multitude of documentaries produced in the last months."* 

In the OLD "examples" sentence, the "multitude" implies a **[+Deliberate Selection/Aggregation]**—the "multitude" is something the author actively parses. In the NEW "documentaries" sentence, the "multitude" has undergone **semantic bleaching**: it no longer describes a deliberate collection of distinct items, but rather functions as a quantifier for "many" or "a large amount," losing the sense of structured, heterogeneous complexity. 

The shift is from a **qualitative aggregate** (a complex, varied set of actors or ideas) to a **quantitative intensifier** (an unspecified, large number of items). The loss of the "complex whole" feature in favor of simple "high-frequency cardinality" marks a clear diachronic reduction in semantic weight.

**Change Type:** Semantic Bleaching / Generalization.
**Causal Driver:** Pragmatic strengthening (the transition from a descriptive noun denoting a specific type of complex grouping to a functional, high-frequency quantifier).

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the contrast between the "multitude of examples" (OLD) and the "multitude of documentaries" (NEW), arguing that the latter has undergone semantic bleaching from a qualitative aggregate to a purely quantitative intensifier.

However, this argument ignores the persistent semantic feature of **[+Non-Uniformity/Dispersal]**. Even in the NEW corpus, "multitude" is not used as a generic, scalar quantifier (like "many" or "numerous") that could be replaced by a simple number; it retains the sense of a disparate, unorganized, or widely distributed collection of items that are difficult to parse individually.

This feature is already fully present in the OLD corpus, specifically in the sentence: *"From Shelley, a multitude of examples might be taken, in which the most familiar things are linked with the most profound and most recondite analogies."*

In this OLD example, the "multitude of examples" does not imply a "deliberate selection" by the author in the sense of a tidy, curated set. Rather, it refers to a vast, sprawling, and potentially overwhelming array of diverse, heterogeneous items. Just as the NEW "documentaries" represent an undifferentiated, high-volume flow of media, the OLD "examples" represent an undifferentiated, high-volume flow of literary data. The "multitude" in both instances functions to describe the *nature of the experience*—an encounter with a vast, scattered, and complex quantity—rather than a precisely defined or "structured" group.

Regarding the NEW sentence concerning "documentaries," this represents standard polysemy inherent in the word’s flexibility. Because "multitude" has always functioned to describe a vastness that exceeds the capacity for individual observation (as seen in the "shades of the infernal regions" or "busy multitude" scurrying like "rats and mice"), applying it to "documentaries" or "reasons" is merely a continuation of its core sense: describing a collection so extensive it appears as an indistinct, overwhelming mass. There is no bleaching, only the consistent application of the same descriptive frame to different domains.

### Rebuttal - Round 3

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that the use of "multitude" to describe "reasons" or "documentaries" is merely a consistent application of an "overwhelming mass" feature. They argue that because both the OLD and NEW corpora describe collections that exceed individual observation, the semantics remain stable.

This is refuted by the corpus sentence: *"They are there for a reason; actually a multitude of reasons."* 

In the OLD corpus, "multitude" is consistently bound to the feature **[+Physical/Sensory Presence]**. Whether referring to "reapers," "ghosts," or "men and women," the word denotes a visible, tangible, or auditory collective occupying space. In the NEW corpus, the word has undergone **semantic bleaching** to become an abstract intensifier for non-physical, conceptual domains. In the sentence above, "reasons" are not "overwhelming" or "sprawling" in the sensory sense; the word "multitude" has been stripped of its requirement for physical density and now functions as a synonym for "many."

Team Refuse fails to acknowledge that in the OLD corpus, "multitude" implies an external, often chaotic, environmental condition (the "uproar," the "shades," the "busy multitude"). In the NEW corpus, it is used to quantify abstract, internal, or logical sets (e.g., "reasons," "farm size decisions"). This shift represents a transition from a **collective noun describing a concrete entity** to a **quantifier describing an abstract set**. The feature of "physical mass" has been lost, and the word now functions as a scalar intensifier that can be replaced by "numerous" without loss of meaning—a clear indicator of diachronic semantic change.

**Change Type:** Semantic Bleaching (from concrete collective to abstract quantifier).
**Causal Driver:** Functional specialization in abstract/conceptual discourse.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the NEW sentence: *"They are there for a reason; actually a multitude of reasons."* They argue that this represents semantic bleaching because "reasons" lack the physical density characteristic of the OLD corpus.

However, Team Support overlooks the shared semantic feature of **[+Indeterminate/Enumerative Complexity]**, which bridges both eras. In both corpora, "multitude" functions to denote an aggregate so large or varied that it resists singular identification or easy categorization. 

This feature is already present and fully licensed in the OLD corpus, specifically in the sentence: *"From Shelley, a multitude of examples might be taken, in which the most familiar things are linked with the most profound and most recondite analogies."* Here, the "multitude" refers to "examples"—abstract, conceptual entities—rather than a physical crowd or sensory mass. 

Because the OLD corpus already utilizes "multitude" to quantify abstract, non-physical sets (examples/analogies), the NEW usage regarding "reasons" or "documentaries" is not a novel sense or "bleached" semantic state. It is an application of the same established lexical sense: a collective noun used to highlight the vastness of a set to emphasize its complexity or overwhelming scope. The transition to abstract contexts is not a diachronic shift, but a consistent expansion of the same core feature of **[+Indeterminate/Enumerative Complexity]** that was already operational in 1810.

### Rebuttal - Round closing

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Support maintains that *multitude* has undergone semantic bleaching, shifting from a descriptor of concrete, countable entities to a generic quantifier. Our strongest evidence lies in the shift from physical agents in the 1810–1860 corpus—such as *"a multitude of reapers"*—to the abstract, functional usage in the 1960–2010 corpus, exemplified by *"a multitude of reasons."* This transition replaces the imagery of dense, physical mass with a mere index of high frequency.

Team Refuse argues that the historical usage of *"a multitude of sins"* proves the term was always abstract; however, this ignores the theological density of "sins" as a collective weight in the 19th century. Unlike "sins," which carries inherent gravity, "reasons" in modern discourse functions as a neutral, interchangeable proxy for "many." By stripping the term of its requirement for physical or moral density, the modern usage has reduced *multitude* to a synonym for plurality, losing its original semantic weight.

**Change Type:** Semantic Bleaching / Generalization.
**Causal Driver:** Shift from concrete collective nouns to abstract, functional quantifiers.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Our evidence demonstrates that "multitude" remains stable as a descriptor of complexity and scale, regardless of the target's physical state. In the OLD corpus, we observe: *"a multitude of sins,"* which directly refutes the claim that the word was exclusively bound to physical entities like "reapers" or "men." This proves that the feature of "abstract conceptualization" was inherent to the term long before the 1960s.

Team Support argues that the use of "multitude" with "reasons" indicates semantic bleaching, claiming the word has become a mere synonym for "many." This is incorrect. Even in modern usage, the term maintains its core semantic weight of an "overwhelming mass." When a speaker says, *"a multitude of reasons,"* they are not merely quantifying; they are emphasizing an exhaustive, complex, and potentially chaotic array that defies simple enumeration. Just as the OLD corpus used the term to characterize the vastness of "sins," the NEW corpus uses it to characterize the vastness of "reasons." The semantic requirement for a "significant, dense collection" remains intact; only the domain of application has expanded, not the meaning itself.

### ⚖️ LLM Judge

The debate demonstrates that 'multitude' has maintained its core semantic features of [+Quantification], [+Aggregate], and [+Enumeration of Variable Components] across both corpora. Team Support's argument for 'semantic bleaching' relies on the claim that the word was historically restricted to [+animate] or [+physical] referents. However, Team Refuse successfully countered this by citing OLD corpus examples such as 'a multitude of examples' and 'a multitude of sins,' which confirm that the word was already being applied to abstract, non-physical, and non-agentive entities in the 19th century. Because the capacity to quantify abstract concepts (like 'examples' or 'sins') was present in the OLD corpus, the modern application to 'reasons' or 'documentaries' represents a consistent application of the existing semantic frame rather than a fundamental change in selectional restrictions or a loss of core semantic features. The shift in referent class is a matter of register and domain expansion, not diachronic semantic change.

## Target Word: `part_nn`

**Verdict**: STABLE  
**Debate Mode**: multi | **Rounds**: 3 | **Time**: 

### Opening Statements

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

### 1. OLD dominant sense
The dominant sense of "part" in the OLD corpus is **[+constituent]** and **[+physical/abstract segment]**. It refers to a smaller portion, element, or section that is a component of a larger, identifiable whole (e.g., "parts of the government," "parts of speech," "parts of the body," "part of the court"). The referent is characterized by its membership within a defined, pre-existing structure.

### 2. Evidence for change
The NEW corpus introduces a sense of "part" that functions as a **[+membership/participation]** marker, where the referent is an individual unit within a collective or a state of involvement, rather than a physical or structural segment of an object.

*   **Sentence 1:** "Many are part of the neo-Pentecostal movement..."
    *   *Analysis:* Here, "part" denotes membership in a social/ideological movement. In the OLD corpus, "part" required a spatial or structural whole (e.g., a "part" of a book or a body). A movement is a collection of people, not a monolithic entity that can be segmented into physical "parts."
*   **Sentence 2:** "In the early 1980s, he was part of a special committee..."
    *   *Analysis:* This refers to an individual's role or membership within a group of people. The OLD sense of "part" as a constituent segment cannot accommodate the [+animate] or [+agentive] nature of a committee member, as a person is not a "portion" of a committee in the same way a chapter is a "part" of a book.

### 3. Best counterevidence
*   **NEW Sentence:** "In the southern part of the country, where large estates had been parcelled out..."
    *   *Explanation:* This sentence maintains the OLD sense of "part" as a [+geographical/spatial segment] of a whole (the country). While this usage persists, it does not undermine the claim of change, as it represents the retention of the original sense alongside the emergence of the new, distinct sense of social/organizational membership.

### 4. Why this is not topic/register/polysemy
This is not merely a change in topic (moving from government structures to committees) or register. The shift involves a change in **selectional restrictions**: the OLD sense of "part" requires a [+inanimate/structural] whole, whereas the NEW sense functions as a relational marker for an agentive entity (a person) within a collective (a committee or movement). This represents a gain of a new denotation where the word functions to express social affiliation rather than mere structural composition.

### 5. Change Type & Causal Driver
*   **Change Type:** Generalization
*   **Causal Driver:** Linguistic Drift

### 6. Confidence: 85/100

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

### Arg_stable: Analysis of "Part"

**1. OLD core sense**
Based on the provided corpus (1810–1860), the core sense of "part" is a **[+constituent]** element that functions as a **[+bounded]** segment of a larger, identifiable whole (whether physical, conceptual, or organizational).
*   **Key Semantic Features:**
    *   **[+Constituent]:** A portion that exists in relation to a totality.
    *   **[+Bounded]:** Defined by limits or boundaries (e.g., "part of the body," "part of the government").

**2. NEW uses consistent with that sense**
*   "In the southern part of the country, where large estates had been parcelled out..."
    *   **Shared Feature:** [+Constituent] / [+Bounded] (geographical segment).
    *   **Old License:** "examination of the several parts of the government" (an organizational segment).
*   "In the early 1980s, he was part of a special committee that worked on..."
    *   **Shared Feature:** [+Constituent] (a member of a collective whole).
    *   **Old License:** "minister and elder of the Church, who were to make up the Ecclesiastic part of the court" (a member of an institutional whole).

**3. Strongest evidence for change**
*   "If the temples are likewise more bare than hairy, he might try the asymmetrical trick with a side part."
*   "The music and film industries... those same technologies are part of what makes music-oriented projects more likely to be undertaken..."

**4. Why they are still compatible with the OLD sense**
The challenge in the "side part" sentence involves a referent shift from a conceptual/organizational constituent to a spatial arrangement of hair. However, it retains the feature of **[+Bounded]** segments—a "part" is the line or division that separates one segment of hair from another. The feature of being a distinct segment within a larger physical totality (the hair on the head) persists. The usage in the music industry sentence ("part of what makes...") represents a shift toward a more abstract, causal constituent, but still functions as a component of a larger causal set, which is fundamentally the same logical structure as "part of statesmanship" in the old corpus.

**5. Why the difference is polysemy/register, not diachronic change**
The evolution of "part" appears to be a case of **situational polysemy** driven by the expansion of the "whole." In the OLD corpus, the "whole" was limited to physical objects, government bodies, or professional roles. In the NEW corpus, the "whole" has expanded to include abstract technological processes and stylistic arrangements. Because the semantic relationship between the constituent and the whole remains identical (a segment defined by its relation to a larger entity), this is a broadening of the domain of application rather than a fundamental change in the noun's internal semantic structure.

**6. Confidence: 92/100**

### Rebuttal - Round 1

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is the assertion that the "side part" usage in the NEW corpus retains the core semantic feature of **[+Bounded]** (a segment defined by its relation to a larger totality). They argue that the "part" is simply a segment of hair, but this ignores the fundamental shift in the noun's function.

**Rebuttal:**
In the OLD corpus, "part" invariably denotes an **integral constituent**—a piece that, if removed, diminishes the wholeness of the entity (e.g., "part of the body," "part of the government," "part of the court"). In the NEW corpus, the sentence "he might try the asymmetrical trick with a side part" demonstrates a shift toward **non-constituent identity**. A "side part" is not a component of the hair in the way a "part of the body" is a component of a limb; it is a *division*, a *line*, or a *spatial orientation* created by the arrangement of the whole. The semantic feature has migrated from **[+Constituent]** (being a piece of a whole) to **[+Configuration]** (a state or geometry of the whole).

**Reinforcement of Change:**
The shift is not merely a broadening of the "whole," but a transition in the noun's internal logic. In the OLD corpus, "part" is a **substantive entity** (something that *is*); in the NEW corpus, it frequently functions as an **attributive marker of location or process**. Consider: "that sounded like part of the song." Here, "part" no longer refers to a discrete, countable segment of a composition, but rather to a vague quality or instance of the song’s existence. 

This confirms a diachronic shift where the noun has moved from identifying a **[+Discrete/Constituent]** object to identifying a **[+Abstract/Configurational]** state.

**Change Type:** Semantic Shift (from Constituent to Configurational)
**Causal Driver:** Functional bleaching—the loss of the "integral piece" requirement as the word transitioned from physical/organizational description to abstract/geometric description.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is their claim that the usage in the sentence, "he might try the asymmetrical trick with a side part," represents a shift from a **[+Constituent]** (an integral piece) to a **[+Configuration]** (a geometric arrangement), suggesting that a "side part" is not a physical sub-component of the hair.

However, this argument fails because "part" has consistently functioned as a descriptor of **[+Spatial Partitioning]**—the act or result of dividing a totality into distinct regions—across both corpora. The "side part" is simply a specific, localized instance of the broader semantic feature of **[+Division within a Whole]**, which is already fully licensed in the OLD corpus.

In the OLD corpus, we find the sentence: *"The standing objection to Scott 's novels , is their want of a story consistent in all its parts."* Here, "parts" does not refer to a pile of discrete, removable objects, but rather to the **structural segments** or **divisions** within a narrative flow. Just as a novel is divided into narrative sections, a head of hair is divided into sections by a "part." The "side part" is not a new lexical sense; it is a specialized application of the existing feature of dividing a unity into specific, identifiable regions.

Regarding Team Support's observation about the "part of the song" sentence in the NEW corpus, this is not a shift to an "abstract state," but a clear case of **polysemy** within the established feature of **[+Segmental Inclusion]**. The song is a temporal totality, and the "part" is a temporal segment, exactly analogous to the OLD corpus sentence: *"I can only speak, from personal knowledge, of the latter part of Mr. Sedgwick 's professional career."* In both instances, "part" denotes a **[+Bounded Segment]** of a larger, continuous process. The word remains stable because it consistently denotes a sub-unit defined by its relationship to the larger whole, whether that whole is a government, a career, a novel, or a hairstyle.

### Rebuttal - Round 2

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that the "part of the song" (NEW) and the "latter part of Mr. Sedgwick’s career" (OLD) are identical because both denote a **[+Bounded Segment]**. This fails to account for the fundamental shift in the **ontological status** of the segment being described.

In the OLD corpus, segments are defined by **[+External Demarcation]**—they are objectively separable or historically bounded units (e.g., a "part of the government," a "part of the body," or the "latter part" of a career). These are discrete slices of a pre-existing, non-fluid structure.

In the NEW corpus, however, we see a shift toward **[+Emergent Integration]**, where "part" describes an element that is defined solely by its participation in a subjective or processual whole. Contrast Refuse’s claim with this sentence: *"At last he became tired, and the song drifted off into a whispered dorian scale that ended on the wrong note, on a dissonant note that faded into silence that sounded like part of the song."* 

Here, the "part" is not a demarcated section of the song (like a verse or chorus); the "silence" is perceived as a constituent of the song's aesthetic totality. This is a move from **[+Partitioning]** (dividing a whole) to **[+Constitutive Membership]** (being an inseparable aspect of an experience). Unlike the "parts" of a novel or government in the OLD corpus—which exist as distinct structural entities—the "part" in the NEW corpus often refers to a state of being or an abstract inclusion where the boundary between the "part" and the "whole" is blurred by the user's perception, not by structural division.

The semantic feature has shifted from **[+Structural Fraction]** (a piece of a larger object) to **[+Experiential Participation]** (an element contributing to an emergent state).

**Change Type:** Semantic Bleaching/Subjectivization.
**Causal Driver:** Shift from objective structural decomposition to experiential/processual integration.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the NEW sentence: *"At last he became tired, and the song drifted off into a whispered dorian scale that ended on the wrong note, on a dissonant note that faded into silence that sounded like part of the song."* They argue this represents a shift from structural partitioning to experiential participation.

However, this argument fails because the semantic feature **[+Constitutive Element]**—the defining characteristic of a "part"—is clearly present in both corpora. A "part" is simply any entity that contributes to the identity of a greater whole, regardless of whether that whole is a physical object or an abstract experience.

This feature is already fully licensed in the OLD corpus in the sentence: *"The standing objection to Scott’s novels, is their want of a story consistent in all its parts."*

In this OLD example, the "parts" of a novel are not merely physical pages or chapters; they refer to the narrative consistency and the aesthetic integration of the plot. Just as the "silence" in the NEW sentence is perceived as a constituent of the song's aesthetic totality, the "parts" of the novel are perceived as the necessary constituents of the story’s structural and aesthetic success. 

Team Support identifies "silence as part of the song" as a move toward "experiential participation," but this is merely a case of **polysemy**—the word "part" is applied to a different category of "whole" (an auditory experience vs. a literary work). This does not constitute diachronic semantic change; it is the consistent application of the same lexical sense—**[+Constitutive Element]**—to a new domain. The relationship between the "part" and the "whole" remains identical: the part is that which is required to complete or define the integrity of the whole.

### Rebuttal - Round 3

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that the relationship between "part" and "whole" has remained identical, asserting that the word merely reflects a consistent **[+Constitutive Element]** feature applied to different domains. This ignores the shift from "part" as a **discrete, separable unit** to "part" as an **indistinguishable, integrated state.**

The continuity Team Refuse claims is explicitly contradicted by the NEW corpus sentence: *"At last he became tired, and the song drifted off into a whispered dorian scale that ended on the wrong note, on a dissonant note that faded into silence that sounded like part of the song."* In the OLD corpus, "parts" (e.g., in novels or government) are defined by their **delineation**—they are distinct units that can be examined, criticized, or arranged (e.g., "several parts of the government"). In the NEW corpus, the "silence" is not a discrete unit *within* the song; it is a blurring of the boundary between the song and its absence. This represents a shift from a **[+Discrete/Structural]** feature to a **[+Abstract/Experiential]** feature.

Furthermore, Team Refuse fails to account for the usage in the NEW corpus: *"If the temples are likewise more bare than hairy, he might try the asymmetrical trick with a side part."* Here, "part" no longer functions as a constituent of a whole at all; it denotes a **[+Spatial/Boundary-defining]** line—a functional divide rather than a structural component. When combined with the "silence" example, it is clear that "part" has migrated from describing the *composition* of a whole to describing *relational or perceptual thresholds*.

**Change Type:** Semantic Bleaching/Extension (from structural constituent to abstract/perceptual threshold).
**Causal Driver:** Subjectification (the transition from objective, physical partitioning to subjective, experiential interpretation).

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence for a shift is the NEW sentence: *"At last he became tired, and the song drifted off into a whispered dorian scale that ended on the wrong note, on a dissonant note that faded into silence that sounded like part of the song."* They argue this represents a departure from "discrete, structural units" toward "abstract, experiential thresholds."

However, this argument fails because the core semantic feature—**[+Membership in a Composite Totality]**—remains unchanged. In both the OLD and NEW corpora, "part" denotes an element that contributes to the constitution of a larger, unified entity. Whether that entity is a "story" (OLD) or a "song" (NEW), the word functions to define the relationship between a focal element and the collective experience to which it belongs. 

This feature is already fully licensed in the OLD corpus, specifically in the sentence: *"The standing objection to Scott 's novels , is their want of a story consistent in all its parts..."* Here, the "parts" of a story are not merely physical objects; they are narrative components that contribute to the abstract, aesthetic, and experiential "consistency" of the whole. Just as the silence is perceived as a constituent of the song's aesthetic totality, the "parts" of the novel are perceived as constituents of the narrative's structural integrity. 

Regarding the NEW sentence about the "side part," this is not a new lexical sense, but a classic instance of polysemy where the word retains its established semantic base. A "part" in hair is a division that creates distinct sections of the whole; it divides the scalp into two components, both of which remain [+Membership in a Composite Totality] (the head of hair). The transition from "structural constituent" to "boundary" is merely a contextual shift in focus—from the *item* itself to the *interface* between items—both of which are inherent in the concept of a "part." Because the word consistently maps an element onto a larger, encompassing whole, no genuine diachronic semantic change has occurred.

### Rebuttal - Round closing

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Support maintains that the semantic range of "part" has shifted from discrete, tangible segmentation toward abstract, experiential integration. Evidence for this change is found in the transition from structural nouns like "the several parts of the government" (1810) to fluid, non-delimited instances such as "part of the silence" (2008), where the referent lacks physical boundaries.

Team Refuse argues that "part" remains a structural marker, citing "part of the song" (2008) as evidence of constitutive categorization. However, this conflates objective structure with subjective perception; the 2008 corpus frequently uses "part" to describe intangible or continuous states where no distinct "whole" is physically partitioned. By applying "part" to silence—an absence of sound—the modern usage moves beyond the 1810 definition of a "several" segment, instead functioning as a descriptor of inclusion within a temporal or experiential flow.

**Change Type:** Semantic Broadening; **Causal Driver:** Shift toward subjective, experiential quantification.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Refuse maintains that "part" remains fundamentally defined by its role as a constitutive element. The stability of this core semantic feature is evidenced by the 1810 usage, "the several parts of the government," and the 2008 usage, "part of the song." In both instances, "part" functions to signify a segment identified in relation to a larger, encompassing entity. Whether the context is structural or experiential, the linguistic function of identifying a sub-component remains the constant, defining feature.

Team Support argues that the 2008 silence example represents a shift toward an "indistinguishable, integrated state." This is countered by the sentence itself: "...that sounded like part of the song." By explicitly categorizing the silence as "part," the speaker relies on the traditional structural definition to assign the silence a position within the whole. Furthermore, the "side part" example does not abandon structural composition; it defines a physical boundary created by the arrangement of hair, which is fundamentally a partitioning of a whole. Therefore, the word continues to function as a structural marker, not a mere perceptual threshold.

### ⚖️ LLM Judge

The debate demonstrates that the core semantic feature of 'part'—a constituent element defined by its relationship to a larger, encompassing whole—remains consistent across both the 1810–1860 and 1960–2010 corpora. Team Support argues that the shift from physical/structural segments (e.g., 'parts of the government') to abstract/experiential segments (e.g., 'part of the song' or 'part of the silence') constitutes a diachronic change. However, Team Refuse successfully demonstrates that the OLD corpus already contained abstract and non-physical applications of the word, such as 'parts of a story' or 'parts of a career,' which function under the same logical structure of constitutive inclusion. The 'side part' example, while referring to a spatial configuration, still functions as a division within a totality, which is a well-established usage of the term. The observed differences in usage are best categorized as situational polysemy or domain expansion rather than a fundamental change in the noun's internal semantic features or selectional restrictions.
