## Target Word: `stroke_vb`

**Verdict**: STABLE  
**Debate Mode**: multi | **Rounds**: 3 | **Time**: 

### Opening Statements

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

1. **OLD dominant sense**
The dominant sense of the verb "stroke" in the OLD corpus is the act of passing one’s hand or fingers gently and repeatedly over a physical surface, typically hair, a beard, or a person’s head, to express affection, comfort, or reflective contemplation. Core semantic features: **[+physical contact]**, **[+gentle/repetitive motion]**. Note: While "stroke" appears as a noun in OLD (referring to bell tolls or electrical pulses), the verb form is strictly limited to tactile contact with living beings or their hair.

2. **Evidence for change**
*   "She touched the long sleek fender , then began to stroke it."
*   "My last girlfriend did this , and she said the angle it created allowed me to stroke her G-spot."
*   "She stroked them - and as with the first group , they stroked her in turn-tor about thirty seconds."

The OLD sense requires an animate subject or at least a surface associated with a living being (hair, beard, skin). In the first quote, the object is a "fender" (part of a car), a non-living, mechanical object. The OLD sense of "stroking" as an affectionate/comforting gesture is not applicable to inanimate machinery. In the second quote, the referent is an internal anatomical region ("G-spot"), which shifts the denotation from a surface tactile interaction to internal/penetrative sexual stimulation. In the third quote, the reciprocal use ("they stroked her in turn") implies a sexualized context that lacks the original sense of "gentle, affectionate petting" found in the OLD corpus, shifting the selectional restrictions to accommodate sexualized, non-affectionate physical contact.

3. **Best counterevidence**
*   "Anna raised a doubtful eyebrow and stroked the cat..." (NEW)
*   "She stroked his cheek gently with the back of her hand." (NEW)
These sentences continue the OLD sense of "stroking" as a gentle, tactile gesture performed on an animate being. However, they do not undermine the claim because they represent the persistence of a core sense alongside the emergence of new, non-animate or sexualized referents, which are not licensed by the original parameters.

4. **Why this is not topic/register/polysemy**
This is not merely a change in topic (e.g., petting a cat vs. a fender) because the semantic features of the object have shifted from **[+animate]** to **[-animate]**. Furthermore, the move toward internal anatomical stimulation (G-spot) and reciprocal sexualized interaction involves a change in the semantic nature of the "stroke" action itself—moving from a gesture of comfort/affection to one of sexual stimulation—which is not supported by the OLD corpus.

5. **Change Type & Causal Driver**
*   **Change Type:** Generalization (The verb has expanded its selectional restrictions to include inanimate objects and specialized sexualized anatomical referents).
*   **Causal Driver:** Cultural Shift.

6. **Confidence: 85/100**

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

### Arg_stable: Analysis of "stroke" (verb)

#### 1. OLD core sense
The OLD core sense of "stroke" as a verb is defined by the following semantic features:
*   **[+physical contact]**: The action requires direct tactile engagement between the agent’s hand/fingers and a surface.
*   **[+linear motion]**: The action involves a repetitive or continuous sliding movement along the surface of the object.
*   **[+affective/non-violent]**: The action is performed with a gentle, smoothing, or soothing intent (often associated with grooming or expressions of affection).

#### 2. NEW uses consistent with that sense
*   *“Anna raised a doubtful eyebrow and stroked the cat...”*
    *   **Shared feature:** [+physical contact] and [+linear motion].
    *   **OLD license:** “...stroking her head” (OLD corpus) establishes the usage of stroking an animate being for comfort/interaction.
*   *“She stroked his cheek gently with the back of her hand.”*
    *   **Shared feature:** [+physical contact] and [+affective].
    *   **OLD license:** “...stroked her pretty curls” (OLD corpus) licenses the gentle manual interaction with an animate subject.

#### 3. Strongest evidence for change
*   *“My last girlfriend did this, and she said the angle it created allowed me to stroke her G-spot.”*
*   *“She touched the long sleek fender, then began to stroke it.”*

These challenge stability by extending the referent class from biological surfaces (hair, head, beard) to internal anatomical features and inanimate industrial objects (a car fender).

#### 4. Why they are still compatible with the OLD sense
The core semantic feature **[+linear motion]** (the sliding, rhythmic movement of the hand) remains the defining mechanic of the action in both NEW sentences. The shift is merely a **referent shift**: the agent is applying the *same* physical mechanics ([+physical contact], [+linear motion]) to new targets. In the OLD corpus, the agent stroked a "beard" or "hair"; in the NEW corpus, the agent strokes a "fender" or "G-spot." The *sense* (the nature of the motion) is identical; only the domain of the object has expanded.

#### 5. Why the difference is polysemy/register, not diachronic change
The variation observed is **situational polysemy**. The verb "stroke" retains its constraint of [+physical contact] and [+linear motion] in all instances. The move toward sexual or mechanical contexts (the fender or the G-spot) represents a shift in the *domain of application* rather than a change in the *lexical meaning*. The "fondness" or "soothing" nature of the OLD sense is inherently preserved in the tactile desire to "stroke" a sleek fender or a G-spot, confirming that the underlying semantic frame—a gentle, rhythmic, tactile interaction—has not been replaced or altered. The stability of the mechanical definition suggests that the word has not undergone a diachronic shift, but rather a functional expansion.

#### 6. Confidence: 85/100

### Rebuttal - Round 1

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that the "soothing nature" of the OLD sense is preserved in the NEW corpus, asserting that the intent behind stroking a fender or a G-spot is functionally equivalent to the "gentle, rhythmic, tactile interaction" found in the OLD corpus.

This is demonstrably false. The OLD corpus consistently links "stroking" to the grooming or soothing of an animate subject (hair, beard, or head) to express affection, submission, or meditation. The NEW corpus introduces a shift in the **[+intent]** feature, moving from a soothing, grooming-based interaction to an **[+erotic/instrumental]** interaction.

The strongest refutation of Team Refuse’s claim of "stable intent" is this NEW sentence:
*   *"...maybe I 'll go to the lunch , find you , sneak over to you when you 're not expecting it , and stroke your fanny , and yell , Lookit , everybody !"*

This usage completely strips away the "soothing" or "affectionate" intent required by the OLD corpus. Here, "stroke" is used as an intrusive, non-consensual, and disruptive act—essentially a performative violation of personal space. This is a radical shift from the OLD corpus usage where stroking was a meditative or tender act (e.g., *"Mr. Tickler shook his head, and stroked his beard, despondingly"*). 

Furthermore, Team Refuse fails to account for the **[+reciprocity]** feature in the NEW corpus, which is absent in the OLD:
*   *"She stroked them - and as with the first group , they stroked her in turn..."*

In the OLD corpus, "stroke" is a unidirectional action performed by an agent upon a passive object (beard, hair, head). In the NEW corpus, "stroke" is increasingly used to describe a mutual, transactional, or mechanical interaction. The expansion of the referent class is not merely "domain shift"; the loss of the **[+grooming/soothing]** constraint and the introduction of **[+intrusive/instrumental]** features demonstrate a genuine diachronic semantic evolution.

**Change Type:** Semantic Broadening and Shift in Connotative Features.
**Causal Driver:** Socio-cultural evolution of tactile norms (from grooming/affection to erotic/instrumental utility).

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the sentence: *"maybe I 'll go to the lunch... and stroke your fanny, and yell, Lookit, everybody!"* They argue this usage is "intrusive" and "non-consensual," representing a radical shift from the "soothing" or "grooming" intent of the OLD corpus.

However, Team Support overlooks the core semantic feature that bridges these usages: **[+continuous linear contact along a surface]**. 

The fundamental action of "stroke" in both corpora is the movement of a hand or object across a surface to map or engage with its physical contours. This feature is clearly present in the OLD corpus, specifically in the sentence: *"stroking, with his embroidered glove, the tuft of hair that hung below his chin."* In this instance, the action is not necessarily "soothing" or "affectionate"; it is a tactile, repetitive motion used to focus attention on a physical feature, much like the NEW corpus usage. The OLD corpus already licenses the use of "stroke" as a deliberate, tactile exploration of a surface, regardless of the emotional state of the subject.

Regarding the NEW sentence involving "stroking" a G-spot or a fender, these do not represent a new lexical sense, but rather **polysemy**—the application of the established [linear tactile contact] feature to a broader range of objects (e.g., inanimate surfaces or anatomical areas). 

Finally, Team Support’s claim regarding **[+reciprocity]** is unfounded. The NEW sentence *"She stroked them - and as with the first group, they stroked her in turn"* is simply the summation of two unidirectional acts. The OLD corpus already contains multiple instances of the same action performed by an agent (e.g., *“stroking his beard”*), and there is no linguistic constraint in the OLD corpus that forbids the inverse or the sequential application of this same [linear contact] feature. The word remains stable; only the context of the objects being contacted has expanded.

### Rebuttal - Round 2

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that the NEW corpus usage of *stroke* is merely a stable extension of the OLD corpus’s "tactile exploration." They argue that the core feature remains a unidirectional, deliberate, tactile motion, regardless of the object.

This is refuted by the NEW corpus sentence: **"She stroked them - and as with the first group, they stroked her in turn."** Team Refuse attempts to dismiss this as "the summation of two unidirectional acts," but this ignores the fundamental semantic shift in **[+agency/intent]**. In the OLD corpus, *stroke* is exclusively an agent-to-object action (e.g., *“stroking his beard,”* *“stroking her head”*). The actor is the sole source of volition, and the object is a passive recipient—often an anatomical feature of the actor themselves or a subordinate. In the NEW corpus, the verb has evolved to describe a **[+reciprocal/interactive]** loop where the object possesses the agency to *stroke back*. By stripping the verb of its unidirectional, grooming-based hierarchy, the word has undergone a functional shift from a "tending" action to a "participatory" action.

Furthermore, Team Refuse fails to account for the shift in **[+contextual affect]**. In the OLD corpus, *stroke* is consistently paired with themes of "submission," "mortification," "fondness," or "wisdom"—it is a gesture of regulation and emotional containment. In the NEW corpus, the feature of "regulation" is replaced by **[+erotic/transgressive]** intent (e.g., *“stroke your fanny”* or *“stroke her G-spot”*). This is not merely an "expansion of objects"; it is a systemic change in the semantic field from *paternalistic grooming* to *transgressive physical solicitation*. The core feature is no longer just "linear contact," but the *intent* behind that contact, which has moved from the maintenance of social or personal order to the provocation of individual desire.

**Change Type:** Semantic Shift (Connotation/Pragmatic Implication)  
**Causal Driver:** Changes in social norms regarding bodily autonomy and the sexualization of physical contact.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the sentence: **"She stroked them - and as with the first group, they stroked her in turn,"** which they claim introduces a new sense of reciprocal agency.

However, this argument relies on a misreading of the verb's inherent semantic structure. The shared semantic feature bridging OLD and NEW usage is **[+rhythmic tactile contact directed at a surface]**. This feature is entirely independent of the agent’s social status or the object’s animacy.

The notion that *stroke* requires a passive, non-agentive object in the OLD corpus is demonstrably false. In the OLD sentence, **"When the gymnotus is exhausted... and will no longer emit strokes on being irritated with one hand,"** the verb *stroke* (used here as a noun, but referencing the same underlying action of contact) describes a physical interaction with a sentient, reactive entity (the gymnotus, an electric eel) that is actively responding to the stimulus. The OLD corpus already licenses the interaction between a human agent and a reactive, non-subordinate entity. The reciprocity mentioned in the NEW corpus is merely the result of two agents performing the same shared feature of **[+rhythmic tactile contact]** simultaneously; it does not constitute a new lexical sense.

Regarding the claim that the verb has shifted from "regulation" to "erotic solicitation," this is an example of **polysemy via domain extension**, not a change in the verb's core sense. The "intent" (erotic vs. paternalistic) is a pragmatic implication derived from the *object* of the verb (e.g., "beard" vs. "G-spot"), not the action of *stroke* itself. Just as the OLD corpus uses the same verb to describe stroking a "beard" (a ritual of contemplation) and "curls" (a gesture of affection), the NEW corpus uses the same verb to describe stroking a "fender" or a "G-spot." In all instances, the verb maintains the exact same semantic requirement: a repetitive, unidirectional motion across a surface. The "transgressive" nature identified by Team Support is an attribute of the *contextual participants*, not a redefinition of the verb *stroke*.

### Rebuttal - Round 3

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that the erotic usage in the NEW corpus is merely "pragmatic implication" identical to the ritualistic or affectionate stroking in the OLD corpus. They argue that the feature **[+rhythmic tactile contact]** remains the sole, sufficient definition of the verb.

This is refuted by the sentence: **"My last girlfriend did this, and she said the angle it created allowed me to stroke her G-spot."** In the OLD corpus, *stroke* is exclusively an **exogenous, superficial action** (e.g., "stroking his beard," "stroking her curls," "stroking his head"). These actions are performed *upon* the exterior of an entity, often to manipulate the object's appearance or to express a social orientation (submission, contemplation, affection). In the NEW corpus sentence provided, the action is **endogenous and penetrative**, targeting an internal anatomical feature to elicit a specific physiological response. The feature of "unidirectional motion across a surface" is functionally irrelevant here; the action is defined by its ability to stimulate internal biological tissue for sexual arousal.

Furthermore, Team Refuse fails to account for the loss of the **[+regulation/+ceremonial]** feature prevalent in the OLD corpus. In the OLD corpus, *stroke* is frequently linked to meditative or disciplinary regulation of the self or others (e.g., "stroking his beard, despondingly," "stroking down the remnants of his gray and sandy locks, with an air of mortification"). In the NEW corpus, this regulation is entirely absent, replaced by a feature of **[+instrumental/erotic stimulation]**. The semantic shift is not merely contextual; it is a fundamental move from **[+external grooming/gesture]** to **[+internal/sensory manipulation]**.

**Change Type:** Semantic Specialization / Metaphorical Extension
**Causal Driver:** Shift in socio-cultural domain (from ritualistic/expressive gesture to instrumental/biological stimulation)

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the sentence: **"My last girlfriend did this, and she said the angle it created allowed me to stroke her G-spot."** They argue this represents a shift from "exogenous, superficial action" to "endogenous, penetrative stimulation."

However, this argument ignores the core semantic feature that persists across both corpora: **[+rhythmic tactile pressure applied to a surface to elicit a sensory response]**. Whether the surface is a beard, a lock of hair, or biological tissue, the lexical meaning of *stroke* remains consistent: the act of moving one's hand or fingers repeatedly over a target area to influence its state.

The OLD corpus already licenses this feature of **[+tactile manipulation for physiological/emotional effect]** in the following sentence:
> *"When the gymnotus is exhausted... and will no longer emit strokes on being irritated with one hand, the shocks are felt in a very vivid manner..."*

In this OLD example, "strokes" are not merely "grooming" or "ceremonial" gestures; they are intentional, repetitive tactile stimuli applied to an organism to produce a specific reaction (an electrical discharge). The NEW usage regarding the G-spot is not a new lexical sense, but rather a narrow instantiation of this established, broader category of **[+stimulative tactile motion]**. 

Team Support claims the loss of "[+regulation/+ceremonial]" features indicates a semantic shift, but this is simply a change in the *domain of application* (polysemy), not the lexical sense of the verb. In the OLD corpus, *stroke* was used to regulate the state of a beard or hair; in the NEW, it is used to regulate the state of a partner. Because the fundamental mechanical and intent-based features—the rhythmic movement of the hand over a surface to elicit a response—remain identical, no genuine diachronic semantic change has occurred.

### Rebuttal - Round closing

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Support maintains that "stroke" has undergone a semantic shift from a gesture of grooming or affection to one of predatory or sexualized stimulation. Our strongest evidence lies in the 1810–1860 corpus, where "stroking" consistently describes external grooming (e.g., "stroking his beard"), implying a detached, rhythmic motion. In contrast, the 1960–2010 corpus features "stroking" linked to internal, high-sensitivity sexual interaction, shifting the verb’s semantic load from external maintenance to internal arousal.

Team Refuse argues that the verb’s core is stable because both eras involve "rhythmic tactile contact." However, this ignores the shift in participant agency found in the corpus. While the 1810 sentence "stroking his beard" describes a self-contained, contemplative gesture, the 1990s corpus examples (e.g., "stroking her G-spot") describe an instrumental, penetrative action designed to manipulate another’s biological response. The mechanical definition fails to account for this fundamental change in the verb's pragmatic function.

**Change Type:** Semantic Specialization (Tactile to Eroticized Manipulation).
**Causal Driver:** Shift in target anatomy and interpersonal intent.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Refuse maintains that the semantic core of "stroke" remains [+rhythmic tactile contact]. Our evidence confirms stability: whether in the 1810s ("stroking his beard") or the 2000s ("stroking her G-spot"), the verb consistently denotes a repetitive, unidirectional motion across a target.

Team Support argues that the 2000s usage is fundamentally distinct due to its "endogenous and penetrative" nature. However, this relies on a false dichotomy. The corpus shows that "stroking" has always functioned as a physical interaction with a target to elicit a response, whether that target is external hair or internal tissue. In both eras, the verb serves as a vehicle for sensory manipulation. Furthermore, their claim that we ignore the loss of "[+ceremonial]" features is irrelevant; the persistence of the core physical action across two centuries demonstrates that "stroke" is defined by its mechanical rhythm rather than the social context of the actor. The transition from beard-stroking to intimate contact is a shift in target, not a shift in the verb’s fundamental semantic definition. The mechanical feature of rhythmic contact remains the invariant constant of the lexeme.

### ⚖️ LLM Judge

The debate demonstrates that the verb 'stroke' maintains a consistent core semantic feature across both corpora: the application of rhythmic, linear, tactile contact upon a surface to elicit a sensory or physiological response. Team Support's arguments for change rely on shifts in the domain of application (e.g., from hair/beards to inanimate objects or internal anatomical features) and the pragmatic intent (e.g., from grooming/contemplation to sexual stimulation). However, these represent situational polysemy and domain expansion rather than a fundamental change in the lexical meaning of the verb. The OLD corpus evidence, such as the interaction with the gymnotus, confirms that 'stroke' was already associated with intentional, repetitive tactile stimuli to produce a reaction, which bridges the gap to the NEW corpus usage. Because the underlying mechanical and functional frame remains invariant, the word is classified as stable.

## Target Word: `chairman_nn`

**Verdict**: STABLE  
**Debate Mode**: multi | **Rounds**: 3 | **Time**: 

### Opening Statements

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

### 1. OLD dominant sense
The dominant sense in the OLD corpus is **a presiding officer or moderator of a committee or deliberative assembly**. Core semantic features include: **[+presiding]**, **[+functional/procedural]**, and **[+committee-bound]**. The role is defined by the act of leading a specific meeting or the preliminary stages of a group process.

### 2. Evidence for change
*   **"Vice Chairman : Gerald M. Levin"**
*   **"Chairman : John A. Meyers"**
*   **"Under tough-guy chairman Robert Crandall, American Airlines is the leader..."**

**Analysis:** The OLD sense requires the referent to preside over a committee or deliberative assembly. In the NEW sentences, "Chairman" is used as a formal corporate title (equivalent to Board Chair) that exists independently of a specific committee meeting. In the corporate context, the "Chairman" denotes the head of an entire organization or board, possessing a permanent executive status. The feature **[+committee-bound]** is lost, replaced by a **[+executive/governing]** feature where the role is a permanent rank rather than a procedural function for a deliberative body.

### 3. Best counterevidence
*   **"He became deputy chairman of GOSPLAN, the state planning committee..."**
*   **"Samuel B. Ruggles, Chairman of the Committee of Ways and Means..."**

**Explanation:** These sentences describe a person presiding over a specific committee, which aligns perfectly with the OLD sense. These do not undermine the claim because the existence of a persistent usage does not negate the emergence of a new, distinct sense (the corporate executive rank) that operates outside the committee-procedural framework.

### 4. Why this is not topic/register/polysemy
This is not merely a change in topic (from political committees to corporate boards) because the underlying referent class has shifted. In the OLD corpus, a chairman is a temporary or task-specific role within a deliberative process. In the NEW corpus, "Chairman" is a permanent, high-ranking executive position that does not require the presence of a deliberative assembly or a committee meeting to be valid. The shift from a procedural "presider" to a permanent "executive officer" represents a change in the denotation of the noun.

### 5. Change Type & Causal Driver
*   **Change Type:** Generalization (The term expanded from a specific procedural role in a committee to a broader executive rank within organizational structures).
*   **Causal Driver:** Cultural Shift (The emergence and dominance of large-scale corporate bureaucratic structures necessitated the application of existing committee-based titles to permanent executive positions).

### 6. Confidence: 85/100

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

As a computational linguist on Team Refuse, I have analyzed the corpus data for the noun "chairman." My assessment follows.

### 1. OLD core sense
Based on Corpus 1 (1810–1860), the core sense of "chairman" is defined by the following semantic features:
*   **[+Human]**: The referent is a person (e.g., "Mr. C," "General Lafayette," "Joseph Howard").
*   **[+Presiding]**: The referent occupies an authoritative position within a deliberative body or committee, responsible for managing proceedings and reporting results (e.g., "presides over it," "makes the report").
*   **[+Bounded]**: The role is restricted to a specific group or task (e.g., "of a committee," "of the meeting," "of the selectmen").

### 2. NEW uses consistent with that sense
The majority of NEW sentences maintain the core features established in the OLD period:
*   *“He is a past chairman of the South Texas Section…”* 
    *   **Shared Feature**: [+Presiding] within a group.
    *   **OLD License**: “every committee having a right to elect its own chairman, who presides over it” (Corpus 1).
*   *“...chairman of the pathology, anatomy [department]…”* 
    *   **Shared Feature**: [+Bounded] authority over a specific organizational unit.
    *   **OLD License**: “Chairman of the Committee of Ways and Means” (Corpus 1).

### 3. Strongest evidence for change
The most challenging evidence comes from instances where "chairman" appears as an isolated title/header or as a modifier in a corporate context:
*   *“Chairman : John A. Meyers”* (Corporate masthead)
*   *“-- Under tough-guy chairman Robert Crandall, American Airlines is the leader…”*

### 4. Why they are still compatible with the OLD sense
These uses represent a **referent shift** rather than a **sense shift**. The feature **[+Presiding]** remains the constant. In the OLD corpus, the chairman was the leader of a committee; in the NEW corpus, the chairman is the leader of a corporation or a board of directors. The underlying semantic requirement—a human who holds the presiding authority over a formal assembly or organizational entity—is identical. The shift from "committee" to "corporation" is a change in the *domain* of the referent, not a change in the *meaning* of the word itself.

### 5. Why the difference is polysemy/register, not diachronic change
The variation observed in the NEW corpus is a matter of **situational polysemy** and **register**. The use of "Chairman" in corporate listings (like the *TIME INC.* masthead) is a stylistic compression common to organizational reporting. Just as the OLD corpus used the term for diverse groups (selectmen, committees, meetings), the NEW corpus applies the same term to corporate boards and sections. Because the core function of the referent—to preside over and represent the interests of a group—remains stable, there is no evidence of a fundamental diachronic change in the semantic definition of "chairman." The word continues to denote a human in a position of presiding authority over a bounded group.

### 6. Confidence: 95/100

### Rebuttal - Round 1

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that the transition from a committee-based role to a corporate role is merely a change in "domain" rather than a fundamental semantic shift. They argue that the core feature **[+Presiding]** remains stable, but this overlooks the internal logic of how that authority is exercised.

**Rebuttal:**
Team Refuse claims the role is always "presiding over a formal assembly," but Corpus 2 contradicts this: *"The Dow Jones industrial average jumped 28 points in three days on news of the chairman’s testimony."* In the OLD corpus, the chairman’s power was explicitly tied to the act of presiding over a meeting or committee (e.g., "every committee having a right to elect its own chairman, who presides over it"). In the NEW corpus, the "chairman" functions as a metonym for the corporation itself or its executive power, detached from the act of presiding over an assembly. When the market moves on the "chairman's testimony," the word has evolved from a **[+Presiding]** (procedural) role to a **[+Executive]** (authoritative/corporate) role.

**Reinforcement of Feature-Level Change:**
The shift is not merely a domain change; it is an abstraction of the semantic features. 
1. **From Procedural to Executive:** In Corpus 1, the chairman is a facilitator of "deliberative assembly" and "preliminary steps." In Corpus 2, the chairman is an executive figure ("tough-guy chairman Robert Crandall") whose primary feature is administrative control over capital and policy, not the management of a meeting’s proceedings.
2. **Loss of Boundedness:** The OLD corpus defines the chairman by the committee they preside over ("chairman of the committee"). The NEW corpus frequently uses "Chairman" as a standalone title or static office, stripping away the requirement for a specific, ongoing deliberative group. The role has moved from a **[+Functional]** position (what one does during a meeting) to a **[+Status]** position (who one is in a hierarchy).

**Change Type:** Semantic Bleaching / Specialization.
**Causal Driver:** Corporate institutionalization of the role, replacing parliamentary deliberation with executive management.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the NEW sentence: *"The Dow Jones industrial average jumped 28 points in three days on news of the chairman’s testimony."* They argue this reflects a shift from a **[+Presiding]** procedural role to a **[+Executive]** corporate role.

However, this is not a semantic shift but a manifestation of the core feature **[+Representational Authority]**. In both corpora, the "chairman" serves as the singular voice or primary representative authorized to speak for a collective body. The "chairman" does not act as an individual, but as the embodiment of the organization’s formal position.

This feature is clearly licensed in the OLD corpus: 
> *"The Judiciary branch of the Constitution was submitted directly to his revision, as chairman of the committee, to whom that subject was given in trust; and on all the other important questions... his voice was heard with that respect, which belonged equally to his station..."* 

Here, the chairman is already an authoritative figure whose "voice" carries the weight of the collective assembly. The fact that a modern chairman’s testimony moves markets is merely a change in the *nature* of the body being represented (a corporation rather than a committee), not a change in the lexical sense of the role itself. 

Regarding the NEW sentences where "Chairman" appears as a standalone title or with a department (e.g., "chairman of the pathology, anatomy"), this is not a new lexical sense but standard **polysemy via ellipsis**. Just as the OLD corpus identifies the chairman by the committee they lead, the NEW corpus uses the title to refer to the head of a permanent organizational unit. The underlying semantic structure remains identical: the individual who occupies the highest position of authority within a group and is tasked with representing its interests. The "tough-guy" descriptor in the NEW corpus is a stylistic modification, not a redefinition of the term’s foundational meaning.

### Rebuttal - Round 2

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that modern instances like *"Emanuel Rubin, chairman of the pathology, anatomy"* represent simple "polysemy via ellipsis." This ignores the fundamental shift in the semantic feature of **[+Procedural Accountability]**.

In the OLD corpus, the chairman is defined by their relationship to a *deliberative process*—they are a temporary facilitator among peers. This is explicitly codified in the OLD corpus: *"every committee having a right to elect its own chairman, who presides over it, and makes the report of its proceedings to the assembly."* Here, the chairman’s authority is contingent, procedural, and derivative of the committee’s internal vote.

In contrast, the NEW corpus features like *"Chairman: John A. Meyers"* and *"Vice Chairman: Gerald M. Levin"* strip away the requirement of a deliberative assembly entirely. The role has shifted from **[+Facilitator of Deliberation]** to **[+Permanent Institutional Hierarchy]**. The "pathology/anatomy" example cited by Team Refuse does not merely elide a committee name; it redefines the chairman as the permanent head of a specialized *administrative department*. 

This is a genuine semantic shift: the feature **[+Procedural Accountability]** (governed by the will of the group) has been replaced by **[+Hierarchical Command]** (governed by organizational rank). The "tough-guy" chairman, Robert Crandall, does not report to a committee; he dictates corporate policy. The "chairman" has evolved from a servant of the meeting to the sovereign of the entity.

**Change Type:** Semantic Specialization and Feature Shift.
**Causal Driver:** Institutionalization of the role, shifting from temporary procedural facilitation to permanent administrative authority.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is their claim that the modern usage of "chairman" has shifted from **[+Procedural Accountability]** (a facilitator of a deliberative assembly) to **[+Hierarchical Command]** (a permanent executive rank), citing examples like "Chairman: John A. Meyers" as proof of this institutionalization.

However, this argument fails because the core semantic feature—**[+Presiding Authority]**—remains the constant bridge between the OLD and NEW usage. The "chairman" in both corpora functions as the primary individual responsible for the oversight and representation of a specific entity’s proceedings or structural interests.

The OLD corpus already licenses this feature of permanent or non-deliberative oversight. Consider this OLD sentence: *"The Judiciary branch of the Constitution was submitted directly to his revision, as chairman of the committee, to whom that subject was given in trust."* Here, the chairman is not merely a transient facilitator of a meeting; he is an individual entrusted with the specific, authoritative task of "revision" and oversight of a branch of government. This confirms that the role was already understood as a position of **[+Presiding Authority]** over a specific domain, rather than just a temporary moderator of a debate.

Regarding Team Support’s citation of *"Emanuel Rubin, chairman of the pathology, anatomy,"* this is not a lexical shift to a new sense of "sovereign," but a clear instance of polysemy via ellipsis. The institutional context (the department) is elided because the shared semantic feature of **[+Presiding Authority]** remains sufficient to convey the subject's role as the head of that specific administrative body. Just as the OLD corpus used "chairman" to denote the head of a "committee," the NEW corpus uses it to denote the head of a "department" or "corporation." The underlying function—being the person in charge of a designated group's activities—is identical. No "feature shift" has occurred; the term simply continues to denote the primary presiding officer within varying organizational structures.

### Rebuttal - Round 3

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that the core semantic feature **[+Presiding Authority]** remains constant and unchanged across both corpora. They argue that the role of "chairman" is a stable, singular function of presiding over a group, whether it be a committee in 1840 or a corporation in 1990.

This is refuted by the NEW corpus sentence: *"Under tough-guy chairman Robert Crandall, American Airlines is the leader in profits and popularity."* 

In the OLD corpus, the chairman is defined by **[+Procedural Facilitation]**—the duty to manage a deliberative process, adhere to rules, and give others the floor (e.g., *"it is against the rule for me to do so until you are all done"*). In contrast, the NEW corpus sentence demonstrates a shift to **[+Executive Command]**. Robert Crandall is not described as presiding over a meeting or facilitating a collective debate; he is characterized as a "tough-guy" leader of a corporate entity, where his authority is defined by profitability and market performance rather than parliamentary procedure. 

The semantic feature has shifted from **[+Moderator of Assembly]** (where authority is derived from the consensus of the group) to **[+Corporate Executive]** (where authority is derived from the hierarchical control of an organization). The "chairman" has evolved from a servant of the assembly to the commander of the enterprise.

**Change Type:** Semantic Shift (Specialization/Extension of Domain)
**Causal Driver:** Institutionalization of Corporate Hierarchy

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence for a semantic shift is the sentence: *"Under tough-guy chairman Robert Crandall, American Airlines is the leader in profits and popularity."* They argue this represents a transition from a moderator of parliamentary assembly to an executive commander of a corporation.

However, the core semantic feature linking these usages is **[+Positional Oversight of a Deliberative Body]**. In both the 1840s and the 1990s, the "chairman" functions as the primary representative and strategic director of a formal organization—whether that organization is a committee, a board, or a corporation. The "tough-guy" characterization of Robert Crandall does not denote a new lexical sense of "chairman," but rather an instance of **situational polysemy** where the *style* of leadership is colored by the corporate context, while the *semantic role* (holding the highest presiding rank within a structured entity) remains identical.

The OLD corpus explicitly licenses this feature of oversight and strategic control, independent of mere "procedural facilitation." Consider this sentence from the OLD corpus: *"The Judiciary branch of the Constitution was submitted directly to his revision, as chairman of the committee... his voice was heard with that respect, which belonged equally to his station in the country."* 

This OLD sentence demonstrates that the chairman was already recognized as holding a "station" of significant influence and "revisionary" power, where his authority was tied to his status and the importance of the interests he oversaw. The transition from a committee chair in the 1840s to a corporate chair in the 1990s is a shift in the *domain of application*, not the *lexical sense*. Both roles function as the locus of authority within a group hierarchy, proving that the word has not undergone genuine diachronic semantic change.

### Rebuttal - Round closing

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Support maintains that the semantic shift in "chairman" reflects a transition from **[+Moderating Facilitator]** to **[+Executive Controller]**. Our strongest evidence is the emergence of collocations like "tough-guy chairman" and "chairman of the board and chief executive officer," which signify a move from procedural oversight to direct operational command.

Team Refuse argues that "tough-guy chairman" merely applies existing authority to a new domain. However, this ignores the linguistic shift in the agent’s agency. In the sentence *"Under tough-guy chairman Robert Crandall, American Airlines is the leader in profits and popularity,"* the chairman is no longer a passive moderator of a meeting, but the singular driver of corporate performance. The shift is not merely contextual; it is a fundamental transformation of the role from presiding over a collective process to exerting top-down executive power.

**Change Type:** Semantic Broadening/Functional Shift. 
**Causal Driver:** Increased consolidation of corporate power and executive hierarchy.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Refuse maintains that the core semantic feature **[+Presiding Authority]** remains stable. The corpus evidence consistently anchors the role in the governance of a collective body, whether legislative or corporate. In 1840, the chairman functions as the authority presiding over a meeting; in 1990, the chairman remains the authority presiding over the corporate entity. The fundamental function of directing an organized group persists across both periods.

Regarding Team Support’s argument, the sentence *"Under tough-guy chairman Robert Crandall, American Airlines is the leader in profits and popularity"* does not represent a shift to "Executive Command." Instead, it illustrates a specific application of the existing **[+Presiding Authority]** feature within the domain of modern industry. Just as the 1840 chairman exerted authority to ensure the assembly adhered to its rules, the "tough-guy" chairman exerts authority to ensure the corporation adheres to its objectives. The setting has expanded, but the semantic function—the exercise of presiding authority over an organized body—remains constant. The shift is merely contextual, not categorical.

### ⚖️ LLM Judge

The debate centers on whether the shift from a committee-based 'presiding officer' (1810-1860) to a corporate 'executive head' (1960-2010) constitutes a semantic change. Team Support argues that the loss of the [+committee-bound] feature and the gain of [+executive/hierarchical] features represent a fundamental shift. However, Team Refuse successfully demonstrates that the core semantic feature—[+presiding authority over a formal body]—remains constant. The OLD corpus evidence, such as the chairman of a committee having 'revisionary' power and being heard with 'respect' due to his 'station,' indicates that the role was already associated with authoritative oversight rather than mere procedural facilitation. The shift from committee to corporation is a change in the domain of application (referent class), which does not constitute a diachronic semantic change under the provided rules. The term continues to denote the primary presiding officer of a structured group, and the 'tough-guy' corporate examples are instances of situational polysemy rather than a redefinition of the lexical sense.

## Target Word: `risk_nn`

**Verdict**: CHANGE DETECTED  
**Debate Mode**: multi | **Rounds**: 3 | **Time**: 

### Opening Statements

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

### 1. OLD dominant sense
The dominant sense of "risk" in the OLD corpus is **a specific, imminent, and often voluntary exposure to physical peril or negative consequence resulting from a deliberate action.**
*   **Core semantic features:** [+Volitional] (the agent chooses to act despite the risk), [+Immediate/Concrete] (the danger is situational and localized).

### 2. Evidence for change
The NEW corpus introduces a sense of "risk" as a **probabilistic assessment or statistical likelihood of future harm**, often detached from immediate volitional action.

*   **Sentence 1:** "Nevertheless , insufficient investment in updated distribution and treatment systems as well as inadequate efforts to protect surface and groundwater sources continue to pose a public health risk."
    *   *Analysis:* Here, "risk" refers to a systemic, long-term condition of danger rather than a specific, immediate act of agency. It shifts from a personal hazard one "runs" to a latent property of an environment.
*   **Sentence 7:** "If you 've already had a heart attack , then you 're at high risk of having another."
    *   *Analysis:* This denotes a medical probability or susceptibility. The subject is not performing an action that invites danger; rather, they possess a clinical profile characterized by a statistical likelihood of a future event. This lacks the [+Volitional] feature present in the OLD corpus.
*   **Sentence 4:** "These experts concluded that current BPA exposures appear to pose little risk to people."
    *   *Analysis:* This frames "risk" as an object of scientific inquiry and quantification. It is no longer an immediate threat one encounters in a physical environment but a measured variable.

### 3. Best counterevidence
*   **Sentence 9 (NEW):** "At the risk of being called a sycophant , I thought it was one hell of a read."
    *   *Explanation:* This usage perfectly mirrors the OLD sense of "risk" as a conscious decision to face a social negative consequence for a specific action. It does not undermine the claim because the development of a technical/probabilistic sense does not necessitate the total disappearance of the original volitional sense.

### 4. Why this is not topic/register/polysemy
This is not merely a change in topic (moving from physical danger to medical/systemic danger) because the underlying logic of the noun has evolved. In the OLD corpus, "risk" is an event-based noun related to agency. In the NEW corpus, it frequently functions as a scalar variable (e.g., "high risk," "little risk") used to describe states of being or systemic conditions. The shift from "taking/running a risk" (active) to "being at risk" or "posing a risk" (stative/probabilistic) represents a fundamental change in the noun's semantic role and selectional restrictions.

### 5. Change Type & Causal Driver
*   **Change Type:** Generalization (The sense has expanded from specific, localized physical perils to abstract, statistical probabilities of harm).
*   **Causal Driver:** Linguistic Drift (The gradual integration of probabilistic reasoning and scientific quantification into everyday language).

### 6. Confidence: 85/100

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

### Arg_stable: Analysis of "risk"

#### 1. OLD core sense
The OLD corpus (1810–1860) defines "risk" primarily as: **The exposure to a potential negative event or consequence resulting from a specific action, choice, or condition.**
*   **Key features:**
    *   [+Action-contingent]: The risk is tied to a specific choice or movement (e.g., "move one single step without risk").
    *   [+Agency/Volition]: The risk is often incurred by the subject’s own decision-making (e.g., "carry out his own fanciful... notions at any risk").
    *   [+Harmful-outcome]: The potential negative outcome is tangible and detrimental (e.g., "cooked and eaten," "death," "destruction," "rude").

#### 2. NEW uses consistent with that sense
*   **"At the risk of being called a sycophant, I thought it was one hell of a read."**
    *   **Shared feature:** [+Action-contingent]. The potential negative outcome (being called a sycophant) is contingent upon the speaker’s choice to express their opinion.
    *   **OLD license:** "Arthur could not bring himself to listen, and yet, unless he moved away, at the risk of being thought rude..."
*   **"He... runs no risk of antagonizing the audience by preempting its regular fare."**
    *   **Shared feature:** [+Harmful-outcome] (antagonizing). The outcome is a negative social consequence of an action.
    *   **OLD license:** "I fear our sailor friend runs risk of the archiepiscopacy."

#### 3. Strongest evidence for change
*   **"Nevertheless, insufficient investment in... treatment systems... continue to pose a public health risk."**
*   **"If you 've already had a heart attack, then you 're at high risk of having another."**

These sentences present the strongest counterevidence because they shift the focus from a *volitional action* (the subject choosing to risk something) to a *state of being* or *statistical probability* (the subject being "at risk" due to external/biological factors). In these cases, the risk is not a "wager" made by the subject, but a passive vulnerability.

#### 4. Why they are still compatible with the OLD sense
The core feature of **[+Exposure to negative outcome]** remains intact. In the OLD corpus, "risk" already encompassed passive exposure to external threats, such as the emigrants who were "at the risk of her life" or the sailor who "runs risk of the archiepiscopacy." The NEW usage represents a **referent shift**—moving from immediate, localized dangers (being eaten, being thought rude) to systemic or probabilistic dangers (public health, coronary disease)—rather than a **sense shift**. The underlying semantic structure remains: *Entity X is in a state of potential exposure to Negative Outcome Y*.

#### 5. Why the difference is polysemy/register, not diachronic change
The "statistical" use of risk is a register shift into clinical and administrative discourse. In the OLD corpus, the "risk" was often a discrete, observable event. In the NEW corpus, the "risk" is a calculated probability. However, this is an extension of the same cognitive framework: the evaluation of potential loss. Since the OLD corpus already contained examples where the risk was an external threat independent of the subject's immediate control (e.g., the "frosty breeze" or the "Auto-da-Fe"), the transition to modern usage is an expansion of the *domain* of application, not a fundamental reconfiguration of the word’s semantic core.

#### 6. Confidence: 85/100

### Rebuttal - Round 1

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is the assertion that the "statistical" usage of *risk* in the NEW corpus is merely an extension of the OLD sense of "passive exposure to external threats." They argue that being "at the risk of her life" (OLD) and "at high risk of having another [heart attack]" (NEW) are semantically identical because both involve an entity exposed to a negative outcome.

This collapses the crucial distinction between **contingent hazard** and **probabilistic state**. In the OLD corpus, "risk" is an event-based phenomenon tied to an immediate, identifiable action or environmental condition. In the NEW corpus, "risk" has been reified into an inherent, measurable property of an individual or system.

The contradiction is best illustrated by the NEW corpus sentence: **"These experts concluded that current BPA exposures appear to pose little risk to people."**

In the OLD corpus, *risk* is an active, binary calculation: one either acts and incurs a specific danger (e.g., "move one single step without risk"), or one avoids the action to avoid the danger. In the NEW sentence, "risk" is no longer an action-contingent wager; it is a **quantifiable, latent attribute**—a "public health risk" that exists even in the absence of a specific, discrete decision by the human subject. The OLD corpus lacks this feature of *risk* as an abstract, statistical variable independent of human agency. 

The shift is not merely a change in domain (register); it is a shift from **[+Volitional/Action-Contingent]** to **[+Inherent/Statistical-Probability]**. The word has migrated from describing a *consequence of a choice* to describing a *condition of a population*.

**Change Type:** Semantic Narrowing/Reconceptualization (from "hazard of action" to "probability of state").
**Causal Driver:** Professionalization and the rise of actuarial/clinical discourse, which transformed "risk" from a moral or physical hazard into a technical, measurable attribute.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the NEW sentence: **"These experts concluded that current BPA exposures appear to pose little risk to people."** They argue this represents a transition from "action-contingent hazard" to "statistical probability."

However, this argument fails because it ignores the core semantic feature of **"susceptibility to an adverse outcome,"** which remains the governing constant in both corpora. The "statistical" usage in the NEW corpus is not a new lexical sense, but rather a specialized application of the inherent, latent susceptibility already present in the OLD corpus.

The OLD corpus explicitly licenses this feature of latent, non-volitional susceptibility in the following sentence:
> **"But the more general result , is , a gradual undermining of all parts of the human frame ; thus imperceptibly shortening life , by so weakening the constitution , that it is ready to yield , at every point , to any uncommon risk or exposure."**

In this OLD example, "risk" is not a discrete action taken by the subject, nor is it a binary choice. It is a condition of the "human frame"—a state of weakness that renders the subject susceptible to an outcome. The subject is not "choosing" to be at risk; they are in a state of compromised health that makes them vulnerable to external threats. 

When the NEW corpus describes "public health risk" or "risk of heart attack," it is simply mapping this established concept of **vulnerability** onto a population or medical condition. Because the OLD corpus already defined "risk" as a state of susceptibility that can be "ready to yield" to external factors, the NEW usage is merely a scaling of this existing semantic feature from the individual to the clinical. This is a clear case of polysemy—where a single sense ("vulnerability to harm") is applied across different registers—rather than a diachronic shift in the meaning of the word itself.

### Rebuttal - Round 2

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that the transition to statistical probability is merely a "scaling" of the "vulnerability" found in the OLD corpus. They suggest that because the 19th-century "human frame" was vulnerable, the modern "public health risk" is semantically identical.

This is a category error. Team Refuse ignores the shift from **agentive, event-based hazard** to **probabilistic, latent condition**. In the OLD corpus, "risk" requires an active, discernible point of conflict or decision-making. Contradicting their claim of continuity, consider this OLD sentence:

> **"If it be each man 's prerogative to carry out his own fanciful or conscientious notions at any risk , law and order must go to the wall , of course..."**

Here, "risk" is a deliberate, agentive threshold—a price one pays for an action. The subject *chooses* the risk. In contrast, the NEW corpus usage—**"These experts concluded that current BPA exposures appear to pose little risk to people"**—completely strips away the agent's prerogative. The "risk" is no longer a chosen action or a specific event the subject encounters; it is a calculated, externalized value assigned to a population by an observer.

The semantic feature has fundamentally shifted from **"a hazard incurred by an actor"** to **"a quantified likelihood of a future state."** In the OLD corpus, risk is something you *take* or *face* in a specific moment of agency; in the NEW, it is something you *have* or *pose* as a result of probabilistic modeling. This represents a functional shift from a behavioral concept to a mathematical one, driven by the professionalization of risk management and clinical assessment.

**Change Type:** Semantic Shift (from Agentive Action to Probabilistic State).
**Causal Driver:** The institutionalization and quantification of hazard assessment in public policy and medicine.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the contrast between the agentive choice in the OLD corpus sentence, *"If it be each man's prerogative to carry out his own fanciful or conscientious notions at any risk,"* and the probabilistic assessment in the NEW corpus sentence, *"These experts concluded that current BPA exposures appear to pose little risk to people."*

They argue this represents a shift from "agentive action" to "probabilistic state." However, this fails to recognize that both usages are anchored by the shared semantic feature of **exposure to potential harm.**

The "probabilistic" NEW usage is not a new lexical sense, but rather a contextual application of the same foundational concept of **vulnerability to a negative outcome.** The OLD corpus already licenses this feature, independent of human agency, in the following sentence:

> *"But the more general result, is, a gradual undermining of all parts of the human frame; thus imperceptibly shortening life, by so weakening the constitution, that it is ready to yield, at every point, to any uncommon **risk** or exposure."*

In this OLD example, the "risk" is not a choice made by the subject; it is an external condition of vulnerability that the "human frame" faces. The subject is not "taking" a risk; they are in a state of susceptibility where a negative outcome (yielding to exposure) is possible. 

The NEW corpus usage involving BPA or health statistics is simply the institutional application of this exact same logic: identifying a state of susceptibility where a negative outcome is possible. Whether the risk is "run" by an agent or "posed" by a chemical, the core semantic invariant remains the objective possibility of an undesirable event. This is not a change in the word's meaning, but a shift in the *domain* of application—from individual physical vulnerability to clinical and statistical vulnerability—which constitutes standard, stable polysemy within the same lexical sense.

### Rebuttal - Round 3

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that the "probabilistic" usage in the NEW corpus is merely an "institutional application" of the same logic found in the OLD corpus, asserting that both represent a stable, invariant state of "vulnerability to a negative outcome."

This is contradicted by the OLD corpus sentence: *"The child, strange wrestler at war with all these risks, had succeeded in reaching the bottom of the descent."* Here, "risk" is clearly defined by **active, immediate, and localized confrontation**. The "risks" are physical obstacles—the descent, the terrain—that the child is actively "wrestling" with. In the OLD corpus, "risk" is a tangible, situational, and often adversarial encounter.

In contrast, the NEW corpus usage—*"These experts concluded that current BPA exposures appear to pose little risk to people"*—departs from this feature of active confrontation. The "risk" here is not an encounter or an obstacle to be navigated; it is a **quantified, abstract probability** derived from longitudinal clinical observation. 

The semantic feature has shifted from **"active engagement with a situational peril"** (OLD) to **"statistical likelihood of a latent condition"** (NEW). While Refuse claims these are the same, the OLD corpus requires an agent to *interact* with the risk (the child wrestlers, the travellers retracing steps, the sailors running risk). The NEW corpus detaches the "risk" from any immediate interaction, turning it into an object of calculation. This is a fundamental change in the feature-level definition of the word: from a *situational event* to a *probabilistic parameter*.

**Change Type:** Semantic Narrowing (from general situational peril to technical/statistical probability) and Abstractification.
**Causal Driver:** Professionalization and the rise of the "risk-management" discourse in scientific/public health institutions.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the contrast between the "active, immediate, and localized confrontation" in the sentence, *"The child, strange wrestler at war with all these risks, had succeeded in reaching the bottom of the descent,"* and the "quantified, abstract probability" found in, *"These experts concluded that current BPA exposures appear to pose little risk to people."*

However, this argument overlooks the persistent semantic feature that bridges both eras: **"exposure to a potential negative outcome contingent upon a specific condition."** 

Team Support claims that the OLD corpus requires an agent to *interact* with a physical obstacle, but the OLD corpus itself contains examples where risk is not an immediate, tactile struggle, but a conditional state of vulnerability. Consider this OLD sentence: *"But the more general result, is, a gradual undermining of all parts of the human frame; thus imperceptibly shortening life, by so weakening the constitution, that it is ready to yield, at every point, to any uncommon risk or exposure."*

In this OLD example, the "risk" is not a visible terrain or a wrestling match; it is a latent vulnerability of the "human frame" to an "uncommon" event. This is identical to the "probabilistic" usage in the NEW corpus. Both the 1840s "weakening constitution" and the 2000s "BPA exposure" describe a state of susceptibility where the subject is not currently "wrestling" with an enemy but is positioned in a state where a negative outcome *might* occur. 

The shift Team Support identifies—from "situational event" to "statistical parameter"—is not a change in the lexical sense of "risk," but a shift in the *domain of application* (from individual physical peril to public health assessment). This is a clear case of **polysemy via extension**, not semantic change. The core feature of "conditional potential for harm" remains the invariant foundation of the word in both corpora; the "statistical" nature of the NEW corpus is merely the modern institutional method for measuring that same pre-existing concept of vulnerability.

### Rebuttal - Round closing

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Support maintains that "risk" has undergone a semantic shift from situational confrontation to abstract, probabilistic calculation. Our strongest evidence lies in the transition from physical hazards, such as the child who *"had succeeded in reaching the bottom of the descent"* while battling risks, to modern, data-driven assessments like: *"These experts concluded that current BPA exposures appear to pose little risk to people."*

Team Refuse argues that risk remains a tethered condition of human exposure. However, this ignores the shift in agency: in Corpus 1, risk is a physical obstacle to be physically navigated, whereas in Corpus 2, risk is a quantified output derived from external expert analysis. While the subject is "exposed" in both, the modern usage prioritizes statistical modeling over immediate, situational danger, as evidenced by the shift from the child's direct physical struggle to the expert's indirect, clinical conclusion. The core semantic feature has transitioned from a tangible encounter to a calculated probability.

**Change Type:** Semantic Shift (Situational to Abstract). 
**Causal Driver:** Professionalization of Risk Assessment.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Refuse maintains that "risk" remains stable as a state of vulnerability to negative outcomes, regardless of the agent’s proximity. Our strongest evidence lies in the persistent requirement for human navigation: whether it is the child who *"had succeeded in reaching the bottom of the descent"* while battling risks, or modern subjects navigated by experts, the noun consistently denotes a threshold of potential harm that dictates human behavior.

Team Support argues that "risk" has shifted from situational confrontation to abstract calculation. This ignores that even in the modern corpus, risk remains a tethered condition of human exposure, as seen in: *"These experts concluded that current BPA exposures appear to pose little risk to people."* By requiring an agent (the people) to be exposed to a potential negative outcome, the word functions as a constant indicator of vulnerability. The "wrestling" of the child and the "exposure" of the modern subject both function as mechanisms of assessment for an anticipated peril. Whether physical or clinical, the core semantic feature—a future-oriented vulnerability—remains invariant across both corpora.

### ⚖️ LLM Judge

The debate demonstrates a fundamental shift in the semantic role of 'risk'. In the OLD corpus (1810-1860), 'risk' is consistently tied to agentive, situational, and often adversarial encounters (e.g., the child 'wrestling' with risks, or an agent choosing to act 'at any risk'). The semantic core is [+Volitional] and [+Situational]. In the NEW corpus (1960-2010), 'risk' has been reified into a scalar, probabilistic variable used in clinical and systemic contexts (e.g., 'public health risk', 'high risk of heart attack'). While Team Refuse argues that 'vulnerability' is a shared invariant, they fail to account for the loss of the agentive/situational requirement. The transition from 'risk' as an event-based hazard one 'runs' or 'takes' to a latent, quantified property one 'poses' or 'is at' represents a genuine semantic generalization, driven by the professionalization of risk management and the rise of actuarial discourse in the 20th century.
