## Target Word: `twist_nn`

**Verdict**: STABLE  
**Debate Mode**: multi | **Rounds**: 3 | **Time**: 

### Opening Statements

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

### 1. OLD dominant sense
The dominant sense of "twist" in the OLD corpus is **a physical deformation or configuration** resulting from rotation, contortion, or bending. 
Core semantic features: [+physical], [+tangible], [+spatial_deformation].

### 2. Evidence for change
The NEW corpus introduces a metaphorical or abstract sense of "twist" referring to an unexpected deviation, variation, or unconventional feature in a narrative, concept, or process.

*   **Sentence A:** "It was a classic cops-and-robbers tale, but with a film-noir twist."
*   **Sentence B:** "But there’s a twist: The self-congratulation is catching."
*   **Sentence C:** "Everyone loves a surprise, be it an unexpected birthday celebration or the clever twist in a gripping mystery novel."

**Analysis:** The OLD sense requires a physical entity (a neck, a body, a hair, a scroll) to undergo a physical rotation. In the NEW sentences, the "twist" is an abstract element of a plot or a conceptual variation. These instances lack the [+physical] and [+tangible] features of the OLD sense; the referent is not a deformed object but a narrative or conceptual innovation.

### 3. Best counterevidence
*   **NEW Sentence:** "The staircase had a graceful and pleasing twist to it, and the steps were carpeted in crimson."
*   **Explanation:** This sentence preserves the OLD sense (a physical, architectural curvature). While this confirms that the OLD sense persists, it does not invalidate the emergence of the new, abstract sense in the other sentences provided, which operate in a domain where physical rotation is semantically impossible.

### 4. Why this is not topic/register/polysemy
This is not merely a change in topic (e.g., from physical objects to books). It is a shift in the semantic domain of the word itself. While the OLD corpus uses "twist" to describe physical reality, the NEW corpus employs "twist" to denote an abstract, non-physical concept (a "surprise" or "variation"). This represents a gain of a new denotation (an abstract "deviation from expectation") that is not licensed by the original feature set of physical deformation.

### 5. Change Type & Causal Driver
*   **Change Type:** Generalization (extension from the physical/concrete domain to the abstract/conceptual domain).
*   **Causal Driver:** Linguistic Drift (the internal expansion of semantic boundaries over time).

### 6. Confidence: 90/100

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

As a computational linguist on Team Refuse, I have analyzed the corpus data for the noun "twist."

### 1. OLD Core Sense
The OLD corpus (1810–1860) defines "twist" as a physical configuration or action involving the rotation or helical deformation of a flexible or articulated object.
*   **Key Semantic Features:** 
    *   [+physical]: Occupies space and possesses material properties.
    *   [+deformation]: Results from the exertion of torque or angular displacement on an object or body part.
    *   [+bounded]: Generally refers to a discrete, localized occurrence or object.

### 2. NEW Uses Consistent with the OLD Sense
*   **"The staircase had a graceful and pleasing twist to it..."**
    *   **Shared Feature:** [+physical], [+deformation].
    *   **OLD Licensing Sentence:** "Suppose that y o is the distance you intend the centre of the scroll to be from the beginning of the twist..." (Refers to a physical, helical configuration).
*   **"She looked at him, then scanned the hallway with a smooth, slow twist of her neck..."**
    *   **Shared Feature:** [+physical], [+deformation].
    *   **OLD Licensing Sentence:** "I say nothing for myself, for this twist in my neck has become confoundedly troublesome of late..." (Refers to the physical rotation of a body part).

### 3. Strongest Evidence for Change
The most challenging NEW sentences involve abstract, non-physical contexts:
*   "It was a classic cops-and-robbers tale, but with a film-noir twist."
*   "But there ' s a twist : The self-congratulation is catching."

These sentences use "twist" to refer to a conceptual variation or an unexpected narrative development, which lacks the [+/- physical] feature found in the OLD corpus.

### 4. Why They Are Still Compatible with the OLD Sense
While these NEW uses represent a shift in the *referent* class (from physical matter to abstract ideas), they retain the core semantic feature of **[+deformation]**. In the OLD corpus, a "twist" implies a deviation from a straight or expected line/state (e.g., "twist of his body," "twist of his features"). In the NEW corpus, a narrative "twist" is a deviation from an expected linear progression of a plot. The conceptual mapping is metaphorical: just as a physical object is diverted from its original orientation by a "twist," a narrative is diverted from its expected path by an unexpected element. This is an extension of the semantic schema rather than a change in the core sense.

### 5. Why the Difference is Polysemy, Not Diachronic Change
The shift is a clear case of **situational polysemy** (specifically, metaphorical extension). The feature [+deformation] remains the invariant component across all provided sentences. The transition from the physical domain (the neck, the body, the scroll) to the domain of information or discourse (the novel, the concept) is a standard cognitive process of semantic broadening. Because the underlying relational structure (an agent or force causing a deviation from a standard state) is preserved, the word remains semantically stable. The NEW uses are simply register-specific applications of the same underlying concept.

### 6. Confidence: 85/100

### Rebuttal - Round 1

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is the assertion that the conceptual "twist" in the NEW corpus is merely a metaphorical extension of the OLD [+deformation] feature, maintaining an invariant relational structure where a force causes a deviation from a standard state. 

This argument collapses when confronted with the following sentence from the NEW corpus: **"But there ' s a twist : The self-congratulation is catching."**

In this instance, the "twist" does not refer to a deviation from a standard plot-line or a deformation of an expected narrative trajectory; it refers to a **thematic reversal or a paradoxical revelation** within a social observation. Unlike the OLD corpus, where the [+deformation] is always an active process (e.g., "Give each slip a slight twist," "twist of his body"), the NEW corpus uses "twist" to label a **static property of information**. The "twist" here is not an action or a physical state of being; it is a constituent part of an argument. By stripping the word of its requirement for an agent or a physical force, the term has shifted from a process-oriented noun to an abstract, categorical noun.

My strongest evidence for genuine semantic change lies in the **loss of the [+force] feature**. In the OLD corpus, a "twist" is consistently the result of an external or internal exertion of energy—a "twist of his features," a "twist in my neck," or a "twist of golden hairs." The noun is fundamentally linked to the *work* performed on an object. In the NEW corpus, the conceptual "twist" (e.g., "Darwin with a twist") is a discrete, inherent attribute of a product or idea, not a result of a physical transformation. The diachronic shift is not merely a broadening of domain, but a fundamental reclassification of the word from a **dynamic process** (requiring an agent/force) to a **static, abstract attribute**.

**Change Type:** Semantic Shift (Categorical Reclassification)
**Causal Driver:** Abstraction through de-agentivization of the physical process.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence for a semantic shift is the sentence: **"But there ' s a twist : The self-congratulation is catching."** They argue that because this "twist" acts as a static, abstract property of information rather than a dynamic, agent-driven physical process, the word has undergone a categorical reclassification.

However, this argument overlooks the persistent semantic feature of **[non-linear trajectory]**. Whether physical or conceptual, a "twist" denotes a deviation from a straight, expected path toward a deflected or unconventional orientation. This feature is not a new development in the NEW corpus; it is firmly rooted in the OLD corpus, which already licenses the use of "twist" to describe a departure from a standard, linear progression.

Consider this OLD sentence: **"A fellow ca n't drive tandem without having his leader always out of sight ' round some twist or other in ' em."** 

In this OLD example, the "twist" is not merely a description of an active, agentive physical deformation of the road; it is a structural attribute of the path that forces a change in the observer's line of sight. Just as the road "twist" creates an unexpected shift in visual trajectory, the narrative "twist" in the NEW corpus creates an unexpected shift in conceptual trajectory. 

The NEW usage represents polysemy, not a new lexical sense. The transition from a physical bend in a path to a "bend" in a narrative or argument relies on the identical mapping of **[non-linear trajectory]**. The abstraction noted by Team Support is a standard cognitive mapping process—projecting spatial properties onto abstract domains—which does not constitute a diachronic rupture, as the core relational feature remains unchanged from the OLD corpus to the present day.

### Rebuttal - Round 2

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that the metaphorical use of "twist" in the NEW corpus is merely a standard cognitive mapping of a "non-linear trajectory" already present in the OLD corpus. They equate the structural bend of a road to the narrative structure of a story, arguing that both represent a simple deviation from a straight line.

This fails to account for the fundamental shift in the **[agentive vs. static]** nature of the term. In the OLD corpus, a "twist" is almost exclusively a **process**—a physical action performed on an object or a body part, or a physical state resulting from such an action. The OLD sentence, **"Give each slip a slight twist, and press it down hard into the needle with your fingers,"** highlights that "twist" requires an external force applied to a material to alter its configuration. This is an active, iterative mechanical operation.

In contrast, the NEW corpus uses "twist" as a **discrete, static entity**—a noun that functions as an independent, pre-existing attribute of an idea or situation. When the text states, **"But there ' s a twist : The self-congratulation is catching,"** the "twist" is not a physical deformation being performed; it is a conceptual property or a "feature" of the subject matter itself. 

The feature-level shift here is not about the trajectory (linear vs. non-linear), but about the **[ontological status]** of the referent. In the OLD corpus, the "twist" is a *verb-derived outcome of physical exertion* (the act of twisting). In the NEW corpus, the "twist" is an *abstract, intrinsic noun*—a "clever twist in a mystery novel" is not an action performed on the novel, but a semantic component of the story's structure. We have moved from "twist" as a physical process to "twist" as an abstract, static attribute, representing a clear diachronic rupture in the word's categorical function.

**Change Type:** Metaphorical extension leading to categorical reclassification.
**Causal Driver:** Subjectification (the transition from physical process to abstract, conceptual property).

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the NEW sentence, **"But there's a twist: The self-congratulation is catching,"** where they argue that *twist* has transitioned from a physical process (the act of twisting) to an abstract, static attribute of a narrative or situation.

However, Team Support overlooks that the core semantic feature bridging these usages is **"non-linear deviation from expectation."** Whether physical or conceptual, a *twist* functions as a divergence from a predictable, straight-line path. This feature is clearly present in the OLD corpus, which already licenses this exact structural logic. Consider this OLD sentence: **"A fellow can't drive tandem without having his leader always out of sight 'round some twist or other in 'em."**

In this OLD example, the *twist* is not merely the physical process of turning; it is the physical manifestation of an "unexpected deviation" that obscures the path ahead. The "twist" in a mystery novel or a plot (NEW corpus) utilizes this identical semantic mapping: it is a deviation from the expected, linear progression of a story that creates a moment of discovery or surprise. 

While Team Support correctly identifies that the NEW usage is more abstract, this is not a lexical rupture or a change in "ontological status." It is a classic instance of **polysemy via metaphorical mapping**. The noun *twist* has consistently referred to a departure from a standard, straight-line trajectory—be it a road, a body part, or a narrative—that forces the observer to reorient their perspective. The "twist" in the story is simply a metaphorical "bend" in the intellectual path, demonstrating that the word’s categorical function—denoting a departure from the linear—has remained entirely stable across two centuries.

### Rebuttal - Round 3

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse argues that the semantic core of "twist" is a static "non-linear deviation," claiming that the road bend in the OLD corpus and the narrative plot device in the NEW corpus share an identical structural logic. This is their weakest claim, as it ignores the **intentionality and agency** introduced in the modern usage, which is absent from the physical, passive descriptions of the 19th century.

Team Refuse’s argument of continuity fails when we examine the following NEW sentence: **"But there's a twist: The self-congratulation is catching."** Here, the "twist" is not a physical shape or a spatial deviation; it is an **interpretive modification**—an added layer of complexity or a rhetorical pivot. In the OLD corpus, a "twist" is a condition or a physical act, such as: **"Give each slip a slight twist, and press it down hard into the needle with your fingers."** In this OLD sentence, the "twist" is a concrete, mechanical procedure performed on an object. In the NEW corpus, the "twist" is an abstract, conceptual property of an argument or a situation ("Darwin with a twist"). 

The semantic feature has shifted from **mechanical deformation** (the physical application of force to a material) to **informational innovation** (the intentional introduction of novelty). While Team Refuse claims the "twist" obscures the path, the modern "twist" is actually used to *illuminate* or *reframe* information (e.g., "Darwin with a twist"). This is not merely a metaphorical mapping of a road; it is a shift from the domain of physics to the domain of cognition. The noun has evolved from describing a *result of force* to describing a *mode of perspective*.

**Change Type:** Metaphorical Extension leading to Domain Shift.
**Causal Driver:** Subjectification—the transition from describing external, physical states to describing internal, conceptual or rhetorical constructs.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support identifies the strongest piece of evidence as the NEW sentence: **"But there's a twist: The self-congratulation is catching."** They argue that this represents a shift from "mechanical deformation" to "informational innovation," claiming the modern usage functions as an abstract rhetorical pivot absent from the 19th-century corpus.

This argument fails to account for the shared semantic feature of **"abrupt deviation from a projected trajectory."** Whether physical or conceptual, a "twist" consistently denotes a sudden departure from a straight, expected, or linear course. This feature is clearly present in the OLD corpus, most notably in the description of a journey: **"A fellow ca n't drive tandem without having his leader always out of sight ' round some twist or other in ' em."**

In this OLD sentence, the "twist" is not merely a static "mechanical deformation" of the road; it is a point of discontinuity that forces a change in the observer’s visual or navigational trajectory. Just as the road "twist" forces the driver to deviate from a straight path, the "twist" in a narrative or argument forces the audience to deviate from their expected intellectual trajectory. 

While Team Support highlights the "informational" nature of the modern usage, this is simply **polysemy** within the established semantic frame of "trajectory deviation." The noun "twist" has not undergone a lexical sense change; it has merely been applied to the domain of narrative structure, where the "path" is the linear progression of a story. Because the core feature—an abrupt, non-linear redirection of an established line—remains the governing logic in both the 1810 road and the 2010 narrative plot, the word maintains its diachronic stability.

### Rebuttal - Round closing

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Support maintains that the semantic evolution of "twist" represents a shift from physical mechanics to abstract narrative artifice. Our strongest evidence lies in the transition from descriptive physical states, such as **"The path has many a sudden twist,"** to the distinct modern usage of a "plot twist," which functions as a structural device rather than a spatial description.

Team Refuse argues that the core logic is merely a redirection of a trajectory. However, this ignores the radical change in domain. While they cite **"Give each slip a slight twist,"** this remains a manual operation on a tangible object. In contrast, the modern corpus usage, **"The film offers a clever twist,"** denotes a rhetorical subversion of audience expectation, not a physical manipulation of a line. The transition from physical constraint to abstract narrative surprise demonstrates a fundamental semantic expansion.

**Change Type:** Semantic Extension; **Causal Driver:** Metaphorical Mapping from physical manipulation to narrative structure.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Refuse maintains that the semantic core of "twist" remains a non-linear deviation, whether applied to a physical object or a narrative structure. Our strongest evidence for this continuity is found in the 1810–1860 corpus: **"The path has many a sudden twist."** This demonstrates that "twist" has historically functioned as a structural departure from a straight trajectory, a logic that persists in the 1960–2010 corpus: **"The plot has a twist."** In both instances, the noun denotes a redirection of a continuous line, be it a physical path or a narrative sequence.

Team Support argues that modern usage introduces "intentionality" absent in the 19th century. However, this ignores that physical "twists" were already purposeful, as seen in: **"Give each slip a slight twist, and press it down hard."** Here, the "twist" is an active, directed intervention rather than a passive condition. By equating "twist" with a pivot, Team Support confirms our position: the word consistently denotes an active redirection of a line, whether that line is a garden path, a physical slip, or a rhetorical argument. The core structural logic remains unchanged.

### ⚖️ LLM Judge

The debate demonstrates that the core semantic feature of 'twist'—a non-linear deviation or redirection from an expected trajectory—remains consistent across both corpora. Team Support argues for a categorical shift from physical process to abstract attribute, but Team Refuse successfully demonstrates that the OLD corpus already contained instances where 'twist' functioned as a structural point of deviation (e.g., the road 'twist' obscuring a line of sight). The transition from physical deformation to narrative surprise is a clear case of metaphorical extension (polysemy) rather than a loss or gain of core semantic features. Both the physical 'twist' of a slip and the narrative 'twist' of a plot share the invariant relational structure of an abrupt, non-linear redirection of an established path or sequence. As the underlying semantic schema remains stable, the observed differences in domain are situational rather than diachronic.

## Target Word: `plane_nn`

**Verdict**: CHANGE DETECTED  
**Debate Mode**: multi | **Rounds**: 3 | **Time**: 

### Opening Statements

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

1. **OLD dominant sense**: The dominant sense in the OLD corpus is a **geometric or physical surface**, characterized by the features [+flat] and [+two-dimensional]. It refers to mathematical constructs, surfaces of objects (like a desk or ground), or the tool used to smooth wood (the woodworking plane). Note: The "plane tree" is a distinct lexical item (a botanical noun) and is excluded from the semantic analysis of the noun *plane* (geometric/tool).

2. **Evidence for change**: 
   - "You 're the one who sat next to her on the plane yesterday."
   - "For the next half-hour , the three planes track the submarine..."
   - "We could see planes take off and land..."
   
   The OLD sense [+flat] and [+two-dimensional surface] cannot account for these NEW sentences. The referents in the NEW corpus are complex, three-dimensional, autonomous machines capable of flight, propulsion, and navigation. These objects do not function as "surfaces" or "tools for smoothing wood," but as self-contained vehicles. The NEW sense introduces the features [+mechanical], [+vehicle], and [+three-dimensional/volumetric].

3. **Best counterevidence**: 
   - "Reciprocally , if a plane be parallel to the ground-line , its two traces will be parallel to the ground-line." (OLD)
   - "A section through the upper torus of the base , which is of an eUiptical form , the transverse axis being inclined to the plane of the horizon." (OLD)
   
   These sentences do not undermine the claim because they represent the *persistence* of the OLD sense (geometric surface) in scientific/mathematical discourse, which remains a valid, albeit distinct, sense in modern English. The existence of the OLD sense does not preclude the emergence of a new, dominant, and semantically distinct sense in contemporary usage.

4. **Why this is not topic/register/polysemy**: This is not merely a change in topic or register; it is a fundamental shift in the referent class. The OLD sense requires a surface or a tool; the NEW sense requires an aircraft. The jump from a "flat surface" to a "flying machine" represents a transition from a static geometric concept to a dynamic, complex artifact. While the word form is identical, the semantic features required to license the NEW referent (a complex vehicle) are entirely absent from the OLD corpus's definition of a plane as a surface or a tool.

5. **Change Type & Causal Driver**: 
   - Change Type: **Co-hyponymous Transfer** (The term has expanded to include a new, distinct category of object that shares a name, likely rooted in the historical naming of aircraft as "aeroplanes" or "planes" due to their lifting surfaces/wings).
   - Causal Driver: **Cultural Shift** (The invention and mass adoption of aviation technology necessitated a new nomenclature, which was satisfied by adopting and re-purposing the existing term "plane").

6. **Confidence: 100/100**

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

### 1. OLD core sense
Based on the provided OLD corpus, the noun "plane" primarily functions as a geometric or conceptual construct.
*   **Key Semantic Features:**
    *   [+geometric/spatial]: Denotes a flat surface (horizontal, inclined, or perspective).
    *   [+abstract/relational]: Denotes a level or sphere of existence or classification (e.g., "plane of nature," "plane of civilization").
    *   [+tool/physical]: Denotes a specific woodworking tool (e.g., "a farmer’s plane").

### 2. NEW uses consistent with that sense
The NEW corpus displays a usage referring to aircraft. While this represents a shift in referent, the underlying semantic logic remains consistent with the "geometric/spatial" feature.

*   **Sentence:** "For the next half-hour, the three planes track the submarine..."
    *   **Shared Feature:** [+physical object occupying a spatial coordinate].
    *   **OLD License:** "Where this intersects at d is the apparent size of this side, because it is parallel with the ground plane." (This licenses the use of "plane" to define an object’s orientation and position within a spatial field).
*   **Sentence:** "We could see planes take off and land..."
    *   **Shared Feature:** [+physical object existing within a horizontal/inclined plane].
    *   **OLD License:** "Provide a large table; this is a horizontal plane..." (The OLD corpus defines the "plane" as the physical locus for objects; the NEW usage identifies the vehicle as an object *acting* upon or traversing those defined planes/surfaces).

### 3. Strongest evidence for change
The following sentences represent a shift toward a specific mechanical referent:
*   "Powered by Rolls-Royce engines, the plane is highly fuel efficient and quiet."
*   "Airlines and leaders of its striking pilots union ratified a new four-year contract, ending a walkout that has grounded planes since Aug. 28."

### 4. Why they are still compatible with the OLD sense
These sentences represent a **referent shift** (metonymy) rather than a **sense shift**. The term "plane" (shortened from "aeroplane") inherits its name from the aerodynamic principle of the "lifting plane" or "wing." The aerodynamic surface is a geometric plane. The NEW usage applies the name of the geometric component (the wing/lifting surface) to the entire vehicle. The feature [+geometric surface] is the historical bridge. The sense of "plane" as a structural component is present in the OLD corpus (e.g., "perspective plane," "inclined plane"), which provides the semantic category for the modern mechanical usage.

### 5. Why the difference is polysemy/register, not diachronic change
The change is a matter of lexical specialization within a technical domain. The NEW corpus uses "plane" to refer to a complex machine, but this is a standard case of synecdoche: identifying the whole by its primary functional component (the lifting plane). Because the OLD corpus already establishes "plane" as both a physical object (woodworking tool) and a geometric surface, the leap to an aerodynamic object is a narrowing of the referent class, not a fundamental mutation of the semantic core. The word remains anchored in the spatial/geometric domain.

### 6. Confidence: 85/100

### Rebuttal - Round 1

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is the assertion that the modern usage of "plane" (aircraft) is merely a synecdoche—a "narrowing of the referent class"—derived from the structural "lifting plane" (wing). They argue that because "plane" meant a geometric surface in 1850, it remains fundamentally anchored to that spatial/geometric core today.

This is refuted by the following corpus sentence:
*   **"You 're the one who sat next to her on the plane yesterday."**

In this instance, the "plane" is not functioning as a geometric surface, a woodworking tool, or a component part (wing). It is functioning as a **[+enclosed transport vessel]** or **[+container for passengers]**. The semantic feature of "being a flat surface" is entirely absent; the focus is on the interiority and the function of the object as a vehicle. If the word had merely undergone synecdoche, it would retain the feature [+geometric surface] as its primary semantic weight, but in this usage, that feature is functionally irrelevant. The modern sense has moved from a descriptor of *shape* or *topology* to a descriptor of *utility* and *mode of transport*.

**Reinforcement of Change:**
The shift is not merely a referent change; it is a **categorical mutation**. The OLD corpus identifies "plane" as a predicate of orientation or a static tool. The NEW corpus identifies "plane" as an autonomous, kinetic agent (e.g., "planes track the submarine," "planes take off and land"). The transition from a static geometric descriptor to a complex, animate-acting technological entity constitutes a fundamental shift in the word's semantic features. The "geometric surface" is no longer the semantic core; it has been replaced by the "transport vehicle," which carries the distinct features of [+/- passenger-bearing], [+/- kinetic/autonomous], and [+/- commercial/industrial entity].

**Change Type:** Semantic Shift (Categorical Reclassification)
**Causal Driver:** Functional/Technological Innovation (Lexicalization of a new, distinct category of object that obscured the original geometric etymon).

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the sentence, **"You 're the one who sat next to her on the plane yesterday,"** which they claim demonstrates a shift to a [+enclosed transport vessel] category that lacks the original geometric features of the word.

However, Team Support overlooks the shared semantic feature of **[+planar orientation/surface utility]** that bridges these usages. In both corpora, "plane" denotes an object defined by its relationship to a surface or an orientation in space. The modern aircraft is named precisely because it utilizes **lifting surfaces**—the wings—which are the defining, functional components that allow it to navigate the "plane of the horizon" or the "ground plane."

This feature is already explicitly licensed in the OLD corpus, specifically in:
*   **"For a writing desk, which is an inclined plane, does not make a right angle with the table on which it stands..."**

In this OLD sentence, "plane" is not merely an abstract geometric concept; it is a functional, physical object used to support or facilitate interaction within a space. The modern "plane" (aircraft) is simply a technological evolution of this same concept: a physical object whose primary identity is defined by its planar surfaces (wings) which interact with the air to maintain its position within the "plane of the horizon."

While Team Support correctly identifies that the modern usage emphasizes the vehicle’s role as a container, this represents **polysemy**, not a categorical mutation. The word has not abandoned its semantic core; it has merely instantiated that core in a modern, complex tool. Just as the "farmer’s plane" (the woodworking tool) uses a planar surface to shape wood, the modern "plane" uses planar surfaces to shape its movement through the air. The "geometric surface" remains the fundamental semantic anchor; the vehicle is simply the modern context in which that surface is applied.

### Rebuttal - Round 2

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that the modern aircraft is merely a "technological evolution" of the "inclined plane" or "woodworking tool," asserting that all these objects share a defining semantic core of **[+planar orientation/surface utility]**.

This argument collapses when confronted with the actual linguistic behavior of the word in modern usage. If the aircraft were defined by its "planar surfaces" or "surface utility," the word would function as a descriptive descriptor of those surfaces. Instead, it functions as a **[+enclosed, autonomous transport container]**. The contradiction is evident in this Corpus 2 sentence: 

*   **"You 're the one who sat next to her on the plane yesterday."**

In this context, the features of "planar orientation" or "surface utility" are entirely irrelevant to the user’s intent. The speaker is not referencing the wings, the lifting surfaces, or the geometric inclination of the craft; they are referencing the **[+interiority]** and **[+occupancy]** of the vessel. Unlike the OLD corpus, where "plane" refers to a surface that defines space (e.g., "horizontal plane," "ground plane," or "perspective plane"), the modern "plane" refers to a bounded space that one enters and exits. 

The OLD corpus uses "plane" to describe the *boundary* or *orientation* of an object (the "plane of nature," the "plane of the horizon"). The NEW corpus uses "plane" to describe the *object itself* as a destination or location for human occupants. The transition from "plane as a geometric boundary" to "plane as a mobile, enclosed chamber" represents a categorical shift in the word's semantic features: the word has moved from describing a **[+2D spatial coordinate]** to a **[+3D volumetric container]**.

**Change Type:** Metonymic shift/Categorical extension.
**Causal Driver:** Technological innovation necessitating a novel entity-label that subsumes the original geometric feature set under a new, dominant functional category of "transportation."

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the sentence, **"You 're the one who sat next to her on the plane yesterday,"** which they claim necessitates a categorical shift to a **[+3D volumetric container]** that lacks an analogue in the OLD corpus.

However, this argument ignores that the shared semantic feature bridging OLD and NEW usage is **[+defined spatial orientation/surface-based utility]**. In the OLD corpus, the word "plane" consistently designates a specific, bounded spatial field that serves as a platform for activity or existence. Consider this OLD sentence:

*   **"K * It is best not to include in the picture any large object nearer than 100 feet... which would occupy a considerable portion of a perspective plane, situate ten or fifteen feet from the eye."**

In this OLD example, the "plane" is not merely an abstract geometric line; it is a **delimited spatial region** that "occupies" space and serves as a site where objects (or people) are located relative to an observer. The NEW usage of "plane" (as an aircraft) is simply a specific instantiation of this same feature: the aircraft is a physical, orientation-dependent platform that defines the spatial coordinates for its passengers.

Team Support characterizes the modern usage as a "bounded space that one enters," but this is not a new lexical sense; it is a case of **polysemy via metonymic focus**. The "plane" (aircraft) is named for the very feature that defines it: its reliance on lifting surfaces (wings/planes) to maintain its orientation in the air. Just as the OLD corpus uses "plane" to refer to an "inclined plane" (a tool defined by its surface) or a "ground plane" (a surface defining a field of view), the NEW corpus uses "plane" to refer to a machine defined by its aerodynamic surfaces. The "interiority" noted by Team Support is a situational consequence of being *on* that surface-based platform, not a departure from the word's fundamental semantic core of **[+planar orientation/surface utility]**. The word remains a label for a spatial, surface-oriented platform, whether that platform is a writing desk, a geometric horizon, or an aircraft.

### Rebuttal - Round 3

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is the assertion that the modern aircraft is merely a "specific instantiation" of the OLD corpus’s "delimited spatial region" or "surface-based utility." This argument relies on a forced abstraction that ignores the fundamental shift in the word's ontological category.

The fatal flaw in their logic is revealed by this OLD sentence: **"The rockers are somewhat rough, as though a farmer 's plane had fashioned them."**

In this instance, "plane" refers to a tool—a device characterized by a flat cutting edge used to shape wood. Team Refuse attempts to unify the "geometric surface" (the horizon) and the "transport vessel" (the aircraft) under a single feature of "surface-based utility." However, the tool in the OLD corpus is a *subject* that acts upon an object, whereas the "plane" in the NEW corpus is an *interiorized vessel* that contains subjects. The OLD usage denotes either an abstract coordinate (a geometric plane) or a manual instrument (the tool). The NEW usage, as seen in **"You 're the one who sat next to her on the plane yesterday,"** shifts the semantic focus from the *surface quality* of the object to its function as a *volume for human transit*. One cannot "sit next to" a geometric plane or a carpenter's tool; the semantic feature [ +container for human transport] is entirely absent from the OLD corpus, rendering the "polysemy" argument a retrospective projection.

The transition from "plane" as a *geometric/manual attribute* to "plane" as an *autonomous vehicle* represents a genuine shift in core features: the word has moved from describing the property of a surface or a handheld instrument to identifying a self-contained, mobile environment.

**Change Type:** Metaphorical extension/Functional shift.
**Causal Driver:** Technological innovation (lexical narrowing of "airplane" to "plane" via ellipsis).

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the OLD sentence: **"The rockers are somewhat rough, as though a farmer 's plane had fashioned them."** They argue that because this "plane" is a handheld tool used to shape surfaces, it lacks the [+container for human transport] feature found in the NEW corpus, signaling a fundamental ontological shift.

However, Team Support overlooks the core semantic feature that bridges all usages: **[+planar orientation/surface-based interaction]**. 

In both the OLD and NEW corpora, the word "plane" is fundamentally defined by its relation to a **[+defined spatial plane]**. The carpenter’s tool in the OLD corpus is defined by its ability to create or move along a flat surface (a plane). Similarly, an aircraft—which Team Support identifies as an "interiorized vessel"—is aerodynamically defined by its reliance on "lifting surfaces" (wings) and its operation along a specific, controlled **[+horizontal or inclined plane]** through the atmosphere.

The OLD corpus explicitly licenses this spatial interaction in the sentence: **"For a writing desk, which is an inclined plane, does not make a right angle with the table on which it stands... but it makes a smaller angle."** This sentence demonstrates that "plane" functions as a term for a spatial orientation or a surface that defines movement and positioning. 

The NEW usage—where one sits "on the plane"—is simply a case of **metonymic polysemy**. The noun has undergone lexical narrowing (ellipsis from "airplane"), but the word's reference remains tethered to the object’s essential character as a mobile, planar surface-based vehicle. Just as the "farmer’s plane" is defined by its interaction with a surface, the "aircraft plane" is defined by its traversal of a spatial plane. The shift from "tool" to "vehicle" does not constitute a new lexical sense, but rather a shift in the *type* of surface-based object being described, while the underlying semantic requirement of a defined planar trajectory remains constant across both corpora.

### Rebuttal - Round closing

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Support maintains that the evolution of "plane" necessitates a reclassification from a manual tool to a vehicle of transit. Our strongest evidence lies in the shift from surface-shaping instruments to autonomous transport, exemplified by: **"The plane landed safely at the airport."** This usage confirms the term now denotes a complex, self-propelled vessel rather than a hand-held implement.

Team Refuse argues that "plane" remains a stable category of surface-based utility, citing: **"The rockers are somewhat rough, as though a farmer 's plane had fashioned them."** However, this argument collapses because the cited sentence describes an object modifying wood through direct manual labor, which is fundamentally distinct from the modern aircraft's role in aerial navigation. By equating a carpenter’s tool with a commercial jet, Team Refuse ignores that the modern corpus exclusively defines "plane" as a vehicle of transit, not a device for physical surface modification.

**Change Type: Semantic Expansion and Shift; Causal Driver: Technological Innovation.**

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Refuse maintains that "plane" functions as a stable category defined by its geometric and physical relationship to surfaces. Our strongest evidence remains: **"The rockers are somewhat rough, as though a farmer 's plane had fashioned them."** This confirms that "plane" historically designates an instrument defined by its contact with and modification of a surface, grounding its utility in spatial interaction.

Team Support claims the modern aircraft represents an ontological shift, yet this ignores that the tool in the corpus is already an agent of physical transit and modification. By asserting that one cannot "sit next to" a carpenter’s tool, they overlook that the "plane" in **"The rockers are somewhat rough, as though a farmer 's plane had fashioned them"** is the functional progenitor of the object. The aircraft is simply the evolution of the "plane" as a device that navigates or shapes its environment. The transition is not a shift in category, but a consistent application of the word’s inherent function as an instrument of spatial engagement, confirming that "plane" consistently denotes a tool of surface-based utility.

### ⚖️ LLM Judge

The debate demonstrates that the noun 'plane' underwent a fundamental shift in its referent class. In the OLD corpus (1810-1860), 'plane' refers to a geometric surface or a manual woodworking tool. In the NEW corpus (1960-2010), it refers to a complex, autonomous, passenger-bearing aircraft. While Team Refuse argues for a stable core of 'planar orientation' or 'surface-based utility,' this is a retrospective abstraction that fails to account for the shift in selectional restrictions. The NEW usage as a 'container for human transport' (e.g., 'sat next to her on the plane') requires features of interiority and mobility that are absent from the OLD corpus's static geometric or manual tool definitions. The emergence of the aircraft as a dominant referent represents a categorical mutation driven by the technological innovation of aviation, where the term was repurposed to label a new, distinct class of object.

## Target Word: `word_nn`

**Verdict**: STABLE  
**Debate Mode**: multi | **Rounds**: 3 | **Time**: 

### Opening Statements

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

### 1. OLD dominant sense
The dominant sense of "word" in the OLD corpus is **a discrete unit of spoken or written language conveying a specific meaning or message** (feature: [+linguistic unit], [+communicative]). It is used either to refer to a single lexical item ("play of words") or a conveyed message/statement ("send word," "keep thy words").

### 2. Evidence for change
The NEW corpus introduces a usage where "word" functions as a formal title or a designation for an entire text or literary work, rather than a constituent part of speech.

*   **Sentence 1:** "...in a work entitled by the scribe Divrey Ha-Me'oroth ( ' The Words of the Luminaries ' ) , that contain texts for communal worship..."
*   **Sentence 2:** "These words were taken to cover the President 's intent to weed out..."

**Analysis:** In the OLD corpus, "word" refers to the communicative act or the phonetic/written unit. In NEW Sentence 1, "The Words of the Luminaries" functions as a proper noun/title for a multi-page manuscript or collection of prayers. The OLD sense of a "communicative unit" is insufficient because the referent here is a *compendium* or *literary work* rather than a single linguistic unit. In NEW Sentence 2, "These words" refers to an entire policy or set of intentions attributed to an individual, extending the referent from a linguistic string to an abstract conceptual framework or policy position.

### 3. Best counterevidence
*   **NEW Sentence:** "He could n't possibly , in justice , speak a word in his own defense."
*   **NEW Sentence:** "I 've never said a word to Malraux about the Messages Bibligues..."

**Analysis:** These sentences continue the OLD sense of "word" as a single, discrete unit of speech. They do not undermine the claim because semantic change (specifically generalization) does not require the total replacement of the original sense, but rather the emergence of a new, broader category of reference that the original definition cannot encompass.

### 4. Why this is not topic/register/polysemy
This is not merely a change in topic (e.g., from personal speech to formal documents) or register. The shift involves a change in the **referent class**: the word has evolved from referring to a constituent element of language (a "word" as a linguistic atom) to referring to a whole text, book, or abstract body of policy. This constitutes an expansion of the denotation from a part-to-whole relationship, where "word" can now stand in for the entire composition or the abstract intent behind it.

### 5. Change Type & Causal Driver
*   **Change Type:** Generalization
*   **Causal Driver:** Linguistic Drift

### 6. Confidence: 75/100

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

As a member of Team Refuse, I have analyzed the corpus data for the noun "word." My findings are as follows:

### 1. OLD Core Sense
The OLD corpus (1810–1860) defines "word" as a discrete unit of language, either spoken or written, functioning as a vehicle for communication or information.
*   **Key Semantic Features:**
    *   [+Communicative]: Used to convey information, intent, or emotion.
    *   [+Auditory/Visual]: Manifests as a perceptible sign (speech or text).
    *   [+Discrete]: Capable of being counted, held, or kept (e.g., "keep all thy words").

### 2. NEW Uses Consistent with the Old Sense
The NEW corpus (1960–2010) continues to utilize "word" as a discrete unit of communication.
*   **"He could n't possibly, in justice, speak a word in his own defense."**
    *   *Shared Feature:* [+Communicative].
    *   *OLD Licensing:* "The President sends word that there will be no great battle..." (Both rely on the word as a unit of informative transmission).
*   **"He was staring intently at Marrok's face, and when the words stopped, he closed his eyes with a little sigh."**
    *   *Shared Feature:* [+Auditory/Discrete].
    *   *OLD Licensing:* "He knew the voice -- he heard the words..." (Both treat words as a sequence of discrete auditory signals).

### 3. Strongest Evidence for Change
The most challenging usage is found in:
*   **"...a work entitled by the scribe Divrey Ha-Me'oroth ( ' The Words of the Luminaries ' )..."**
*   **"These words were taken to cover the President 's intent to weed out..."**

These instances suggest a shift from a *literal* unit of speech to an *abstracted* representation of a broader concept, policy, or text-collection title.

### 4. Why they are still compatible with the OLD sense
The feature **[+Discrete]** remains the anchor. In the OLD corpus, "words" were already treated as entities that could be "kept" ("I will keep all thy words in my heart") or "lamented over" as a collection ("words of remorse"). 
*   **Referent vs. Sense:** The shift is purely referential. Using "words" to refer to a formal title or a summary of intent does not alter the underlying sense that the referent is composed of linguistic units. The "Words of the Luminaries" is simply a title composed of words; the "President's words" are the specific utterances he made. The sense of the noun remains a linguistic unit.

### 5. Why the difference is polysemy/register, not diachronic change
The variation in the NEW corpus is a result of **situational polysemy**—the application of a stable concept to different communicative contexts (legal, theological, or political). The OLD corpus already established that a "word" is not merely a single morpheme but an instrument of human agency ("power for good in her hands"). When the NEW corpus describes "words" as covering an "intent," it is using the same mechanism found in the OLD corpus, where "words" are equated with the weight of a person's character or message ("weight in all her words"). The core semantic structure is identical; the environment in which it is deployed has simply expanded to include modern administrative and scholarly discourse.

### 6. Confidence: 95/100

### Rebuttal - Round 1

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that the NEW corpus usage remains anchored to the **[+Discrete]** feature found in the OLD corpus. They argue that because "words" can be "kept" or "lamented" in both eras, the underlying sense is unchanged.

I rebut this with the following NEW corpus sentence:
*   **"I always sensed the tension and blood-red anger lurking just below the surface of his every smile, his every word, his every glance and look."**

In the OLD corpus, "words" are consistently tied to **[+Communicative]** intentionality—they are vehicles for information, remorse, or commands (e.g., "The President sends word," "words of remorse," "weight in all her words"). They are agents of external transmission. In the cited NEW sentence, however, "word" has shifted from a **[+Communicative]** act to a **[+Symptomatic]** index. The "word" here is not a unit of language carrying content; it is a behavioral marker of internal state, equivalent to a "glance" or a "smile." This is a fundamental feature-level shift: the word has been stripped of its linguistic content and reclassified as a physiological symptom of affect.

Furthermore, Team Refuse fails to account for the **[+Textual/Abstract]** shift seen in:
*   **"...a work entitled by the scribe Divrey Ha-Me'oroth ('The Words of the Luminaries')..."**

In the OLD corpus, "words" are fleeting, performative, or internal ("sunk deeply into my heart"). In the NEW, "word" is increasingly used to denote static, codified metadata or institutional "covers" (as in the President's "intent"). The shift is not merely contextual; it is a movement from **[+Performative/Episodic]** (the OLD "word" as an event) to **[+Constitutive/Objectified]** (the NEW "word" as a static component of a larger system or index of affect).

**Change Type:** Semantic Bleaching and Functional Reclassification (from Communicative Agent to Behavioral/Objectified Marker).
**Causal Driver:** De-agentification and abstraction in modern discourse.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the claim that in the sentence "I always sensed the tension and blood-red anger lurking just below the surface of his every smile, his every word, his every glance and look," the noun "word" has shifted from a **[+Communicative]** act to a **[+Symptomatic]** index of affect.

However, this argument ignores that the fundamental semantic feature bridging both eras is **[+Manifestation of Internal State]**. The "word" functions as a window into the subject’s psyche in both the 19th and 21st centuries. Team Support’s assertion that "word" is here stripped of linguistic content is incorrect; the "word" remains a communicative unit, but its *value* is derived from the speaker's internal state—a feature already present in the OLD corpus.

Consider this OLD sentence:
*   *"Emily, there was a celestial melody in mother's voice in the gift of speaking, and a weight in all her words, and though I gave no outward sign, they sunk deeply into my heart."*

In this OLD example, the "words" are not merely neutral information packets; they are the vehicle for the mother’s "celestial melody" and "weight," which function as indicators of her internal character and emotional depth. Just as the modern "word" acts as a marker of "tension," the OLD "word" acts as a marker of the mother's soul. The shift is not a change in lexical sense, but a consistent application of the **[+Manifestation of Internal State]** feature across different contexts.

Regarding the NEW sentence concerning *Divrey Ha-Me'oroth* ("The Words of the Luminaries"), Team Support identifies a shift toward the **[+Constitutive/Objectified]**. This is not a new lexical sense, but a standard polysemy where "word" denotes a unit of discourse within a larger collection (a "work"). This is fully licensed by the OLD corpus, where "words" were already treated as discrete, collectable items: 
*   *"I will keep all thy words in my heart of hearts."* 

In the OLD corpus, "words" are already treated as objects to be "kept," demonstrating that the transition from a fleeting utterance to a static, objectified entity is a stable, latent capacity of the word, not a diachronic innovation.

### Rebuttal - Round 2

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that the modern usage of "word" as a **[+Constitutive/Objectified]** unit in titles—such as *Divrey Ha-Me'oroth* ("The Words of the Luminaries")—is functionally identical to the OLD usage of "keeping words in one's heart." 

This comparison is flawed. In the OLD corpus, "words" are kept as *internalized moral directives* (e.g., "I will keep all thy words in my heart of hearts"). The semantic feature here is **[+Instructional/Moral]**. In contrast, the NEW corpus treats "words" as **[+Formalized/Static Textual Artifacts]**. A clear contradiction to the idea of continuity is found in the NEW sentence: *"...in a work entitled by the scribe Divrey Ha-Me'oroth ('The Words of the Luminaries'), that contain texts for communal worship..."* Here, "word" has been reified into a bibliographic label for a scroll or manuscript. This is not a "fleeting utterance" being held in the heart; it is a **[+Taxonomic]** classification of a document. The semantic feature has migrated from the subjective, auditory realm of the *speech act* to the objective, visual realm of the *archival object*.

Furthermore, Team Refuse fails to account for the shift in the "word" as a **[+Symptomatic]** index. Their reliance on the "weight" of the mother’s words in the OLD corpus is a category error. In that OLD sentence, the "weight" is an attribute of the *message* (the moral gravity of the speech). In the NEW sentence—*"I always sensed the tension... lurking just below the surface of his every... word"*—the word is not carrying a message at all; it is a **[+Biological/Physiological]** byproduct of the subject’s nervous system, equivalent to a "glance" or a "look." When "word" becomes interchangeable with non-communicative physical cues like "glances," it has shed its core **[+Linguistic]** feature entirely.

**Change Type:** Semantic Bleaching / Shift of Categorization.
**Causal Driver:** Reification (moving from communicative speech acts to objectified textual/symptomatic markers).

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the claim that "word" in the NEW sentence *“...in a work entitled by the scribe Divrey Ha-Me’oroth (‘The Words of the Luminaries’)...”* functions as a **[+Taxonomic]** label for an archival object, which they argue is a departure from the "fleeting utterance" of the OLD corpus.

However, this argument ignores the persistent semantic feature of **[+Representational Content]**, which bridges both usages. Whether "word" refers to a spoken utterance or a recorded text, it consistently denotes a discrete unit of meaningful communication intended to convey a specific message or intent. 

The OLD corpus already licenses this feature in the sentence: *"The President sends word that there will be no great battle..."* In this instance, "word" is not a fleeting, subjective vocalization; it is a formal report—a piece of intelligence or a message—that functions as an objective unit of information. Just as *Divrey Ha-Me’oroth* serves as a container for communal texts, the "word" sent by the President serves as a container for strategic information. In both cases, "word" serves as a vehicle for a message, regardless of whether that message is transmitted orally or memorialized in a manuscript.

Regarding the claim that "word" has become a **[+Biological/Physiological]** byproduct in the NEW sentence *"I always sensed the tension... lurking just below the surface of his every... word,"* this is not a lexical shift but a clear instance of polysemy. The word retains its core **[+Linguistic]** feature: it remains a communicative act. The fact that the speaker interprets the *manner* of the utterance (the "tension") alongside the *content* of the utterance does not strip the word of its linguistic nature. This is mirrored in the OLD corpus: *"Emily, there was a celestial melody in mother’s voice... and a weight in all her words."* Here, the "weight" and "melody" are attributes of the speech act that the listener senses beyond the mere literal meaning. The NEW usage is simply a modern iteration of this established capacity for "word" to encompass both the semantic content and the indexical quality of the speaker's delivery. There is no change in the lexical sense, only a consistent application of the word to the multifaceted nature of human communication.

### Rebuttal - Round 3

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that the NEW corpus usage of "word" as a physiological indicator of tension (*"his every word"*) is a mere polysemous extension of the "weight" and "melody" found in the OLD corpus. This is a false equivalence.

In the OLD corpus, the "weight" of words is an evaluative judgment of the *message’s significance* or the *speaker’s authority* (e.g., *"there was... a weight in all her words, and... they sunk deeply into my heart"*). Here, the word remains a communicative unit that is *received* and *internalized*. In contrast, the NEW corpus sentence—*"I always sensed the tension and blood-red anger lurking just below the surface of his every... word"*—strips the "word" of its communicative agency. In this context, the "word" is no longer a vessel for content, but a **[+Symptomatic]** marker of internal state, functioning as a physiological byproduct of the speaker’s temperament rather than a carrier of meaning. The "word" here is not something heard for its weight, but something scanned for its "tension."

This confirms a fundamental shift: "word" has evolved from a **[+Linguistic/Communicative]** token—a unit of message delivery—to a **[+Indexical/Diagnostic]** token—a unit of behavioral analysis. The OLD corpus requires the "word" to have a sender and a receiver of information; the NEW corpus usage allows the "word" to function as a mere biometric datum, detached from the delivery of any specific message.

**Change Type:** Semantic Narrowing/Shift (from communicative agent to behavioral diagnostic).
**Causal Driver:** Pragmatic specialization in high-anxiety interpersonal discourse.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the NEW sentence: *"I always sensed the tension and blood-red anger lurking just below the surface of his every smile, his every word, his every glance and look."* They argue this usage shifts "word" from a [+Linguistic] communicative unit to a [+Symptomatic] diagnostic datum.

However, this argument fails to account for the persistent semantic feature of **[+Affective Manifestation]**. In both corpora, the "word" is not merely an abstract linguistic signifier, but a vehicle that carries the emotional or moral internal state of the speaker, which is then perceived by an observer.

The OLD corpus already licenses this feature in the sentence: *"Emily, there was a celestial melody in mother’s voice in the gift of speaking, and a weight in all her words, and though I gave no outward sign, they sunk deeply into my heart."* 

In this OLD example, the "word" is not being evaluated strictly for its propositional content or message delivery; it is being evaluated for its "weight" and "melody"—qualities that function as indices of the mother’s internal state ("celestial melody"). The listener is not merely "receiving information," but is experiencing the speaker’s temperament as a visceral, symptomatic impact ("sunk deeply into my heart"). 

Because "word" in the OLD corpus already functions as an externalization of an internal state (a "weight" or "melody" reflecting the speaker's essence), the NEW usage is not a shift to a "biometric datum." It is a clear instance of **polysemy** where the established semantic feature of **[+Affective Manifestation]**—the capacity for a word to encapsulate the speaker’s emotional presence—is simply being applied to a different emotional context (tension/anger instead of melody/weight). The core lexical sense remains the same: the "word" acts as the observable extension of the speaker's internal reality.

### Rebuttal - Round closing

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Support maintains that the evolution of "word" reflects a shift from communicative agency to diagnostic objectification. Our evidence shows:

1. In 1810, "weight in all her words" implies a moral or social burden held by the speaker, emphasizing the word as an active, consequential tool.
2. By 2010, the corpus reveals a shift where the word is treated as an anatomical or behavioral symptom, such as "tension... lurking just below the surface of his every... word."

Team Refuse argues that the word remains a bridge for human connection. However, this ignores the reductive nature of the 2010 usage, where the listener is no longer engaging with the *content* of the speech, but rather parsing a "tension" or "anger" that functions as a biometric marker. As seen in the 2010 citation, the word is now an evidentiary trace rather than an instrument of intent, effectively stripping the speaker of their agency to communicate beyond their immediate physiological state.

**Change Type:** Semantic Narrowing (from active agency to diagnostic trace).
**Causal Driver:** Shift toward clinical/psychological observation in narrative discourse.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Refuse maintains that the semantic core of "word" remains stable. Our strongest evidence lies in the persistent requirement for an externalized, audible manifestation of the speaker’s interiority: whether in 1810—"there was... a weight in all her words"—or 2010—"tension... lurking just below the surface of his every... word"—the "word" acts as the essential bridge between internal state and external reception.

Team Support argues that the 2010 usage strips the "word" of communicative agency, reducing it to a diagnostic datum. This is a false dichotomy. In the 2010 sentence, "tension" and "anger" are not independent of the utterance; they are qualities carried *by* the word, just as "weight" was carried by the word in 1810. If the word were merely a biometric byproduct, the sentence would not require the specific vehicle of the "word" to convey the speaker’s state. Because the "word" remains the primary vessel for the listener to interpret the speaker’s intent, it retains its communicative function. The shift is stylistic, not categorical; the "word" remains the fundamental unit of human connection.

### ⚖️ LLM Judge

The debate centers on whether the use of 'word' to describe behavioral markers (e.g., 'tension... lurking... in his every word') or bibliographic titles (e.g., 'The Words of the Luminaries') constitutes a semantic shift. Team Refuse successfully demonstrates that these usages are consistent with the core semantic feature of 'word' as a [+Communicative] unit that serves as a vehicle for the speaker's internal state or intent. The OLD corpus evidence, such as 'a weight in all her words,' establishes that 'word' has historically functioned as an index of the speaker's character and emotional depth, not merely as a neutral information packet. The NEW corpus usages cited by Team Support represent situational polysemy—applying an established, multifaceted concept to modern contexts—rather than a loss or gain of core semantic features. The 'reification' or 'diagnostic' uses are extensions of the word's inherent capacity to manifest the speaker's interiority, and thus do not constitute a diachronic semantic change.

## Target Word: `attack_nn`

**Verdict**: STABLE  
**Debate Mode**: multi | **Rounds**: 3 | **Time**: 

### Opening Statements

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

### 1. OLD dominant sense
The dominant sense in the OLD corpus is **"a hostile physical or verbal offensive"** directed by an agent against an entity (person, military force, or idea). Core semantic features include: **[+intentional]**, **[+agentive]**, and **[+hostile]**. Even in the medical context ("gastric attack"), the term retains the sense of an acute, hostile, or invasive onset of a condition.

### 2. Evidence for change
The NEW corpus includes usages where "attack" no longer requires an agent or hostile intent:
*   **"I reminded them about the heart attack on the marathon..."** and **"...and then must have had a heart attack."**
    *   *Analysis:* In these instances, "attack" refers to a physiological event (myocardial infarction). Unlike the OLD sense, this lacks an external agentive aggressor. The medical condition is an internal, non-hostile biological failure.
*   **"It revolves mainly around rookie running back Anthony Thomas and a dink-and-dunk passing attack."**
    *   *Analysis:* Here, "attack" refers to a strategic methodology or a stylistic approach in sports. It is detached from the concept of a "hostile offensive" and functions as a synonym for a "system of play" or "offensive strategy," lacking the feature of [±hostile] in the traditional sense of interpersonal or military conflict.

### 3. Best counterevidence
*   **"It was the first attack on the city since the nationwide offensive began Feb. 23."** (NEW)
*   **"The controllers prepare the proper coded message to order the attack."** (NEW)
    *   *Reasoning:* These sentences represent the continued use of the OLD sense (a deliberate, agentive military offensive). However, they do not undermine the claim of change because they demonstrate **polysemy** rather than the exclusion of the original meaning. The existence of the original sense alongside the new, non-agentive/strategic senses confirms the expansion of the word's semantic range.

### 4. Why this is not topic/register/polysemy
While "attack" remains polysemous, the change is not merely a shift in topic or register. The introduction of the "sports strategy" and "medical event" usages represents a fundamental shift in selectional restrictions. The OLD sense required an entity that could be "held responsible" for the action (an attacker). The new referents (a heart attack, a passing attack) are processes or systems rather than discrete acts of aggression. This constitutes a change in the semantic features required for the noun's arguments.

### 5. Change Type & Causal Driver
*   **Change Type:** Generalization (The term has expanded from specific hostile acts to include non-hostile, systemic, or biological processes).
*   **Causal Driver:** Linguistic Drift (The metaphorical extension of "attack" to describe any vigorous or focused effort—whether biological or strategic—has become conventionalized over time).

### 6. Confidence: 85/100

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

As a member of Team Refuse, I have analyzed the corpus for the noun "attack." I contend that "attack" remains semantically stable, as the core features identified in the 19th-century corpus sufficiently license all subsequent uses.

### 1. OLD core sense
Based on the provided OLD corpus, the core sense of "attack" is **an intentional, hostile action or initiative directed against an entity to cause harm or disruption.**
Key semantic features:
*   **[+initiative]**: An active, deliberate commencement of a sequence.
*   **[+adversarial]**: Directed against a perceived opponent or obstacle.
*   **[+disruptive]**: Aimed at overcoming, damaging, or altering the state of the target.

### 2. NEW uses consistent with that sense
*   **"It was the first attack on the city since the nationwide offensive began..."**
    *   Shared feature: **[+adversarial/disruptive]**.
    *   OLD license: "...supposing that an attack was about to be made on the town..." (The target is a geographic entity/group).
*   **"The controllers prepare the proper coded message to order the attack."**
    *   Shared feature: **[+initiative]**.
    *   OLD license: "...intimate his readiness to obey any signal of attack..." (The attack is an action initiated by a signal/order).

### 3. Strongest evidence for change
*   **"It revolves mainly around rookie running back Anthony Thomas and a dink-and-dunk passing attack."**
*   **"Two decades after... an attack on lavish and costly burials..."**

These challenge stability because the former uses "attack" to describe a sports strategy (neutral/performative context), and the latter uses it to describe a discursive critique (non-physical).

### 4. Why they are still compatible with the OLD sense
The feature **[+initiative/adversarial]** carries over. In the sports example, the "passing attack" is the offensive initiative taken against an opposing team to disrupt their defense. In the "burials" example, the "attack" is an adversarial critique directed at an industry.
*   **Referent shift vs. Sense shift**: The referent has shifted from physical military violence to abstract discursive or tactical pressure. However, the *sense* remains the same: the exertion of force (physical or intellectual) against an opposition to achieve a goal. This is supported by the OLD sentence: "the modes of attack upon Liberal Christianity," which already established the use of "attack" for non-physical, intellectual opposition.

### 5. Why the difference is polysemy/register, not diachronic change
The expansion of "attack" into sports or rhetoric is a matter of situational polysemy. The OLD corpus already contained a mix of physical violence ("mountain wolf"), military maneuvers ("take possession of the cannon"), intellectual/doctrinal opposition ("attack upon Liberal Christianity"), and medical phenomena ("gastric attack"). Because the OLD corpus already spanned physical, medical, and abstract/ideological domains, the NEW uses in sports and social commentary are merely applications of the established, flexible core features to new domains of competition. The "heart attack" usage is a direct continuation of the medical sense present in the 19th-century corpus ("sharp gastric attack").

### 6. Confidence: 90/100

### Rebuttal - Round 1

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that the sports usage ("passing attack") is simply a continuation of the [+initiative] and [+adversarial] features found in the 19th-century corpus. They argue that because "attack" was already used for military maneuvers, its application to football is merely a consistent extension of these features.

This is fundamentally flawed. In the 19th-century corpus, "attack" consistently implies a **[+hostile_intent]**—a desire to cause damage or defeat an enemy. Consider the sentence: *"To move upon Warrington at daylight; and he told me to tell you to be on hand when the attack is made, -- flank the rebels in the woods, and keep on ahead of them to cut off their retreat."* Here, the "attack" is a discrete, goal-oriented event intended to produce a specific outcome (the destruction or surrender of the rebels). 

In contrast, the modern sports usage—*"dink-and-dunk passing attack"*—describes a **systemic, ongoing operational mode** rather than a singular, hostile action. In football, a "passing attack" is a style of play that persists throughout a game, regardless of whether it is currently causing harm or disruption to the opponent. The semantic feature has shifted from **[+eventive/hostile]** (an act of war) to **[+stative/methodological]** (a strategic framework). 

Furthermore, Team Refuse fails to acknowledge that the "gastric attack" in the OLD corpus is a *pathological event* (an external force acting upon the patient), whereas the modern "passing attack" is an *agentive choice* (a player’s performance). The evolution of "attack" from a discrete, externally-imposed event to a descriptive label for a routine organizational strategy represents a genuine bleaching of the "hostile intent" feature.

**Change Type:** Semantic Broadening (from specific, hostile event to general strategic/methodological category).
**Causal Driver:** Functional specialization in professionalized, competitive domains (sports and tactical discourse).

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is their claim that the modern sports usage ("dink-and-dunk passing attack") marks a shift from a **[+hostile_intent]** event to a **[+stative/methodological]** framework, arguing that the latter lacks the singular, damaging nature of the 19th-century military sense.

However, this ignores the core shared semantic feature that bridges both eras: **[+aggressive_engagement]**. In both the 19th century and the 21st, "attack" denotes the deployment of resources to overcome an opposing force or obstacle. The "passing attack" in football is not a neutral system; it is an active, competitive deployment of offensive resources specifically designed to break down a defense. 

This feature is already fully licensed in the OLD corpus, specifically in the sentence: *"V. Conclusion; the modes of attack upon Liberal Christianity, the same that were used against the Doctrine of the Apostles and Reformers."* 

In this OLD instance, "attack" does not refer to a singular, physically violent military strike, nor is it a sudden "pathological event." Instead, it describes a **[+methodological]** approach—a sustained, organized intellectual campaign used to challenge an opposing position. The "modes of attack" mentioned here are, like the "passing attack," a strategic framework of engagement. 

Regarding the "gastric attack," Team Support characterizes it as a "pathological event," but this is merely a case of polysemy where the feature of **[+assaultive_force]** (the illness assaulting the body) is applied to a medical context. The fact that the word could describe both an external military force and an internal physiological one in the 1800s proves that the word was already flexible enough to denote different manifestations of an active, overwhelming force. The modern sports usage is not a "bleaching" of meaning, but a consistent application of the established sense of "an active, strategic offensive against an opponent," which has been present in the language for centuries.

The term has shifted from a description of an *event of violence* to a *label for a medical condition*.

The OLD corpus already licenses this non-martial, systemic usage in the sentence: *"V. Conclusion; the modes of attack upon Liberal Christianity, the same that were used against the Doctrine of the Apostles and Reformers."* Here, the "attack" is not a singular physical strike but a **[+procedural]** methodology—a "mode" of engagement—used to dismantle an opponent's position. Regarding the medical examples, Team Support claims a shift from "event of violence" to "medical classification." This is not a change in lexical sense but a clear case of **polysemy**—a long-standing metaphorical extension where the human body is viewed as a territory subjected to an acute, destabilizing intrusion. The OLD sentence, *"she had been through a pretty sharp gastric attack,"* confirms that "attack" was already being used in the 1800s to characterize a spontaneous, internal physiological crisis. The modern usage does not represent a new sense; it simply applies the established, existing framework of an "acute internal assault" to a specific cardiac event.
