### Rebuttal - Round 3

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that the modern sports usage of "attack" is merely a continuation of the "modes of attack upon Liberal Christianity," asserting that both represent a **[+procedural]** methodology to overcome resistance. 

This is refuted by the contrast between these two sentences:
1. **OLD:** *"V. Conclusion; the modes of attack upon Liberal Christianity, the same that were used against the Doctrine of the Apostles and Reformers."*
2. **NEW:** *"It revolves mainly around rookie running back Anthony Thomas and a dink-and-dunk passing attack."*

In the OLD sentence, "attack" functions as a **[+polemical]** instrument; it requires an ideological adversary and a deliberate intent to refute or dismantle a belief system. In the NEW sentence, however, the "passing attack" is a **[+technical]** descriptor of a functional system. It does not target an adversary’s legitimacy or existence; it is a neutral, internal component of a game strategy. The semantic feature of "hostility" or "antagonism" has been entirely bleached, leaving only a "method of operation."

Furthermore, Team Refuse fails to address the fundamental shift in the medical sense. While they claim the "gastric attack" and "heart attack" are identical, the OLD sentence (*"pretty sharp gastric attack"*) describes a *symptomatic event* occurring *within* a subject, whereas the NEW sentence (*"heart attack on the marathon"*) treats the "attack" as an *external incident* that can be survived or avoided. The shift is from an **[+internal/physiological]** disturbance to an **[+external/event-based]** phenomenon. 

Ultimately, "attack" has evolved from a term requiring a **[+hostile_agent]** (even in abstract forms) to a term describing **[+functional_maneuvers]** or **[+discrete_incidents]**, marking a clear transition from interpersonal conflict to abstract categorization.

**Change Type:** Semantic Bleaching / Generalization
**Causal Driver:** Functional shift from adversarial intent to systematic/procedural classification.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the contrast between the polemical "modes of attack upon Liberal Christianity" and the technical "dink-and-dunk passing attack." They argue that the transition from a [+polemical] intent to a [+technical] strategy represents a loss of antagonism.

However, this ignores the shared semantic feature of **[+directed-force-application]**. Whether the "attack" is an ideological critique or a football strategy, the term consistently denotes the focused, kinetic, or systematic application of pressure toward a specific objective to overcome resistance. 

The OLD corpus already licenses this non-hostile, systematic usage in the sentence: *"To move upon Warrington at daylight; and he told me to tell you to be on hand when the attack is made, -- flank the rebels in the woods, and keep on ahead of them to cut off their retreat."* Here, the "attack" is not merely an emotional outburst of hostility, but a structured, tactical maneuver (flanking/cutting off retreat) performed as part of a larger plan. The "passing attack" in the NEW corpus is simply a specialized iteration of this same tactical methodology, where the "force" is the movement of the ball rather than the movement of troops.

Regarding the medical sense, Team Support claims a shift from "internal disturbance" to "external incident," yet the OLD corpus already frames a "gastric attack" as an event that *happens to* the subject—the subject is a patient who "had been through" it. The NEW corpus usage ("heart attack on the marathon") does not represent a new lexical sense, but rather **polysemy** within the established category of an "acute onset of a pathological condition." In both cases, the "attack" is a discrete, localized event of physiological failure. 

Because the core feature of **[+directed-force-application]** remains the constant cognitive anchor for the word, "attack" has not undergone genuine semantic change; it has merely undergone a natural expansion of its domain of application to new, technical fields.

### Rebuttal - Round closing

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Support concludes that "attack" has transitioned from a primarily external, adversarial event to a frequent, internal, and often neutral functional descriptor.

Our evidence rests on two points: 
1. The emergence of the "passing attack," which describes a systematic team strategy rather than an external offensive act. 
2. The shift in medical usage from the "gastric attack" (an acute, externalized affliction) to "heart attack," which functions as a standardized clinical label for a condition rather than an aggressive intrusion.

Team Refuse argues that the "passing attack" maintains a [+hostile_agent] requirement because it seeks to "dismantle an opponent's defense." However, the corpus sentence "the team’s passing attack gained 300 yards" demonstrates that the noun denotes a statistical output or a strategic phase of play, not an act of malice. The "attack" here is a neutral, internal component of a game’s mechanics, lacking the adversarial intent present in "modes of attack upon Liberal Christianity."

**Change Type:** Semantic Bleaching / Functionalization.
**Causal Driver:** Domain expansion from interpersonal/theological conflict to technical/systemic processes.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Refuse maintains that the noun "attack" remains structurally stable, anchored by its consistent role as a disruptive force against an established state.

First, the corpus confirms that whether in 1810 or 2010, the term necessitates a target. The "passing attack" functions precisely because it disrupts a defensive formation, mirroring the "modes of attack upon Liberal Christianity" that target a theological position. Both require a resistance to be overcome; the methodology remains inherently adversarial, not merely functional.

Second, the medical evidence remains uniform. Team Support claims a shift, yet the corpus shows both "pretty sharp gastric attack" and "heart attack" function as acute, involuntary physiological disruptions. Neither sentence treats the event as a neutral "external incident"; both describe a sudden, forceful intrusion upon the body’s integrity.

Team Support’s claim that "passing attack" is neutral ignores the inherent aggression required in sports. By definition, a "passing attack" is designed to dismantle an opponent's defense, proving that "attack" retains its [+hostile_agent] requirement even in modern usage. The term has not bleached; it has merely expanded its domain of conflict.

### ⚖️ LLM Judge

The debate demonstrates that the core semantic features of 'attack'—specifically the application of directed force or initiative against a resistance—remain consistent across both corpora. Team Support argues for semantic bleaching based on the 'passing attack' in sports and the 'heart attack' in medical contexts. However, Team Refuse successfully demonstrates that these uses are consistent with the established polysemy of the 19th-century corpus. The OLD corpus already contained non-martial, abstract, and medical usages (e.g., 'modes of attack upon Liberal Christianity' and 'gastric attack'), which share the same underlying conceptual structure of an active, disruptive force directed against a target or state. The shift in domain (from theology/warfare to sports/clinical medicine) constitutes a change in register and situational application rather than a fundamental change in the noun's semantic features or selectional restrictions. The term remains anchored by the requirement of an active, disruptive initiative against an opposing force or state.

## Target Word: `edge_nn`

**Verdict**: CHANGE DETECTED  
**Debate Mode**: multi | **Rounds**: 3 | **Time**: 

### Opening Statements

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

### 1. OLD dominant sense
The dominant sense of "edge" in the OLD corpus is **[+physical]** and **[+boundary]**, referring to the outer limit, margin, or perimeter of a tangible object (e.g., a cloth, a rock, a leaf, a frame, or a dock). It denotes the physical termination of a surface or the geometric line where a plane meets space.

### 2. Evidence for change
The NEW corpus introduces a metaphorical or abstract usage where "edge" no longer denotes a physical boundary of an object, but a state of extreme proximity to an event, condition, or threshold.

*   **"Some species of wildlife, such as Baltic seals, have been brought to the edge of extinction."**
    *   *Analysis:* "Extinction" is an abstract state, not a physical object with a spatial perimeter. The OLD sense of a physical, tangible boundary cannot account for this usage, as it requires a spatial object to delimit.
*   **"A second EXPLOSION drives him back from the edge and we PULL AWAY."**
    *   *Analysis:* Here, "the edge" refers to a point of danger or a critical threshold of an event. While it implies a spatial location, the referent is no longer the "edge of the canyon" (physical) but the "edge of safety/the event" (abstract/temporal).

### 3. Best counterevidence
*   **"Some species of wildlife, such as Baltic seals, have been brought to the edge of extinction."** (NEW)
*   **"She picked an eyebrow pencil off the edge of the sink and tossed it to him."** (NEW)

The second sentence clearly reflects the OLD physical sense. However, this does not undermine the claim because the existence of a persistent primary sense (polysemy) does not negate the emergence of a new, distinct, and dominant abstract sense in other contexts. The NEW corpus shows that "edge" has expanded its semantic range to include abstract thresholds, which is a clear diachronic development from the strictly physical OLD usage.

### 4. Why this is not topic/register/polysemy
This is not merely a change in topic (e.g., from rocks to extinction) because the conceptual referent has shifted from a **physical object boundary** to a **non-physical threshold**. It is not merely polysemy because the "abstract threshold" sense is a distinct development that relies on the loss of the [+physical] constraint, allowing the word to combine with abstract nouns like "extinction" or abstract situations, which were not present in the OLD corpus. The OLD corpus restricted "edge" to objects (cloth, leaf, rock, pews, dock).

### 5. Change Type & Causal Driver
*   **Change Type:** Generalization (The word has shed its [+physical] restriction to apply to abstract states).
*   **Causal Driver:** Linguistic Drift (The semantic expansion of spatial metaphors into abstract domains is a standard process of internal linguistic evolution).

### 6. Confidence: 85/100

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

As a computational linguist on Team Refuse, I have analyzed the corpus data for the noun "edge." My findings are as follows:

### 1. OLD Core Sense
Based on the 1810–1860 corpus, the core sense of "edge" is: **The physical boundary or limit of a two- or three-dimensional object.**
*   **Key Semantic Features:**
    *   [+Physical]: Refers to a tangible material entity.
    *   [+Spatial_Boundary]: Denotes the terminal limit or perimeter of a surface or volume.
    *   [+Localized]: Occupies a specific, identifiable position in space.

### 2. NEW Uses Consistent with the Old Sense
*   "She picked an eyebrow pencil off the edge of the sink and tossed it to him."
    *   **Shared Feature:** [+Spatial_Boundary].
    *   **Old Licensing Sentence:** "There is a three-line gold frame around the edges with an ornate fern design in each corner." (Both define the limit of a physical surface).
*   "She sits on the edge of the mattress in her blouse and slip..."
    *   **Shared Feature:** [+Physical], [+Spatial_Boundary].
    *   **Old Licensing Sentence:** "The floor was covered in the centre by a carpet... while its edges... were of unspotted cleanliness." (Both define the perimeter of a piece of furniture/fabric).

### 3. Strongest Evidence for Change
The most challenging usage is: "Some species of wildlife, such as Baltic seals, have been brought to the edge of extinction."

### 4. Why They Are Still Compatible with the Old Sense
While the referent of "extinction" is abstract rather than a physical object, the word "edge" retains its semantic function as the **[+Boundary]** point of a state. The shift is merely in the domain of the object being bounded (from physical space to a conceptual state). The feature [+Terminal_Limit] is present in both the OLD sense ("edge of the dock") and this NEW usage. This is a **referent shift** (mapping the spatial concept onto a temporal/conceptual continuum) rather than a shift in the core sense of the word itself.

### 5. Why the Difference is Polysemy, Not Diachronic Change
The NEW usage is a classic instance of situational polysemy via metaphor. The "edge" of extinction functions exactly like the "edge" of a rock or a dock; it marks the point immediately preceding a transition into a new state (falling off the rock/dock vs. falling into extinction). The lexical entry remains stable because the underlying semantic framework—that an "edge" is the point where a defined area concludes—remains intact across both periods. The "edge of the canyon" or "edge of the thicket" in the OLD corpus already established the "edge" as a threshold; applying this to the "edge of extinction" is a natural extension of that threshold concept, not a breakdown of the word's original meaning.

### 6. Confidence: 90/100

### Rebuttal - Round 1

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is their assertion that the "edge of extinction" is merely a minor metaphorical extension of the physical boundary sense, arguing that the underlying semantic framework remains unchanged. This ignores the shift from **[+Static_Spatial_Boundary]** to **[+Dynamic_Threshold_Event]**.

### Rebuttal
Team Refuse claims that the "edge" in the NEW corpus functions exactly like the "edge" of a rock or dock in the OLD corpus. This is contradicted by:
> *"A second EXPLOSION drives him back from the **edge** and we PULL AWAY."* (NEW Corpus)

In the OLD corpus, "edge" consistently marks a static, observable physical perimeter (e.g., "edge of the dock," "edge of the thicket," "edges of the dark cloth"). These boundaries are inherent, passive features of the object itself. In the NEW corpus sentence above, the "edge" is not a property of the canyon; it is a **functional hazard zone** defined by the trajectory of the explosion and the subject's proximity to a catastrophic event. Here, "edge" has evolved from a static descriptor of a physical object into a **deictic marker of danger**, where the boundary is defined by the interaction between the subject and an external force rather than the physical geometry of a surface.

### Feature-Level Analysis
The core semantic feature has shifted from **[+Spatial_Extent]** (the limit of a surface) to **[+Proximity_to_Crisis]** (the threshold of an event). 
*   **OLD (1810–1860):** The feature is **[+Static]**. The "edge" exists regardless of the subject's intent or the presence of external threats.
*   **NEW (1960–2010):** The feature is **[+Event-Oriented]**. The "edge" is now frequently used to denote a precarious state of transition (e.g., "edge of extinction," "edge of the canyon" during an explosion). 

The term has migrated from a geometric noun to a **psychological and situational marker**, signifying the critical point of an imminent loss of control. This is not mere polysemy; it is a fundamental shift in the word's semantic role from describing the *container* of an object to describing the *tension* of an experience.

**Change Type:** Semantic Broadening/Subjectification.
**Causal Driver:** Conceptual Metonymy (transitioning from spatial limits to situational thresholds).

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the usage in the sentence: *"A second EXPLOSION drives him back from the **edge** and we PULL AWAY."* They argue this represents a shift from a static physical boundary to a "functional hazard zone" or "threshold of an event."

However, this argument ignores that the fundamental semantic feature—**[+Terminal_Proximity]**—remains unchanged. In both corpora, "edge" identifies the precise point of transition between a defined space and the void or danger beyond it. The "edge" does not acquire a new, abstract meaning; rather, the context of the sentence (an explosion) merely defines the stakes of that proximity. 

This feature is already fully licensed in the OLD corpus, most notably in this sentence:
> *"The stranger collected his limbs together and rose slowly from the ground... for he perceived a solitary sixpence... lying glittering on the **edge of the dock**; he stooped to pick it up, but before his agitated hand could grasp this fraction of the metallic currency, a young... varlet... pounced upon it."*

In this OLD example, the "edge of the dock" is not merely a static geometric descriptor of the wood; it is the site of a precarious interaction and a potential loss (the sixpence being snatched away). The "edge" serves as the critical boundary where a desired object is vulnerable to being lost. 

The NEW corpus uses "edge" to describe the "edge of extinction" or the "edge of the canyon" precisely because it maps the same **[+Terminal_Proximity]** feature found in the OLD corpus. Whether the subject is at risk of losing a coin or a species, the semantic role of "edge" remains the same: it marks the limit beyond which the subject or object is removed from the current sphere of security or existence. This is not a change in lexical sense; it is a consistent application of the same spatial logic to different domains, which is the definition of polysemy, not semantic shift.

### Rebuttal - Round 2

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is their assertion that the "edge of the dock" (OLD) and the "edge of extinction" (NEW) function under the same semantic logic of **[+Terminal_Proximity]**. They argue that because both involve a "site of vulnerability," the meaning is identical. This is a category error: the dock is a physical coordinate where a physical object (a sixpence) is located, whereas "extinction" is a temporal, existential state.

The contradiction is found in the NEW corpus:
> *"Some species of wildlife, such as Baltic seals, have been brought to the **edge of extinction**."*

In the OLD corpus, "edge" is exclusively a geometric limit of a physical object (a dock, a leaf, a pew, a rock). In the NEW corpus, "edge" has undergone a **metaphorical extension into the temporal/existential domain**. The "edge of extinction" does not denote a physical boundary of a surface or a material object; it denotes the **[+Temporal_Threshold]** of a process. In the OLD corpus, the "edge" is a place where you stand; in the NEW corpus, the "edge" is a state of being that you occupy. 

Team Refuse fails to account for the loss of the **[+Spatial_Extent]** feature. In the OLD corpus, the edge is always the termination of a physical dimension (the "edges of the dark cloth," the "edges of the leaf"). In the NEW corpus, specifically in "edge of extinction," the word has shed its requirement for physical materiality entirely, shifting from a spatial container to a point on a metaphorical timeline. This is not merely a change in context; it is a shift from **[+Physical_Boundary]** to **[+Abstract_Threshold]**.

**Change Type:** Metaphorical Extension (Domain Shift)
**Causal Driver:** Abstraction (from spatial geometry to existential/temporal states)

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence for a semantic shift is the phrase **"brought to the edge of extinction,"** which they argue represents a departure from spatial geometry toward an abstract, existential threshold.

However, this argument ignores the shared semantic feature of **[+Point_of_Transition]**, which governs both the physical and metaphorical uses of "edge." Whether referring to a physical object or an existential state, "edge" consistently marks the final, infinitesimal boundary between a contained state and the "beyond."

This feature is already fully licensed in the OLD corpus, specifically in the sentence:
> *"In rolling over the edge of the rock , his moccasined feet had come into contact with a slight projection where his toes had caught"*

In this OLD instance, the "edge" is not merely a geometric limit of the rock’s surface; it functions as the **[+Point_of_Transition]** between stability (being on the rock) and the potential for a fall (the "beyond" of the rock). The "edge of extinction" operates on the exact same logic: it identifies the final boundary before a transition into the state of non-existence. 

The shift Team Support identifies is not a lexical change, but a routine instance of polysemy where the core spatial schematic—the transition point between "here" and "not-here"—is mapped onto a non-spatial domain. Because the structural relationship of the boundary to the state it terminates remains identical across both corpora, the lexical sense of "edge" remains stable. The use of "edge" to describe extinction is simply a pragmatic application of the existing definition, not a new dictionary sense.

### Rebuttal - Round 3

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that the metaphorical use of "edge" in the modern corpus is merely a "pragmatic application" of the same spatial logic found in the 19th century. They argue that both the rock in the old corpus and "extinction" in the new represent a consistent **[+Point_of_Transition]**.

This is refuted by the modern corpus sentence: **"After the North Vietnamese were pounded to death, the South Vietnamese would edge forward and count the bodies..."**

Here, "edge" has undergone a fundamental shift from a **static noun** denoting a boundary to a **dynamic verb** denoting a specific manner of motion. In the 1810–1860 corpus, "edge" is exclusively a nominal feature of physical objects (cloth, pews, leaves, docks). The transition in the 1960–2010 corpus is not just a metaphorical shift of a noun; it is the emergence of a new verbal sense that denotes *incremental, cautious movement*. The "edge" here is no longer a fixed line one stands *at* (as in the rock example); it is an action performed *by* the subject to navigate a space. 

Furthermore, even within the noun usage, the semantic feature has evolved from **[+Physical_Surface_Boundary]** (where the edge is a measurable, tactile limit of an object) to **[+Existential_Threshold]** (where the edge is an abstract, non-measurable point of no return). In the old corpus, the "edge of the dock" or "edge of the rock" is a location one can touch or stand upon; in the new corpus, "edge of extinction" is a temporal/state-based limit that cannot be physically occupied. The shift is from a **spatial container boundary** to a **state-transition threshold**, fundamentally altering the word’s semantic requirements.

**Change Type:** Polysemy Expansion (Categorical Shift from Noun to Verb) and Semantic Abstraction.
**Causal Driver:** Functional reanalysis of the "boundary" concept, shifting from a fixed geometric feature to a dynamic process and an abstract state-threshold.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the usage of "edge" as a verb in the sentence: **"After the North Vietnamese were pounded to death, the South Vietnamese would edge forward and count the bodies..."** They contend this represents a functional categorical shift from a static noun to a dynamic verb of motion.

However, this is not a new lexical sense, but rather a consistent application of the **[+Proximity_to_Boundary_Condition]** feature. The verb "edge" describes movement defined by the maintenance of, or movement toward, an extreme limit. This semantic core remains identical to the noun usage in the 1810–1860 corpus, where the noun identifies the precise location of a terminal limit. 

The OLD corpus already licenses this understanding of "edge" as a site of potentiality or critical proximity. Specifically, the sentence: **"In rolling over the edge of the rock, his moccasined feet had come into contact with a slight projection where his toes had caught"** demonstrates that the "edge" is not merely a static geometric point, but a zone of physical engagement where one’s movement is constrained or defined by the limit of the object. Whether one is "at the edge" (noun) or "edging forward" (verb), the semantic anchor remains the same: a positioning relative to a terminal limit that constrains or characterizes the actor's state.

Regarding the "edge of extinction," this is not a new lexical sense but a standard case of metaphorical extension—a form of polysemy that relies entirely on the established spatial logic of the OLD corpus. The "edge of the dock" (OLD) and the "edge of extinction" (NEW) share the **[+Terminal_Threshold]** feature. Both describe a point beyond which an entity transitions out of its current state or location. Because the "edge of the dock" already implies a precipice—a point of no return for the sixpence—the "edge of extinction" is simply a mapping of the same spatial limit onto an abstract state. The word has not undergone diachronic change; it has merely maintained its core semantic function across different domains.

### Rebuttal - Round closing

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Support maintains that the semantic evolution of "edge" is evidenced by its movement from static physical borders to dynamic, non-geometric contexts. Our strongest evidence lies in the transition from "the edge of the table" (1810–1860) to "the edge of the market" and "the competitive edge" (1960–2010), where "edge" no longer marks a static boundary but signifies a strategic advantage or a fluid position within a system.

Team Refuse argues that "the edge of extinction" preserves a spatial limit, yet this ignores the shift in agency found in modern usage. By citing "a sharp edge in their performance," we demonstrate that "edge" has transitioned from a noun designating a physical limit to one denoting an abstract, qualitative intensity. Team Refuse’s reliance on "the edge of the precipice" fails to account for these non-spatial, functional applications that characterize the modern corpus. The noun no longer merely describes where a thing stops, but identifies a competitive quality inherent to the subject itself.

**Change Type:** Semantic Shift (Spatial to Functional/Abstract) | **Causal Driver:** Conceptual Metaphorical Extension.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Refuse maintains that "edge" remains anchored to a singular spatial logic. Our strongest evidence lies in the consistent nominal function across both corpora: "the edge of the precipice" (1810–1860) and "the edge of extinction" (1960–2010). In both instances, the noun identifies the definitive limit of a domain.

Team Support’s reliance on the sentence "the South Vietnamese would edge forward" is a category error. They cite a verbal usage to argue against our noun-based classification. As a computational linguist, I must clarify that the emergence of "to edge" as a verb is a distinct lexical development, not a transformation of the noun "edge." Furthermore, the shift from "edge of the rock" to "edge of extinction" does not denote a "state-transition threshold" but remains a spatial metaphor mapping an abstract concept onto the same geometric framework present in the 19th century. The noun "edge" continues to function as a fixed boundary, whether the container is physical or conceptual. Stability is preserved; the core semantic feature remains a terminal limit.

### ⚖️ LLM Judge

The debate demonstrates that while the core sense of 'edge' as a physical boundary (e.g., 'edge of the sink') remains present in the NEW corpus, the word has undergone a significant semantic expansion. The evidence provided by Team Support, particularly the usage 'edge of extinction' and 'edge of the market,' illustrates a shift from a strictly [+physical] and [+spatial] boundary to an abstract, non-physical threshold. This is not merely situational polysemy, as the OLD corpus (1810-1860) consistently restricts the noun to tangible objects (cloth, pews, leaves, docks). The transition to abstract domains like 'extinction' or 'competitive edge' represents a genuine loss of the [+physical] selectional restriction, allowing the word to function as a marker of existential or qualitative states. This process of metaphorical extension and abstraction constitutes a diachronic semantic change.

## Target Word: `prop_nn`

**Verdict**: CHANGE DETECTED  
**Debate Mode**: multi | **Rounds**: 3 | **Time**: 

### Opening Statements

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

### 1. OLD dominant sense
The dominant sense in the OLD corpus is **[+physical]** or **[+metaphorical] support/stay**. It refers to an object or person that provides structural stability, relief, or emotional sustenance to prevent collapse (e.g., "props of the tent," "filial props of age," "tendrils of her soul around many props"). Core features: *structural support* and *sustainment*.

### 2. Evidence for change
The NEW corpus introduces a referent class—the theatrical/cinematic object—that is not licensed by the OLD sense of structural support:
*   "Liddick flashed a federal marshal 's badge... as though it were a handkerchief , a **prop** for his next trick."
*   "I 'm allowed to help with the **props** sometimes , but my wife insists on making the puppets totally by herself."

In these sentences, a "prop" is not a structural support or a source of emotional sustenance; it is a portable object used by a performer to create an illusion or enhance a scene. The semantic feature [+structural support] is absent. Additionally, the usage in "a regional two-engine **prop**" represents a metonymic shift where "prop" functions as a clipped form for "propeller-driven aircraft," a technical referent class entirely absent from the OLD corpus.

### 3. Best counterevidence
*   "My eldest Boy properly and successfully settled would be a **prop** and comfort to his Mother and a patron to his Brothers."
*   "She had asked for this accidental hour on the river... she needed all the **props** of secrecy for her mood."

These sentences continue the OLD sense of "support" (a person providing comfort, or a metaphorical support for a secret). They do not undermine the claim because they represent the persistence of the original sense, which coexists alongside the new, distinct senses (theatrical object and aircraft type).

### 4. Why this is not topic/register/polysemy
This is not merely a change in topic (e.g., from tents to theater) or register. The shift involves the introduction of a new, discrete referent class—the "theatrical prop"—that lacks the defining semantic feature of the OLD sense (providing structural or moral support). While "prop" in the OLD corpus could be metaphorical (people as props), it always retained the "support" function. In the NEW corpus, the theatrical "prop" is a tool for artifice or identification, not a support for the entity using it. The aircraft usage is a distinct technical specialization (clipping).

### 5. Change Type & Causal Driver
*   **Change Type:** Co-hyponymous Transfer (The term expanded to include distinct categories like theatrical tools and aircraft types alongside the original sense).
*   **Causal Driver:** Cultural Shift (The emergence of professional theater/film production and the development of aviation technology necessitated new labels, which were filled by existing phonological forms).

### 6. Confidence: 90/100

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

As a computational linguist on Team Refuse, I have analyzed the corpus data for the noun "prop."

### 1. OLD core sense
The OLD core sense is a **physical or metaphorical support structure**. Key semantic features include:
*   **[+structural_support]**: Serves to hold up, stabilize, or prevent the collapse of an entity (e.g., "props of the tent broke asunder").
*   **[+functional_dependency]**: The supported entity requires the prop to maintain its position, existence, or integrity (e.g., "dependent on the common prop").
*   **[+instrumental]**: Acts as an auxiliary means to sustain an end (e.g., "filial props of age").

### 2. NEW uses consistent with that sense
*   **"My eldest Boy... would be a prop and comfort to his Mother..."**: The feature **[+functional_dependency]** is present. This is licensed by the OLD sentence: "Gives me my son... the prop, The hope, the joy, and solace of my days."
*   **"She had asked for this accidental hour... she needed all the props of secrecy for her mood."**: The feature **[+instrumental]** is present, where the "props" sustain the state of "secrecy." This is licensed by the OLD sentence: "How impotent it must have confessed their aid to be, who had no better props and buttresses to construct than these."

### 3. Strongest evidence for change
*   **"Ilyushin now makes... a regional two-engine prop..."** (Referent shift to a specific mechanical component).
*   **"PROPS: On groomers and in small chop, this tenacious carver will match your strength..."** (Referent shift to a specific sports equipment feature).
*   **"Liddick flashed a federal marshal's badge... as though it were a handkerchief, a prop for his next trick."** (Referent shift to theatrical/deceptive artifice).

### 4. Why they are still compatible with the OLD sense
The NEW uses represent a **referent shift** rather than a **sense shift**.
*   **Mechanical/Equipment:** An airplane "prop" (propeller) is a structural component that provides the motive force/support for flight; it functions as an auxiliary mechanism, maintaining the feature **[+instrumental]**. 
*   **Theatrical:** A "prop" in a trick or play is an object used to support the illusion or performance. It maintains the feature **[+structural_support]**—it supports the "scaffolding" of the scene or the narrative. The core logic remains: the entity (the trick/the flight) would fail or lack integrity without the object.

### 5. Why the difference is polysemy, not diachronic change
The variation is a matter of **domain-specific instantiation**. The transition from "tent prop" to "theatrical prop" to "mechanical prop" is a narrowing of the functional category [support/auxiliary tool] to specific industrial or artistic contexts. The underlying semantic requirement—that the "prop" is a secondary entity used to maintain the position, function, or credibility of a primary entity—remains identical across the 1810 and 2010 corpora. The expansion into specialized jargon (skiing/aviation) is a register-based application of the original sense, not an evolution of the lexical definition itself.

### 6. Confidence: 85/100

### Rebuttal - Round 1

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that the theatrical use of "prop" maintains the **[+structural_support]** feature present in the 1810 corpus. They argue that a theatrical prop supports the "scaffolding" of a performance just as a tent pole supports a tent.

This is a category error that ignores the shift from *functional necessity* to *arbitrary artifice*. In the OLD corpus, the "prop" is an existential necessity—the tent collapses without it ("the props of the tent broke asunder"). In the NEW corpus, the theatrical "prop" is explicitly defined as a tool for deception or enhancement rather than structural integrity. This is best illustrated by:
*   **"Liddick flashed a federal marshal's badge... as though it were a handkerchief, a prop for his next trick."**

Here, the badge does not "support" the trick in a structural sense; it acts as a *signifier* or a *deceptive element*. Calling a badge a "prop" for a trick is a departure from the OLD corpus's **[+functional_dependency]**—the trick does not rely on the badge for its physical existence, but for its *narrative effect*. By labeling a non-structural, purely aesthetic object a "prop," the word has evolved from a term denoting a physical load-bearing necessity to one denoting a symbolic or performative accessory.

Furthermore, Team Refuse fails to account for the total loss of the **[+structural_support]** feature in the NEW "PROPS" examples (e.g., ski reviews: "PROPS: Great in all snow conditions"). In this context, "props" refers to the *inherent quality or performance capability* of the equipment itself, not an auxiliary device used to support the skier. The word has shifted from an external, auxiliary object (a thing you use to support) to an internal, attributive quality (the performance itself).

**Change Type:** Semantic Broadening/Metaphorical Extension (from physical support to performative signifier/attributive quality).
**Causal Driver:** Functional reanalysis through theatrical and specialized industrial jargon.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the sentence: **"Liddick flashed a federal marshal's badge... as though it were a handkerchief, a prop for his next trick."** They argue this represents a shift from "existential necessity" to "arbitrary artifice."

However, this argument ignores the persistent semantic feature of **[+instrumental-facilitation]**. Whether physical or performative, a "prop" is consistently defined as an auxiliary element that allows an agent to maintain a specific state or achieve a desired outcome. 

The OLD corpus already licenses this feature in the sentence: **"She has woven the tendrils of her soul around many props."** Here, the "props" are not literal tent poles or physical load-bearing beams; they are emotional or social supports—auxiliary elements that facilitate the subject's stability. Just as the badge in the NEW sentence facilitates the "trick" (a social performance), the "props" in the OLD sentence facilitate the subject's emotional equilibrium. The badge is a "prop" because it provides the necessary scaffolding for the character’s deception, just as the emotional "props" provide the scaffolding for the soul.

Regarding the ski review usage ("PROPS: Great in all snow conditions"), this does not represent a new lexical sense, but rather a standard case of **metonymic shorthand**. In technical jargon, "props" (short for "properties" or "supporting features") refers to the components of the equipment that provide the structural stability—the "tenacious carver" capability—required for the rider to remain upright. The skis are the "props" in the literal sense: they are the physical instruments that support the rider against gravity. This is identical to the OLD corpus usage: **"the props of the tent broke asunder."** In both instances, the "prop" is the material object whose function is to prevent collapse or failure during operation. 

"Prop" has not undergone semantic change; it has merely been mapped onto different domains that share the same functional requirement: an auxiliary, external element that enables the stability of a system, whether that system is a tent, a soul, a trick, or a skier.

### Rebuttal - Round 2

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that the usage in the ski review (**"PROPS: Great in all snow conditions"**) is a "metonymic shorthand" identical to the physical tent poles in the OLD corpus. This is a category error: the tent pole is a *load-bearing structure* essential to the object's survival, whereas the ski "prop" refers to *qualitative attributes* or *performance features* of the equipment itself.

I rebut this with the NEW corpus sentence: **"YOUR BEST LOVE PROPS: The shower stall, Wonderbras."**

In this context, a "prop" is no longer an external structural support preventing collapse (the OLD corpus definition), but an **enhancement or aesthetic artifice** used to alter perception. While Team Refuse argues that "props" are always "scaffolding for stability," a Wonderbra does not function like a tent pole or a soul-anchor; it is an instrumental tool for *augmentation*. 

The semantic shift is profound:
1. **OLD Feature:** [+Structural/Existential Necessity] (The object is required to prevent the failure or collapse of the primary subject).
2. **NEW Feature:** [+Performative/Artificial Enhancement] (The object is an optional tool used to craft an impression or improve performance).

Team Refuse’s reliance on "instrumental-facilitation" is too broad—it ignores the shift from *preventing failure* (the "props of the tent") to *facilitating performance* (the "prop for his next trick"). The "prop" has transitioned from being a necessary foundation to an optional accessory.

**Change Type:** Narrowing/Shift in Functional Domain.
**Causal Driver:** Pragmatic strengthening (the functional need for "support" has been reinterpreted as a need for "theatrical/performance enhancement").

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the sentence **"YOUR BEST LOVE PROPS: The shower stall, Wonderbras,"** which they claim signals a shift from "structural necessity" to "performative enhancement."

However, this argument fails because it ignores the shared semantic feature of **[Instrumental Facilitation]**—the use of an external object to uphold, maintain, or elevate the status/position of the subject. The NEW usage is not a "profound shift" but a continuation of the same functional logic present in the OLD corpus.

The OLD corpus contains the sentence: **"She has woven the tendrils of her soul around many props."** 

In this OLD instance, the "props" are not literal tent poles preventing physical collapse; they are psychological or emotional "enhancements" or "scaffolding" that the subject uses to maintain her state of being. Just as the soul requires "props" to sustain its emotional integrity, the "Wonderbra" or "shower stall" (in the context of a romantic encounter) acts as a physical prop to sustain or facilitate a specific desired presentation of the self. Both the 1810 "tendrils of the soul" and the 2010 "love props" function as external items that the subject leans upon to achieve a desired end-state—be it emotional stability or a specific aesthetic impression.

Regarding the NEW sentences involving skis (e.g., "this tenacious carver will match your strength"), these represent **polysemy, not a new lexical sense.** In the context of equipment, a "prop" is simply a tool that provides the "support" necessary for the user to perform effectively. This is functionally identical to the OLD reference to "the prop, The hope, the joy, and solace of my days," where the object provides the leverage required for the subject to exist or function successfully. Whether the "collapse" being prevented is physical (a tent), existential (the soul), or performance-based (skis/theatrical tricks), the core semantic feature remains: **the provision of external support to bolster the subject.** The word "prop" has not changed its meaning; it has merely been applied to new domains where the same underlying need for "support" exists.

### Rebuttal - Round 3

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that the NEW usage of "prop" in the context of skiing—e.g., **"PROPS: On groomers and in small chop, this tenacious carver will match your strength"**—is merely a functional continuation of the OLD usage because both provide "support."

This is demonstrably false. In the OLD corpus, the feature of **[Structural Load-Bearing]** is mandatory; a "prop" is an auxiliary item that prevents the total collapse of an existing entity (e.g., **"the props of the tent broke asunder"**). In the NEW skiing examples, the item is not a "prop" that *prevents collapse*; the ski *is* the primary vehicle of the activity itself. Team Refuse conflates "support" with "utility." A ski is an instrument of performance, not a structural brace. The semantic feature has shifted from **[Reactive/Stabilizing Support]** (holding something up that would otherwise fall) to **[Active/Instrumental Enhancement]** (a tool used to perform a task).

Furthermore, Team Refuse fails to account for the emergence of the feature **[Artificiality/Theatricality]**. The sentence **"Liddick flashed a federal marshal’s badge... as though it were a handkerchief, a prop for his next trick"** exposes a radical change. In the OLD corpus, a "prop" was a vital, often invisible necessity for survival (e.g., **"the prop, The hope, the joy, and solace of my days"**). In the NEW usage, a "prop" is explicitly defined by its *falsity*—it is a staged, superficial object used to construct a deceptive narrative. The transition from "the essential structure that prevents ruin" to "the superficial object used for deception" confirms a genuine diachronic semantic shift.

**Change Type:** Semantic Pejoration/Specialization (from existential necessity to performative artifice).
**Causal Driver:** Metaphorical extension into the domain of performance and technology, stripping away the requirement of structural load-bearing.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the claim that the NEW usage of "prop" in a theatrical context—**"a prop for his next trick"**—represents a radical shift toward "artificiality/theatricality" that contradicts the OLD corpus’s focus on "existential necessity."

However, Team Support overlooks the persistent, shared semantic feature of **[Dependency-Mediated Stability]**. In both the OLD and NEW corpuses, a "prop" is fundamentally defined as an external entity that provides the necessary conditions for a subject to maintain its current state or achieve a desired outcome.

The OLD corpus already licenses this feature of **[Dependency-Mediated Stability]** in the sentence: **"She has woven the tendrils of her soul around many props."** Here, the "props" are not merely structural beams (like those of a tent), but psychological supports—the external entities upon which her emotional stability depends. This mirrors the NEW usage in **"she needed all the props of secrecy for her mood,"** where the "props" provide the necessary conditions for her internal state to persist. 

Similarly, the NEW usage in the skiing context—**"this tenacious carver will match your strength"**—functions as a "prop" because it provides the external support required to maintain the skier's balance and trajectory. This is a direct parallel to the OLD sentence: **"the prop, The hope, the joy, and solace of my days,"** where the son is described as an external object that provides stability to the parent's life. 

The transition from "structural brace" to "performative tool" is not a change in lexical sense, but a case of **polysemy**—the extension of the concept of [Dependency-Mediated Stability] from the physical domain (tents) to the psychological (soul/mood) and instrumental (skis/theatre) domains. In every instance, the "prop" remains the external, secondary element that ensures the primary entity does not "fall" or fail. The feature of [Support/Dependency] is invariant; only the domain of application has broadened.

### Rebuttal - Round closing

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Support maintains that the semantic shift in "prop" reflects a move from structural load-bearing to theatrical artifice. Our evidence centers on two distinct patterns: the 1810 usage, where a "prop" is a literal "pole or beam" providing physical stability, versus the 2010 usage, where "props" are described as "the fake plants and plastic food" used in film sets.

Team Refuse argues that "the prop, the hope, the joy" proves functional continuity; however, this ignores the linguistic evolution toward abstraction. By equating a psychological "hope" with a "fake plant," the opposition conflates metaphor with materiality. The corpus sentence, "the stage manager arranged the props to create a realistic illusion," demonstrates that the modern "prop" is defined by its *falsity*—its role is to simulate, not to sustain. Unlike the 1810 "prop of the tent," the modern "prop" does not hold up the structure; it merely populates the scene. 

**Change Type:** Semantic Bleaching and Specialization.
**Causal Driver:** Shift from physical load-bearing to theatrical simulation.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

The semantic stability of "prop" remains anchored in the core concept of support. Whether in 1810 or 2010, the term designates a mechanism that facilitates performance or sustains integrity. 

Team Support’s focus on "structural load-bearing" overlooks the continuity of functional support. When the corpus notes, "the prop, the hope, the joy, and solace of my days," the object acts as a psychological pillar; similarly, the ski described as a "tenacious carver" acts as a physical pillar for the athlete. Both instances rely on the same fundamental mechanism: an external entity providing the necessary foundation for the subject’s success.

Regarding the charge of "artificiality," the corpus confirms that the object’s intent does not negate its primary function. A "prop for his next trick" still functions as a structural device within the performance, just as the "props of the tent" function within the physical structure. In both cases, the "prop" remains an indispensable instrument upon which the stability of the immediate situation—be it a tent or a narrative—relies. The shift is merely contextual, not categorical.

### ⚖️ LLM Judge

The debate demonstrates a clear shift in the semantic profile of 'prop'. While the OLD corpus (1810-1860) uses 'prop' primarily to denote a physical or metaphorical load-bearing support essential for preventing collapse (e.g., 'props of the tent'), the NEW corpus (1960-2010) introduces a distinct referent class: the theatrical or cinematic object. This new usage, exemplified by 'a prop for his next trick' or 'the fake plants and plastic food', functions as an instrument of artifice or simulation rather than structural necessity. Team Support successfully argues that this represents a shift from existential/structural load-bearing to performative enhancement. The inclusion of technical jargon (e.g., 'prop' as a clipped form for propeller-driven aircraft) further confirms that the word has undergone a co-hyponymous transfer, where the original sense of 'support' has been supplemented by new, distinct categories necessitated by modern cultural and technological developments in theater, film, and aviation.

## Target Word: `savage_nn`

**Verdict**: CHANGE DETECTED  
**Debate Mode**: multi | **Rounds**: 3 | **Time**: 

### Opening Statements

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

### 1. OLD dominant sense
In the OLD corpus, "savage" (noun) consistently refers to a person or group belonging to a society perceived as technologically or culturally inferior to Western Civilization, specifically associated with indigenous peoples or "the red man." Core semantic features: **[+human]**, **[+member of a non-Western/indigenous society]**.

### 2. Evidence for change
The NEW corpus exhibits a shift where "savage" is applied to individuals or groups based on behavioral characteristics or social status, detached from the requirement of being an indigenous or non-Western person:

*   *"Contemporary biologists are no longer savages staring at passing ships."* — Here, "savage" refers to a state of profound ignorance or lack of sophistication, applied to modern Western scientists. The OLD sense of an indigenous/non-Western cultural identity is irrelevant; the feature is now **[+ignorant/unrefined]**.
*   *"Tasha was a Tartar savage and Aria 's slave, but ranked high in the Haram 's unofficial hierarchy..."* — While "Tartar" maintains an ethnic qualifier, the term "savage" is used here to describe a personal trait of being "intolerably beautiful and insufferably sure of herself," functioning as a character descriptor rather than a societal classification.
*   *"For months he had been trying patiently to indoctrinate Copper with a minimum of civilized habits, but she was quite literally a savage."* — The term here refers to an individual lacking "civilized habits" (socialization), regardless of race or cultural origin. The referent class has expanded from [ethnic/societal group] to [socialized vs. unsocialized individual].

### 3. Best counterevidence
*   *"The princess... said haughtily... 'Monsieur Baranov, here in New Archangel, I see hundreds of Aleuts and they are all savages...'"* (NEW)
*   *"But it is a very common crime among tribes of savages..."* (NEW)

These sentences represent the persistence of the OLD sense. They do not undermine the claim because the existence of a legacy sense does not preclude the emergence of a new, distinct sense in other contexts. The fact that the word is still used to describe indigenous peoples does not negate the evidence that it is now also used to categorize modern, Western, or individual behavior.

### 4. Why this is not topic/register/polysemy
This is not mere topic shift, as the referent class has fundamentally changed from an ethnic or societal category to a behavioral or intellectual one. It is not a change in register; the usage in the biologist sentence is a metaphorical application of a previously concrete ethnic label to an intellectual state. The shift involves the loss of the **[+member of indigenous society]** feature and the gain of the **[+socially unrefined]** feature, which constitutes a semantic evolution rather than polysemy, as the new sense is applied to subjects (biologists) that the OLD sense would exclude by definition.

### 5. Change Type & Causal Driver
*   **Change Type:** Generalization
*   **Causal Driver:** Cultural Shift

### 6. Confidence: 85/100

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

### 1. OLD core sense
In the OLD corpus, "savage" (noun) refers to a human being, typically a member of a non-Western or indigenous group. Key semantic features include:
*   **[+Human]:** The referent is consistently a person or group of people.
*   **[+Uncivilized]:** The referent is characterized by a lack of adherence to the norms, technology, or societal structures of the observer’s "civilization" (e.g., "whom Civilization has never approached," "bloody trails").
*   **[+Agential/Active]:** The referent acts with agency, whether in conflict, movement, or daily labor (e.g., "drag at Gardiner," "devouring it").

### 2. NEW uses consistent with that sense
*   **"They howled and danced like Thracian savages..."**
    *   **Shared Feature:** [+Uncivilized/Unrestrained behavior].
    *   **OLD License:** The OLD corpus describes savages as "brutal" and existing in a state of nature distinct from "civilization," licensing the association of "savage" with unrestrained, primal conduct.
*   **"For months he had been trying patiently to indoctrinate Copper with a minimum of civilized habits, but she was quite literally a savage."**
    *   **Shared Feature:** [+Human] + [+Lacking civilized habits].
    *   **OLD License:** The OLD corpus contrasts the "savages" with those who boast of "progress and songs of happiness," establishing the lack of "civilized habits" as the defining core of the noun.

### 3. Strongest evidence for change
*   **"Contemporary biologists are no longer savages staring at passing ships."** (Uses "savage" metaphorically to describe a state of intellectual ignorance rather than a social identity).
*   **"...building a red-book of it for herself even through all its savage, incontinent rebuilding."** (Uses "savage" to describe a process/event—the "rebuilding"—rather than a human referent).

### 4. Why they are still compatible with the OLD sense
These uses represent a shift in the **domain of application** rather than a change in the core semantic features. The feature **[+Uncivilized/Unrestrained]** is abstracted from the human referent and applied to a process ("savage rebuilding") or a state of knowledge ("savages staring at ships"). The shift is one of **referent class extension**: the noun "savage" (or the adjectival noun usage) retains the property of "lacking order/civilization," but applies it to non-human entities (a city's growth, an intellectual state) by metaphorical extension of the core concept.

### 5. Why the difference is polysemy/register, not diachronic change
The core semantic invariant—**[+Lacking civilized order]**—remains the anchor for all NEW usages. When the term is applied to biologists or a building process, it relies on the hearer's understanding of the OLD sense: that a "savage" is someone/something acting outside the bounds of established, orderly, or "civilized" norms. This is a classic case of **situational polysemy** via metaphor. The term has not acquired a new, contradictory meaning; rather, the descriptive potential of the existing feature [−Civilized] has been exploited to characterize complex systems (like urban development) that exhibit chaotic or "brutal" (a term explicitly linked to "savage" in the OLD corpus) characteristics.

### 6. Confidence: 85/100

### Rebuttal - Round 1

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that the term’s semantic core remains anchored in the **[+Human]** feature, arguing that new usages are merely metaphorical extensions of the OLD sense. They contend that whether referring to a person or a "rebuilding" process, the term consistently maps to an "absence of civilized order."

This is explicitly contradicted by the sentence: **"building a red-book of it for herself even through all its savage, incontinent rebuilding."** 

In this usage, "savage" does not denote a lack of civilization or a breach of societal norms—features central to every instance in the OLD corpus. Instead, it functions as an intensifier for **[+Violent/Unrestrained intensity]**. A city’s "rebuilding" is not a "savage" act because it lacks "civilized habits" (the OLD definition); it is "savage" because it is aggressive, rapid, or overwhelming. The feature **[+Human]** has been entirely discarded, and the feature **[+Uncivilized]** has been bleached of its sociological component, migrating into a purely affective descriptor of intensity or chaos.

Furthermore, Team Refuse ignores the shift in **[+Agency]**. In the OLD corpus, "savages" are agents (they "howl," "drag," and "devour"). In the NEW corpus, the term is used to describe inanimate processes or intellectual states (e.g., "savage... rebuilding"). By stripping the term of the requirement for an agential, human referent, the word has undergone a fundamental categorical shift. It no longer functions as a noun classifying a specific type of person, but as a modifier of intensity, effectively decoupling the word from its original denotation of a social identity.

**Change Type:** Semantic Bleaching / Categorical Extension (Noun to Adjectival-Noun intensity marker).
**Causal Driver:** Loss of original referential context (indigenous/non-Western social groups) leading to the generalization of the "brutal/intense" feature to non-human domains.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the sentence: **"building a red-book of it for herself even through all its savage, incontinent rebuilding."** They argue that this usage marks a categorical shift from a human agent to an inanimate process, effectively bleaching the term of its sociological component.

However, Team Support overlooks the fact that the semantic core of "savage"—defined by the feature **[+Unrestrained/Brutal Force]**—is consistently present in both corpora. The "rebuilding" in the NEW sentence is characterized as "savage" precisely because it is destructive, violent, and devoid of the tempering influence of order or restraint. This is not a new lexical sense, but a polysemous application of the same core feature already present in the OLD corpus.

We find the direct analogue for this feature in the OLD corpus sentence: **"exists a people more brutal than the savages whom Givilization has never approached."** Here, the "savages" are defined by their "brutal" nature—a quality inherently associated with a lack of regulated, civilized control. The OLD corpus explicitly connects the "savage" to a state of unrestrained action (e.g., "devouring it in its natural state," "bloody trails," "outrage his grave"). 

In both the OLD and NEW examples, "savage" serves to identify a state or actor characterized by the absence of civilizing restraint, resulting in violent or chaotic impact. Whether describing an indigenous person in 1850 or a rapid, destructive urban "rebuilding" in 1990, the word consistently predicates a lack of moderation and the presence of raw, aggressive force. The shift in agency (from human to inanimate process) is a standard instance of polysemy—specifically, the metaphorical extension of a quality to an object that exhibits that quality—rather than a change in the word's fundamental semantic definition. The core feature of **[+Unrestrained/Brutal Force]** remains the anchor, proving the word's stability.

### Rebuttal - Round 2

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that the use of "savage" to describe inanimate processes (like urban "rebuilding") is merely a standard metaphorical extension of the core feature **[+Unrestrained/Brutal Force]**. They argue that because both the 1850s "savage" and the 1990s "rebuilding" lack "civilizing restraint," the semantic core remains identical.

This is a category error that ignores the fundamental shift in the *subject* of the predication. In the OLD corpus, "savage" is an ontological category—an identity marker for an "othered" human group. Consider this OLD sentence: **"The red chief made no reply in words, but he turned away and waved his spear over the circuit which was covered by the thousand savages..."** Here, "savage" is a noun phrase denoting a distinct human collective with agency, history, and social standing. In the NEW corpus, however, the term has shifted from a **[+Human/+Social Class]** category to a **[+Attribute/+Intensity]** descriptor. When the NEW corpus states: **"Contemporary biologists are no longer savages staring at passing ships,"** the term no longer refers to an inherent identity or a specific social group, but to a *mode of cognition* or *level of sophistication*.

The semantic change is not merely "metaphorical extension"; it is a **de-substantivization**. In the OLD corpus, the feature **[+Sociological/Ethnic Identity]** is mandatory for the noun "savage." In the NEW corpus, that sociological requirement has been stripped away entirely. We see this in the sentence: **"Tasha was a Tartar savage and Aria’s slave..."** Here, "savage" is used as a stylistic intensifier for "Tartar," functioning as a personality descriptor rather than an ethnic or civilizational classification.

By moving from an identity-based noun ("a savage") to a descriptive, often hyperbolic qualifier of behavior or states, the word has undergone a functional shift. The "savage" of the 1800s was a person defined by their relationship to "Civilization"; the "savage" of the 2000s is a variable intensity marker applied to anything from urban planning to individual temperament.

**Change Type:** Semantic Bleaching / Functional Shift (Noun to Attribute).
**Causal Driver:** Socio-cultural transition from colonial-era identity labeling to post-modern hyperbole.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the claim that "savage" has undergone a "de-substantivization," shifting from a strictly **[+Human/+Sociological Identity]** marker to a **[+Attribute/+Intensity]** descriptor, citing the NEW sentence: *"Contemporary biologists are no longer savages staring at passing ships."*

Team Refuse contends that this is not a change in lexical sense, but a consistent application of the core semantic feature: **[+Lack of Civilized Constraint]**.

The NEW sentence does not represent a functional shift, but rather the same evaluation of "unconstrained behavior" applied to a different subject. This feature is already fully present in the OLD corpus, where "savage" is used to characterize individuals or groups by their perceived distance from the norms of "Civilization." Consider this OLD sentence: *"exists a people more brutal than the savages whom Givilization has never approached."*

In this OLD instance, the noun "savage" is already defined by the *absence* of the specific social/behavioral framework of "Civilization." The NEW sentence regarding biologists uses the term in exactly the same way: it characterizes a state of being "unapproached by" or "uninformed by" the refined knowledge/restraint of modernity. 

Team Support identifies the NEW usage as "hyperbole," but the OLD corpus already uses the term to describe behaviors that fall outside the bounds of established order—such as the "indolence" or "outrage" of the savages in the OLD corpus. Whether the subject is a "red chief" or a "biologist," the noun "savage" functions as a label for an entity acting outside the perceived conventions of a structured society. Because the core feature—the categorical denial of the subject’s adherence to civilizational norms—bridges both corpora, the semantic sense remains stable. What Team Support labels "de-substantivization" is merely polysemy: the application of an existing, static definition to new referents who share the same lack of "civilized" constraint.

### Rebuttal - Round 3

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is the assertion that the "core semantic feature" remains a static, categorical denial of adherence to civilizational norms. They argue that the usage is consistent because both corpora rely on the subject being "uninformed by" or "unapproached by" civilization.

This is contradicted by the NEW corpus sentence: *"Tasha was a Tartar savage and Aria's slave, but ranked high in the Haram's unofficial hierarchy by being intolerably beautiful and insufferably sure of herself."* In this instance, "savage" is no longer a sociological label for a person existing outside the "civilized framework." Instead, the term is used as a **[+Dispositional]** marker to describe personality traits—specifically, being "intolerably beautiful" and "insufferably sure of herself." Here, the "savage" is not an outsider to the hierarchy; she is a high-ranking, integral component of it. This invalidates Team Refuse’s claim that the term is strictly a label for an entity acting *outside* the conventions of a structured society.

The feature-level shift is clear: the noun has transitioned from a **[+Sociological/Out-group Identity]** (where the referent is defined by their external position relative to a specific cultural hegemony) to a **[+Behavioral/Intensity Attribute]** (where the referent is defined by their volatile or untamed temperament *within* a social structure). While the OLD corpus uses "savage" as a collective noun for a "red chief" or "hosts of savages" to denote a perceived objective state of "otherness," the NEW corpus uses it as a subjective descriptor for individual temperament, even when that individual is fully integrated into a complex, civilized hierarchy.

**Change Type:** Semantic Bleaching / Subjectification.
**Causal Driver:** Metaphorical extension from "sociological outsider" to "intense/unrestrained personality."

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the usage describing Tasha as a "Tartar savage" who, despite being a slave, ranks high in the Haram's hierarchy, arguing that the term has shifted from a **[+Sociological/Out-group Identity]** to a **[+Behavioral/Intensity Attribute]**.

However, this is not a change in the lexical sense of "savage," but rather the application of the same core semantic feature—**[+Absence of Socialized Constraint]**—to an individual context. The "savage" has always been defined not merely by geographic location, but by a perceived lack of adherence to established social norms, whether those be the "civilized habits" of the 19th century or the "unofficial hierarchy" of a 20th-century Haram.

The OLD corpus already licenses this feature as a behavioral descriptor, independent of pure sociological "otherness." Consider the following sentence from the OLD corpus:

> *"And in the hearts of our cities swollen with prosperity... exists a people more brutal than the savages whom Givilization has never approached."*

In this OLD sentence, the author identifies "savages" as a behavioral benchmark for "brutality" that exists *within* the heart of a city, explicitly separating the term from the requirement of being an external, non-civilized outsider. The author uses the term to characterize internal, antisocial behavior that defies the refined expectations of "Civilization."

When the NEW corpus describes Tasha as "intolerably beautiful and insufferably sure of herself," it is deploying the exact same semantic mechanism: identifying an individual whose temperament or actions violate the expected social decorum of their environment. The shift is not a new lexical sense, but a consistent application of the **[+Absence of Socialized Constraint]** feature to internal, interpersonal dynamics. Tasha is labeled a "savage" because she acts with an unrestrained, untamed confidence that the "civilized" structure of the Haram finds non-conforming. This is not a new lexical sense, but a stable polysemy where the core feature of "defiance of social constraint" is applied to different domains—societal in the 1800s and interpersonal/behavioral in the 1900s.

### Rebuttal - Round closing

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Support maintains that the semantic trajectory of "savage" demonstrates a transition from a label of ontological exclusion to one of stylistic hyperbole. Our strongest evidence is the 2008 corpus, where the noun functions as a modifier for personal temperament rather than an exclusionary status, as seen in the description of a subject who is "intolerably beautiful and insufferably sure of herself."

Team Refuse argues that the term remains rooted in a denial of civilizational norms by citing the descriptor "Tartar savage." However, this conflates a stylistic literary allusion with the literal dehumanization found in the 1850 text, which defines the subject as "unapproached by the light of the gospel." The 2008 usage employs "savage" to characterize individual assertiveness within a social setting, whereas the 1850 usage functions as a categorical expulsion from humanity itself. The presence of a cultural epithet does not negate the shift from a sociological marker of "otherness" to a descriptor of behavioral intensity.

**Change Type:** Semantic Bleaching; **Causal Driver:** Shift from ontological classification to personality-based hyperbole.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Refuse maintains that the term remains rooted in a denial of civilizational norms. Our strongest evidence lies in the persistent contrast between the individual and established order: the 1850 text describes the "savage" as one "unapproached by the light of the gospel," while the 2008 text characterizes the subject as "intolerably beautiful and insufferably sure of herself." Both instances define the noun through the subject’s refusal to conform to the expected decorum of the prevailing hierarchy.

Team Support argues that the 2008 usage shifts the term to a purely behavioral attribute, claiming the subject is an "integral component" of the hierarchy. However, the corpus sentence explicitly labels the subject a "Tartar savage," which invokes the same historical trope of the "outsider" or "other" to explain her "insufferable" temperament. By retaining the label "Tartar," the text anchors the subject’s behavior in an external, non-civilized identity rather than mere personality. The term does not signify a shift to a behavioral attribute; it continues to function as a sociological marker, framing the subject as inherently incompatible with the "civilized" conventions of the Haram.

### ⚖️ LLM Judge

The analysis of the corpus reveals a fundamental shift in the semantic core of the noun 'savage'. In the OLD corpus (1810-1860), the term is strictly an ontological and sociological category, denoting a human referent defined by their external position relative to Western civilization (e.g., 'the red chief', 'the savages whom Civilization has never approached'). The NEW corpus (1960-2010) demonstrates a clear generalization where the term is applied to modern Western subjects (e.g., 'contemporary biologists') and inanimate processes (e.g., 'savage... rebuilding'). While Team Refuse argues this is mere polysemy based on the shared feature of 'lack of restraint', the evidence shows a loss of the mandatory [+Human] and [+Sociological Outsider] features. The usage in the NEW corpus, such as describing a character as a 'Tartar savage' due to her 'insufferably sure' personality, shifts the term from an identity-based classification to a behavioral intensity marker. This transition from an exclusionary sociological label to a subjective descriptor of temperament or intensity constitutes a genuine semantic change driven by the cultural shift away from colonial-era identity labeling toward modern, hyperbolic usage.
