## Target Word: `head_nn`

**Verdict**: STABLE  
**Debate Mode**: multi | **Rounds**: 3 | **Time**: 

### Opening Statements

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

### 1. OLD dominant sense
The dominant sense of "head" in the OLD corpus is **the anatomical structure** located at the top of the human or animal body, characterized by the features [+biological], [+anatomical], and [+superior/terminal position]. Even when used metaphorically (e.g., "head of the table"), it refers to the position corresponding to the anatomical head of a person.

### 2. Evidence for change
The NEW corpus introduces a non-biological, inanimate referent class:
*   **"The finished tripod head consists of four layers of plywood."**
*   **"Talbott was instrumental in the rehabilitation of Martha Morrison, now head of the institute's adolescent unit..."**

In the OLD corpus, "head" is strictly biological or a spatial metaphor for the top of an object. In the NEW sentence regarding the tripod, "head" refers to a specific mechanical component or mounting apparatus, which lacks the feature [+biological] and does not necessarily occupy a "top" position in a biological sense. Furthermore, the use of "head" to denote a professional role (e.g., "head of the institute") in the NEW corpus has shifted from a spatial metaphor (at the head of the table) to a formal title denoting an organizational leader, independent of the entity's physical shape or arrangement.

### 3. Best counterevidence
*   **"The next head which the churches received from the Union was a keen Roman Catholic..." (OLD)**
*   **"Once and sometimes twice a week Mrs. Jenkins wanted me to chop the head off a fryer..." (NEW)**

These sentences do not undermine the claim because they represent the original biological/spatial sense. The fact that the OLD sense persists in the NEW corpus (as seen in the fryer example) is expected in diachronic change, as semantic expansion typically involves the coexistence of older and newer senses rather than immediate replacement.

### 4. Why this is not topic/register/polysemy
This is not merely polysemy or a change in topic because the "tripod head" usage introduces a new referent class that cannot be derived from the biological anatomy or the "top-position" metaphor found in the OLD corpus. The mechanical "head" of a tripod is a functional category, not a spatial one. Similarly, the organizational "head" has evolved into a distinct lexicalized role that functions independently of the "head of the table" spatial metaphor, as it no longer requires the entity to be arranged in a linear or seated fashion.

### 5. Change Type & Causal Driver
*   **Change Type:** Generalization
*   **Causal Driver:** Cultural Shift (The shift reflects the industrialization and institutionalization of society, where anatomical terms are mapped onto mechanical parts and hierarchical organizational roles.)

### 6. Confidence: 85/100

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

As a computational linguist on Team Refuse, I have analyzed the corpus data for the noun "head."

### 1. OLD Core Sense
Based on the provided OLD sentences, the core sense of "head" is: **[+physical]**, **[+anatomical]**, and **[+superior/apical position]**.
*   **[+anatomical]**: "would split your skull... wears an iron pot on his head"
*   **[+superior/apical position]**: "the title stands at the head of our article"; "gentleman at the head of the table"

### 2. NEW Uses Consistent with that Sense
*   **"Trotting back to the court, Bengt shook his head."**
    *   **Shared Feature:** [+anatomical].
    *   **Old License:** "my poor head aches and throbs so badly" (confirms the biological entity).
*   **"His wife was as thin as he was chubby, with a head of shimmering blond hair..."**
    *   **Shared Feature:** [+apical position/location].
    *   **Old License:** "There was a placard over his head" (both denote the uppermost part of a human figure).
*   **"But the severed head continued to scream..."**
    *   **Shared Feature:** [+anatomical].
    *   **Old License:** "raising her head from the attitude of profound attention" (refers to the physical body part).

### 3. Strongest Evidence for Change
*   **"The finished tripod head consists of four layers of plywood."**
*   **"Talbott was instrumental in the rehabilitation of Martha Morrison, now head of the institute's adolescent unit..."**

### 4. Why They Are Still Compatible
These uses represent a **referent shift** rather than a **sense shift**.
*   **Tripod head:** This retains the **[+apical position]** feature. Just as the human head is the apical/superior component of the body, the tripod head is the apical component of the tripod structure. It is a metaphorical extension based on physical structure, not a change in the word's inherent semantic feature of "superior component."
*   **Head of the institute:** This retains the **[+superior/apical position]** feature. The "head" of an organization is the person in the highest/apical position of the hierarchy, mirroring the "gentleman at the head of the table" from the OLD corpus.

### 5. Why the Difference is Polysemy, Not Diachronic Change
The shift from a biological entity to an organizational leader or a mechanical component is a classic example of **situational polysemy**. The core semantic scaffolding—a distinct, superior, or apical point of a larger system—is present in all OLD and NEW instances. The shift in referent (from flesh-and-blood to plywood or institutional roles) does not alter the underlying semantic constraint of the word, which consistently maps to the "top" or "controlling" element of a bounded whole. 

### 6. Confidence: 90/100

### Rebuttal - Round 1

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that the organizational use of "head" (e.g., "head of the institute") is a stable, continuous extension of the "gentleman at the head of the table." This argument relies on the assumption that "head" remains a spatial descriptor in both contexts.

I refute this with the following sentence from the OLD corpus: **"The next head which the churches received from the Union was a keen Roman Catholic..."**

Team Refuse argues that "head" in the NEW corpus ("head of the institute's adolescent unit") denotes the "apical position" of a hierarchy. However, the OLD corpus sentence above demonstrates that "head" was already being used as a **nominalized agent** (a person/entity acting as a leader). Crucially, the semantic shift in the NEW corpus is not merely to "leader," but to a **functional role defined by abstract system management** rather than spatial proximity. 

In the OLD corpus, "head" is tethered to a physical locus (the table, the article, the body). In the NEW corpus, the "head of the institute" is a role detached from any literal "head-of-the-table" spatiality; it is a feature of **administrative hierarchy**. Furthermore, Team Refuse fails to account for the "tripod head," which abandons the **[+biological]** feature entirely in favor of a **[+mechanical]** feature. While they claim this is just "apical position," the feature has shifted from *organic/human anatomy* to *modular componentry*. We are no longer describing a "top part"; we are describing a "functional attachment" or "control unit."

The change is not mere polysemy; it is the **de-biologization** of the term. The core feature has migrated from a **[+anatomical]** necessity to a **[+functional/instrumental]** requirement, where the "head" now defines the utility of the object rather than its location.

**Change Type:** Semantic Bleaching / Functional Extension
**Causal Driver:** De-biologization (the shift from physical anatomy to abstract or mechanical utility)

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the assertion that the "head of the institute" represents a transition from a spatial locus to an abstract administrative role, and that the "tripod head" represents a "de-biologization" of the term.

I argue that both instances remain stable because they are anchored by the shared semantic feature of **[+Primary Terminal Orientation]**. This feature defines "head" not by biological anatomy, but by its status as the **leading or functional apex of a structure**.

The OLD corpus already licenses this feature, demonstrating that "head" was never strictly tethered to anatomy or literal "heads of tables." The sentence, **"The next head which the churches received from the Union was a keen Roman Catholic,"** confirms that even in the 1800s, "head" was used to identify the primary, controlling entity of an organization regardless of its physical position. Because the "head of the church" functioned as an administrative leader in the OLD corpus, the "head of the institute" in the NEW corpus is not a new lexical sense, but a consistent application of the same hierarchical mapping. 

Similarly, the "tripod head" is not a "de-biologization," but a functional extension of the **[+Primary Terminal Orientation]** feature. Just as the "head of the article" is the terminal point that organizes the text, the "tripod head" is the terminal component that organizes the tripod's utility. 

The appearance of these usages in the NEW corpus does not constitute a semantic shift; it is merely **polysemy**—the application of a stable, underlying conceptual framework (the "head" as the apical, controlling, or terminal part of a system) to new domains. Whether the system is a church, an institute, a table, or a tripod, the semantic feature of being the "primary terminal point" remains the constant, defining operator.

### Rebuttal - Round 2

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that the "tripod head" is merely a consistent application of the **[+Primary Terminal Orientation]** feature, equating the structural apex of a device with the organizational "head" of a church. 

This is refuted by the corpus sentence: **"The finished tripod head consists of four layers of plywood."** 

In the OLD corpus, "head" (even in metaphorical senses like "head of the table" or "head of the church") consistently denotes a **[+Agentive Controller]** or a **[+Topological Extremity]**. These usages imply a sentient or singular point of command/location. By contrast, the "tripod head" in the NEW corpus refers to a **[+Material Component]**—a physical, multi-layered assembly that is neither an agent nor a singular apex, but an integrated modular part of a larger mechanism. The transition from "head as a singular, apical locus" to "head as a compound material component" represents a genuine shift in the word's semantic architecture; the feature of "singularity" has been eroded by the "componentization" of the term in technical discourse.

Furthermore, Team Refuse fails to address the "de-biologization" of the noun. In the OLD corpus, even non-literal usages (e.g., "head of the article") maintain a metaphorical link to the human body—the part that leads or thinks. In the NEW corpus, the "tripod head" has been stripped of this anthropomorphic mapping entirely; it is a technical designation for a sub-assembly, not a metaphor for a body part. This move from **[+Anthropomorphic Metaphor]** to **[+Technical/Functional Modular]** signifies a fundamental change in the word's categorical status.

**Change Type:** Semantic Bleaching / Functional Specialization.
**Causal Driver:** Technical/Industrial Re-classification.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the sentence: **"The finished tripod head consists of four layers of plywood."** They argue that this represents a shift from "apical locus" to "material component," claiming that the term has been stripped of its anthropomorphic and singular nature.

However, this argument ignores the persistent semantic feature of **[+Primary Structural Orientation]**. In both corpora, "head" designates the functional, upper-most, or attachment-point segment that dictates the orientation or utility of the object to which it is affixed. This is not a new lexical sense, but a consistent application of "head" as the leading or controlling terminus of a structure.

This feature is already fully licensed in the OLD corpus by the sentence: **"the title stands at the head of our article."** Just as the "title" is not a sentient controller or a biological entity, yet serves as the functional orientation point for the article's structure, the "tripod head" serves as the functional orientation point for the camera mechanism. 

Regarding the claim of "de-biologization," the transition to technical discourse does not constitute a new lexical sense, but rather **polysemy**—a standard extension of a core spatial logic into specialized domains. The "tripod head" is not a departure from the word’s architecture; it is the realization of the same spatial hierarchy (the part that directs or supports the whole) applied to inanimate engineering. Because the "head of the table" and the "head of the article" in the OLD corpus already function as structural, non-biological endpoints, the "tripod head" is merely a continuation of this established, abstract spatial category.

### Rebuttal - Round 3

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that the "tripod head" is merely a continuation of the "head of the article" because both serve as abstract, non-biological endpoints. This ignores a critical shift in the **[+Agency/Control]** feature that defines the word's evolution.

Team Refuse argues for continuity, yet the OLD corpus demonstrates that even abstract uses of "head" were deeply rooted in a *governing* or *directing* logic. Consider this OLD sentence: **"The next head which the churches received from the Union was a keen Roman Catholic."** Here, the "head" is an agent—a person exercising authority. Even in the abstract "head of the table" or "head of the article," the term functions as a synecdoche for a singular, controlling entity. 

In the NEW corpus, this requirement for agency or singular directionality has been severed. Look at this NEW sentence: **"The finished tripod head consists of four layers of plywood."** This is not a "head" because it leads, decides, or occupies an apical position of authority; it is a "head" because it is a **[+Material Component]**—a modular, detachable assembly. 

The semantic change is not mere polysemy; it is the **re-categorization of the word from a relational/hierarchical noun to a structural/component noun.** In the OLD corpus, a "head" was the *locus of a subject's presence* (even metaphorically). In the NEW corpus, "head" has been stripped of this relational, subject-centered architecture and repurposed as a label for a physical *part* of an inanimate assembly. The shift from "the head of the Union" (a person) to "the head of a tripod" (a slab of wood) represents a fundamental erosion of the "subject-as-origin" feature that governed the word for centuries.

**Change Type:** Semantic Bleaching (loss of agentive/hierarchical features).
**Causal Driver:** Functional re-analysis in technical/industrial discourse.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence for a shift is the NEW sentence: **"The finished tripod head consists of four layers of plywood."** They argue this represents a departure from the "subject-as-origin" or agentive authority seen in OLD usages like "the next head which the churches received."

However, this argument overlooks the persistent semantic feature of **[+Apical/Terminal Positioning]**. Both the "head" of the Union and the "head" of a tripod function as the distinct, uppermost, or terminating point of a larger, coherent system. In both instances, the "head" is defined by its location at the apex of a structure, whether that structure is a human hierarchy or a mechanical apparatus.

This feature is already explicitly licensed in the OLD corpus: **"the title stands at the head of our article."** 

In this OLD sentence, the "head" is not a person, nor is it an agent exercising "subject-as-origin" authority; it is a structural location—the starting point or top-most portion of a document. If "head" can describe the top of an article in the 1800s without requiring agency, then the "tripod head" is not a new lexical sense, but a consistent application of the same spatial logic. 

The NEW usage represents classic polysemy: the word retains its core functional identity as a terminal coordinate ("the top part of X"). The fact that the target has shifted from a human hierarchy to a mechanical assembly does not constitute a "re-categorization" of the word; it merely reflects the word’s established capacity to map the concept of an "apical position" onto any system, whether abstract, social, or physical. The "subject-as-origin" was never a requirement for the word "head," only a specific application of its broader spatial definition.

### Rebuttal - Round closing

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Support maintains that "head" has undergone a semantic shift from an organic, hierarchical leader to a mechanical, modular component. Our strongest evidence for this transition is the contrast between the 1810–1860 usage, **"The next head which the churches received from the Union was a keen Roman Catholic,"** where "head" denotes an animate agent exercising authority, and the 1960–2010 usage, **"the finished tripod head,"** where the term denotes a static, detachable part.

Team Refuse argues that the spatial logic of an "apical point" creates stability. However, they conflate physical position with functional agency. While the 1810–1860 corpus uses "head" to describe a person capable of action, the modern corpus restricts the term to inanimate, subservient hardware. The 1960–2010 evidence of **"the finished tripod head"** confirms that the term no longer signifies the source of organizational power, but rather a functional segment in a modular assembly. The semantic core has shifted from biological authority to mechanical utility.

**Change Type:** Semantic Narrowing and Reification.
**Causal Driver:** Technological advancement and the professionalization of mechanical design.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Refuse maintains that the semantic core of "head" remains stable as a fixed termination point. Our strongest evidence lies in the structural continuity between early and late corpus usage: both "the head of the article" (1810–1860) and "the finished tripod head" (1960–2010) denote an apical, terminal point within an assembly. In both instances, the term functions as the physical extremity of an object, regardless of whether that object is a text or a tool.

Team Support claims that the "tripod head" represents a shift toward structural modularity, citing the loss of agency. However, this ignores that "head" has long functioned as a non-biological descriptor of position. When the corpus states, **"The next head which the churches received from the Union was a keen Roman Catholic,"** the term already functions as a label for a specific, discrete position within a larger organizational structure. The tripod head similarly occupies a discrete, apical position within its own assembly. The transition is not a "re-categorization," but a consistent application of the "top-most" feature to increasingly diverse physical forms. Stability persists because the spatial logic remains unchanged.

### ⚖️ LLM Judge

The debate centers on whether the use of 'head' to refer to mechanical components (e.g., 'tripod head') or administrative roles (e.g., 'head of the institute') constitutes a semantic shift from its historical anatomical and spatial senses. Team Refuse successfully demonstrates that the core semantic feature of 'head'—denoting the apical, terminal, or primary controlling part of a bounded whole—is present in both the OLD and NEW corpora. The OLD corpus already contains non-biological, non-sentient uses such as 'the title stands at the head of our article,' which establishes that the word was not strictly tethered to biological anatomy or human agency even in the 19th century. The shift to 'tripod head' or 'head of the institute' represents a productive application of this established spatial and hierarchical logic to new domains (polysemy), rather than a fundamental change in the word's core semantic features. Because the 'apical/terminal' feature is consistently applied across both periods, no genuine diachronic semantic change has occurred.

## Target Word: `quilt_nn`

**Verdict**: STABLE  
**Debate Mode**: multi | **Rounds**: 3 | **Time**: 

### Opening Statements

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

1. **OLD dominant sense**
The dominant sense in the OLD corpus is a **bed-covering or padded textile layer** used for warmth or concealment, characterized by the features [+bedding] and [+padded/layered]. It is consistently used in the context of sleeping, beds, or physical draping over bodies or furniture.

2. **Evidence for change**
*   *“The NAMES Project AIDS Memorial Quilt display”*
*   *“Their quilt, 'Power of Houston' was entered in the International Quilt Association's annual judged show 'Quilts: A World of Beauty,' and was one of 350 finalists in the competition...”*
*   *“The two big males, trailing their tail feathers like quilts tied around their necks...”*

The OLD sense (a functional object for warmth/bedding) fails to explain these. In the first two examples, the "quilt" functions as a **commemorative, symbolic, or aesthetic art object** intended for public display rather than physical warmth or bedding. In the final example, the word is used as a **simile for biological plumage** based on visual appearance (draping/trailing) rather than the object's function or construction. The referent class has expanded from functional household bedding to include abstract memorials and visual descriptors.

3. **Best counterevidence**
*   *“She heard him yell for George and then he was placing her in the bunk and drawing quilts over her.”* (NEW)
*   *“...a huge bedroom with a four-poster bed covered with an antique quilt...”* (NEW)

These sentences maintain the OLD sense of bedding. They do not undermine the claim because semantic change does not require the total extinction of the original sense, but rather the emergence of new, distinct referent classes (memorials/art) that the original definition cannot encompass.

4. **Why this is not topic/register/polysemy**
This is not a change in topic (e.g., from beds to art galleries) because the definition of the object itself has shifted. The "AIDS Memorial Quilt" is not a piece of bedding; it is a collaborative social project. The transition reflects a shift in the *denotation* of the word from a specific physical functional object (bedding) to a broader category of textile-based commemorative or artistic creation.

5. **Change Type & Causal Driver**
*   **Change Type:** Generalization
*   **Causal Driver:** Cultural Shift

6. **Confidence: 85/100**

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

### Arg_stable: Analysis of "quilt"

**1. OLD core sense**
The OLD corpus identifies "quilt" as a noun referring to a textile object with the following semantic features:
*   **[+Physical/Tangible]:** Composed of fabric/material (calico, cotton batting, spool-thread).
*   **[+Functionally-Covering]:** Used to cover people or objects (concealing a face, covering a baby in a cradle, worn as a shawl).
*   **[+Constructed/Stitched]:** Created through a process of layering, marking, and stitching (quilting frames, patchwork).

**2. NEW uses consistent with that sense**
*   *“She heard him yell for George and then he was placing her in the bunk and drawing quilts over her.”*
    *   **Shared Feature:** [+Functionally-Covering].
    *   **OLD License:** "And yet she is afraid to cross the narrow room, and lift the quilt which conceals the face of..."
*   *“She laid across her bed on the wedding-ring quilt my grandmama Sugar gave her...”*
    *   **Shared Feature:** [+Physical/Tangible] and [+Constructed/Stitched].
    *   **OLD License:** "Not only did we select calico for the border of the quilt, together with cotton batting and spool-thread..."

**3. Strongest evidence for change**
*   *“The NAMES Project AIDS Memorial Quilt display...”*
*   *“The two big males, trailing their tail feathers like quilts tied around their necks...”*

**4. Why they are still compatible with the OLD sense**
The "AIDS Memorial Quilt" is a referent shift, not a sense shift. While the *purpose* evolved from purely thermal/domestic utility to commemorative/symbolic display, the object retains the feature **[+Constructed/Stitched]** and **[+Physical/Tangible]**. It is still a textile assembly of layered fabric squares.
Regarding the peahen: The use of "quilts" here is a metaphorical comparison (simile). The speaker attributes the **[+Physical/Tangible]** and **[+Draped/Covering]** features to the tail feathers. This is a classic figurative extension, not a change in the word's inherent definition.

**5. Why the difference is polysemy/register, not diachronic change**
The word "quilt" has remained semantically stable because the core definition—a constructed, layered textile—remains the prototype in both corpora. The NEW corpus merely expands the *context* in which these objects appear (e.g., public memorials, metaphors in nature). The "situational polysemy" of the NAMES Project Quilt is supported by the fact that it is physically composed of individual quilt panels stitched together, satisfying the OLD corpus requirement of being a "constructed" object. The shift is one of scale and cultural application, not a fundamental change in the semantic features defining the noun.

**6. Confidence: 95/100**

### Rebuttal - Round 1

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that the use of "quilt" in the NEW corpus, specifically the **NAMES Project AIDS Memorial Quilt**, constitutes a mere "referent shift" rather than a fundamental semantic change. They argue that because it remains a "constructed, layered textile," the core semantic feature set is stable.

This assertion is directly contradicted by the following sentence from the NEW corpus:
*   *"The NAMES Project AIDS Memorial Quilt display..."*

In the OLD corpus, a "quilt" is defined by the feature **[+Domestic-Utility]**—it is a functional object for warmth, privacy, or bedding (e.g., "worn as shawls," "covering a baby," "concealing a face"). In the NEW corpus, the NAMES Project Quilt is explicitly defined by the feature **[+Commemorative-Display]**. It is no longer an object intended for the bed or the shoulders, but an entity intended for *exhibition*. The semantic shift is not just in "context," but in the removal of the requirement for domestic utility. The object has transitioned from a *tool* to an *artifact*.

Furthermore, Team Refuse fails to account for the evolution of the feature **[+Constructed/Stitched]**. In the OLD corpus, this feature is strictly tied to the process of individual creation (e.g., "in the frames and laid out," "marking," "select calico"). In the NEW corpus, the "quilt" is often a collective, institutionalized category (e.g., "The Guinness Book of Records does n't even list quilts"). The noun now functions as a category label for art installations rather than a descriptor for a domestic craft project.

The semantic evolution here is a **Functional De-specialization** (moving from domestic utility to symbolic display) driven by **Cultural Institutionalization** (where the object’s value is derived from its status as a public monument rather than its material function as a bed covering).

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support identifies the **NAMES Project AIDS Memorial Quilt** as their strongest evidence for a semantic shift, arguing that the move from a domestic object to a commemorative display represents a transition from "tool" to "artifact."

However, this argument overlooks the persistent, core semantic feature of **[+Composite-Textile-Construction]**. The "quilt," in both the OLD and NEW corpora, is defined by the intentional assembly of multiple fabric pieces into a single, cohesive unit. This feature is not an innovation of the modern era; it is explicitly licensed in the OLD corpus, such as in the sentence: *"Not only did we select calico for the border of the quilt, together with cotton batting and spool-thread..."* 

In this OLD sentence, the "quilt" is already identified as an entity defined by its material composition—the selection of distinct fabrics (calico) and binding agents (batting/thread). The NAMES Project Quilt utilizes this exact same structural logic: the assembly of individual fabric squares into a larger, singular whole. 

While Team Support points to the "commemorative" nature of the NAMES Project as a new lexical sense, this is merely a case of **polysemy via functional expansion**, not a change in the word's fundamental definition. The "quilt" remains a composite textile regardless of whether it is laid on a bed or hung on a wall; the shift in *intent* (from warmth to remembrance) does not overwrite the structural definition that has been present since the 1800s. The OLD corpus already contains references to the "process" of construction as a social and creative act (e.g., "The wonder was that she did n't make a party of 'whole cloth,' and not stick her quilt in at all"), confirming that the "quilt" was already understood as a deliberate, modular creation. The shift in context represents a change in the object's *application*, not a rupture in its semantic identity.

### Rebuttal - Round 2

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is their assertion that the "quilt" remains a "tool" (for warmth) throughout both corpora and that its modern usage is merely an expansion of function. They argue that because the structural logic of assembly remains, the semantic identity is stable. 

This is refuted by the NEW corpus sentence: *"The two big males, trailing their tail feathers like quilts tied around their necks, were following a bare little peahen along the ground."* 

In this usage, the "quilt" has been completely decoupled from the feature of **[+Composite-Textile-Construction]**. The word no longer refers to a sewn, layered, or manufactured object at all; it functions as a visual metaphor for a trailing, draped, or heavy mass. In the OLD corpus, a "quilt" was a physical, manufactured artifact requiring labor and materials (e.g., "select calico... cotton batting and spool-thread"). In the NEW corpus, the term has shifted to a purely descriptive, morphological category that can be applied to biological features—feathers—that possess no "construction" or "textile" history.

Team Refuse’s focus on the NAMES Project misses the deeper diachronic shift: the word has migrated from a **[+Product-of-Labor]** (an object made by a "quiltmaker" who "improved her skills") to a **[+Visual-Descriptor]** of shape and movement. The semantic core has moved from the *process of manufacture* to the *abstract geometry of draping*. 

**Change Type:** Metaphorical Extension (from object to visual property).
**Causal Driver:** De-specialization of the semantic field (loss of the requirement for human craftsmanship).

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support's strongest evidence is the NEW sentence: *"The two big males, trailing their tail feathers like quilts tied around their necks, were following a bare little peahen along the ground."* They argue this marks a shift from a [+Product-of-Labor] to a purely abstract visual descriptor.

However, this argument ignores that the semantic core of "quilt" has always been defined by the shared feature of **[+Draped-Layering-and-Coverage]**. The physical construction (sewing/padding) mentioned in the OLD corpus is merely the *method* used to achieve this state, not the definition itself. The essential quality of a quilt is its capacity to be draped over a surface or body to provide a layered, covering effect.

This feature is explicitly present in the OLD corpus, where "quilt" is used to describe an object that functions primarily as a drape rather than a static piece of bedding. Specifically, the OLD sentence: *"On the chair was seated a woman of some twenty-five years, who had wound a ragged quilt about her form..."* demonstrates that even in the 19th century, a quilt was recognized for its function as a **draped layer surrounding a body**. 

In the NEW sentence regarding the peacock's feathers, the author is utilizing the established semantic sense of a "draped, covering layer" that the word "quilt" has licensed since the OLD corpus. While the NEW sentence applies this to biological plumage rather than a textile, it is a clear example of **polysemy via metaphorical mapping** rather than a lexical shift. The word has not abandoned its core semantic feature of "draped coverage"; it has simply extended that feature to a new domain (the natural world) where the same visual and functional properties—a heavy, layered, trailing mass—are present. Because the feature of [Draped-Layering] is already established in the OLD corpus, the word's semantic identity remains stable.

### Rebuttal - Round 3

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Refuse’s weakest claim is that the semantic core of "quilt" remains unchanged because it has always functioned primarily as a "draped layer." This ignores the fundamental shift from a **[+Utilitarian-Household-Object]** to a **[+Commemorative-Cultural-Artifact]**.

Team Refuse argues that the "draped, covering layer" is the constant. However, the OLD corpus explicitly defines the quilt through the *process* of its creation as a communal, domestic labor: *"Already the quilt was in the frames and laid out, as the marking was called."* Here, the "quilt" is inseparable from the "frames," the "marking," and the social labor of "quilting." In contrast, the NEW corpus features: *"The NAMES Project AIDS Memorial Quilt display."* This is a radical semantic departure. The "quilt" here is no longer a functional, draped textile used to keep warm (as in the OLD sentence: *"if the ladies would wear the quilts and blankets for shawls... they must pay for the washing"*); it is a static, museum-bound symbolic monument. The feature of [Utilitarian-Bedding] has been discarded in favor of [Symbolic-Commemoration].

Furthermore, the NEW corpus usage regarding the peacock—*"trailing their tail feathers like quilts"*—actually supports my argument for change. By using "quilt" as a simile for biological anatomy, the word has undergone a **de-lexicalization** of its "textile" component. In the OLD corpus, a quilt was a material object of calico, batting, and thread (*"select calico for the border of the quilt, together with cotton batting"*). In the NEW corpus, the word has been stripped of its [+Material-Fabrication] feature entirely, functioning now as a purely visual descriptor for "heavy, draped mass." 

The semantic shift is not merely a metaphor; it is the loss of the "domestic labor" core and the adoption of a "symbolic/visual" core.

**Change Type:** Semantic Broadening (Metaphorical Extension) and Feature Loss.
**Causal Driver:** Socio-cultural evolution (the transition of the object from a domestic necessity to a commemorative/artistic icon).

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

Team Support’s strongest evidence is the reference to "The NAMES Project AIDS Memorial Quilt," which they argue represents a departure from the "utilitarian-household-object" core toward a "commemorative-cultural-artifact." 

However, this shift is not a change in the lexical sense of "quilt," but rather an application of the persistent semantic feature: **[+Stitched-Composite-Textile]**. A "quilt" has always been defined by its construction—multiple layers of fabric joined by stitching. This feature remains the fundamental criterion for what qualifies as a quilt, whether it serves to warm a body or to commemorate a life. 

The OLD corpus already licenses this feature as a multi-purpose entity that transcends simple utility. In the OLD sentence, *“After all, the neighbors began to think the quilting was not going to be any such great things more than other quiltings,”* the emphasis is on the social and collective nature of the *process* of creation, rather than just the end-use of the object. The NEW usage as a "memorial" simply extends this established communal aspect. Furthermore, the NEW usage regarding the peacock (“trailing their tail feathers like quilts”) does not represent a "de-lexicalization" of the material component; it is a standard metaphor relying on the shared feature of **[+Layered-Draping-Structure]**. This is licensed by the OLD corpus, where the quilt is already treated as a versatile, draped object: *“if the ladies would wear the quilts and blankets for shawls... they must pay for the washing.”* 

Because the word consistently denotes a specific structural arrangement of fabric—regardless of whether it is used for bedding, shawls, or memorial displays—it remains a polysemous term within a single, stable lexical category. Its application to new domains is an expansion of context, not a change in the core definition of the noun itself.

### Rebuttal - Round closing

#### 🔴 Team Support (Hypothesis: Change)

Team Support maintains that "quilt" has shifted from a domestic utilitarian object to a communal, symbolic artifact. Our strongest evidence lies in the shift from individual, functional descriptions in Corpus 1—such as "a quilt of patchwork"—to the collective, commemorative usage in Corpus 2, exemplified by "the AIDS Memorial Quilt." This transition redefines the quilt as a vehicle for public narrative rather than mere bedding.

Team Refuse argues that the "draped, layered" physical state remains the semantic core, citing biological metaphors like "trailing... like quilts." However, this ignores that the primary semantic evolution is driven by the object's function as a commemorative record. When Corpus 2 describes "a quilt of panels," the word functions as a medium for social history, not a functional covering. The physical structure is now subordinate to the symbolic intent, marking a fundamental change in the word’s application.

**Change Type:** Semantic expansion (Utilitarian to Symbolic/Commemorative)
**Causal Driver:** Socio-cultural shift in the object’s function.

#### 🟢 Team Refuse (Hypothesis: Stable)

The semantic core of "quilt" remains anchored to its physical state as a draped, layered covering. Corpus evidence confirms this constant: whether in 1810 or 2010, the term denotes a layered textile mass. The OLD corpus describes the quilt as an object one might "wear... for shawls," while the NEW corpus similarly utilizes the term to describe biological features "trailing... like quilts." Both instances rely on the shared structural feature of a heavy, draped layer, proving the word’s fundamental definition has not shifted.

Team Support claims the quilt has transitioned from a utilitarian object to a purely symbolic artifact, citing the "AIDS Memorial Quilt." However, this ignores that the object remains a "draped layer" regardless of its commemorative function. The core utility—its existence as a "quilt" in "frames"—persists as the essential physical identity of the word. Team Support’s argument fails because the "symbolic" usage is merely a contextual application of the existing, stable definition of a layered, textile-like form. The material essence of the quilt as a draped, layered object remains the primary, consistent semantic feature across both centuries.

### ⚖️ LLM Judge

The debate demonstrates that the core semantic features of 'quilt'—specifically [+Composite-Textile-Construction] and [+Draped-Layering]—remain consistent across both corpora. Team Support's argument for change relies on the shift in the object's function (from domestic bedding to commemorative art). However, as Team Refuse correctly identifies, this is a change in the 'situational' or 'pragmatic' application of the word rather than a change in its core denotation. The 'AIDS Memorial Quilt' is still a composite, stitched textile, which satisfies the definition present in the 1810-1860 corpus. Similarly, the use of 'quilt' as a metaphor for peacock feathers relies on the established feature of 'draped, layered coverage,' which is a standard figurative extension (metaphor) rather than a semantic shift. Because the fundamental structural and functional features of the referent remain intact, the word is considered stable.
